A chap might be\r\nas willing and eager to make good as he liked; but when it came to this\r\nabout atmosphere and intense passages and plasticity\xe2\x80\x94well, really, what\r\ncould a chap do? Make a note of it. It was the only thing.\r\n\r\nIn Mr. Albemarle\xe2\x80\x99s private room Casimir Lypiatt thumped the table.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSize, Mr. Albemarle,\xe2\x80\x9d he was saying, \xe2\x80\x9csize and vehemence and spiritual\r\nsignificance\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what the old fellows had, and we haven\xe2\x80\x99t....\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\ngesticulated as he talked, his face worked and his green eyes, set in\r\ntheir dark, charred orbits, were full of a troubled light. The forehead\r\nwas precipitous, the nose long and sharp; in the bony and almost\r\nfleshless face, the lips of the wide mouth were surprisingly full.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrecisely, precisely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Albemarle in his juicy voice. He was a\r\nround, smooth, little man with a head like an egg; he spoke, he moved\r\nwith a certain pomp, a butlerish gravity, that were evidently meant to\r\nbe ducal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what I\xe2\x80\x99ve set myself to recapture,\xe2\x80\x9d Lypiatt went on: \xe2\x80\x9cthe size,\r\nthe masterfulness of the masters.\xe2\x80\x9d He felt a warmth running through him\r\nas he spoke, flushing his cheeks, pulsing hotly behind the eyes, as\r\nthough he had drunk a draught of some heartening red wine. His own words\r\nelated him, and drunkenly gesticulating, he was as though drunken. The\r\ngreatness of the masters\xe2\x80\x94he felt it in him. He knew his own power, he\r\nknew, he knew. He could do all that they had done. Nothing was beyond\r\nhis strength.\r\n\r\nEgg-headed Albemarle confronted him, impeccably the butler,\r\nexacerbatingly serene. Albemarle too should be fired. He struck the\r\ntable once more, he broke out again:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s been my mission,\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted, \xe2\x80\x9call these years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll these years.... Time had worn the hair from his temples; the high,\r\nsteep forehead seemed higher than it really was. He was forty now; the\r\nturbulent young Lypiatt who had once declared that no man could do\r\nanything worth doing after he was thirty, was forty now. But in these\r\nfiery moments he could forget the years, he could forget the\r\ndisappointments, the unsold pictures, the bad reviews. \xe2\x80\x9cMy mission,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nrepeated; \xe2\x80\x9cand by God! I feel, I know I can carry it through.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWarmly the blood pulsed behind his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Albemarle, nodding the egg. \xe2\x80\x9cQuite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how small the scale is nowadays!\xe2\x80\x9d Lypiatt went on, rhapsodically.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow trivial the conception, how limited the scope! You see no\r\npainter-sculptor-poets, like Michelangelo; no scientist-artists, like\r\nLeonardo; no mathematician-courtiers, like Boscovitch; no\r\nimpresario-musicians, like Handel; no geniuses of all trades, like Wren.\r\nI have set myself against this abject specialization of ours. I stand\r\nalone, opposing it with my example.\xe2\x80\x9d Lypiatt raised his hand. Like the\r\nstatue of Liberty, standing colossal and alone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNevertheless,\xe2\x80\x9d began Mr. Albemarle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPainter, poet, musician,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Lypiatt. \xe2\x80\x9cI am all three. I....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c... there is a danger of\xe2\x80\x94how shall I put it\xe2\x80\x94dissipating one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nenergies,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Albemarle went on with determination. Discreetly, he\r\nlooked at his watch. This conversation, he thought, seemed to be\r\nprolonging itself unnecessarily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is a greater danger in letting them stagnate and atrophy,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nLypiatt retorted. \xe2\x80\x9cLet me give you my experience.\xe2\x80\x9d Vehemently, he gave\r\nit.\r\n\r\nOut in the gallery, among the boats, the views of the Grand Canal, and\r\nthe Firth of Forth, Gumbril placidly ruminated. Poor old Lypiatt, he was\r\nthinking. Dear old Lypiatt, even, in spite of his fantastic egotism.\r\nSuch a bad painter, such a bombinating poet, such a loud emotional\r\nimproviser on the piano! And going on like this, year after year,\r\npegging away at the same old things\xe2\x80\x94always badly! And always without a\r\npenny, always living in the most hideous squalor! Magnificent and\r\npathetic old Lypiatt!\r\n\r\nA door suddenly opened and a loud, unsteady voice, now deep and harsh,\r\nnow breaking to shrillness, exploded into the gallery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c... like a Veronese,\xe2\x80\x9d it was saying; \xe2\x80\x9cenormous, vehement, a great\r\nswirling composition\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x98swirling composition\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94mentally, the young\r\nassistant made a note of that), \xe2\x80\x9cbut much more serious, of course, much\r\nmore spiritually significant, much more\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLypiatt!\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril had risen from his chair, had turned, had advanced,\r\nholding out his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s Gumbril. Good Lord!\xe2\x80\x9d and Lypiatt seized the proffered hand\r\nwith an excruciating cordiality. He seemed to be in exuberantly good\r\nspirits. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re settling about my show, Mr. Albemarle and I,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nexplained. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know Gumbril, Mr. Albemarle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPleased to meet you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Albemarle. \xe2\x80\x9cOur friend, Mr. Lypiatt,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nadded richly, \xe2\x80\x9chas the true artistic temp\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s going to be magnificent.\xe2\x80\x9d Lypiatt could not wait till Mr.\r\nAlbemarle had finished speaking. He gave Gumbril a heroic blow on the\r\nshoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c... artistic temperament, as I was saying,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued Mr. Albemarle. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\r\nis altogether too impatient and enthusiastic for us poor people ...\xe2\x80\x9d a\r\nducal smile of condescension accompanied this graceful act of\r\nself-abasement ... \xe2\x80\x9cwho move in the prosaic, practical, workaday world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLypiatt laughed, a loud, discordant peal. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to mind being\r\naccused of having an artistic temperament; he seemed, indeed, to enjoy\r\nit, if anything. \xe2\x80\x9cFire and water,\xe2\x80\x9d he said aphoristically, \xe2\x80\x9cbrought\r\ntogether, beget steam. Mr. Albemarle and I go driving along like a steam\r\nengine. Psh, psh!\xe2\x80\x9d He worked his arms like a pair of alternate pistons.\r\nHe laughed; but Mr. Albemarle only coldly and courteously smiled. \xe2\x80\x9cI was\r\njust telling Mr. Albemarle about the great Crucifixion I\xe2\x80\x99ve just been\r\ndoing. It\xe2\x80\x99s as big and headlong as a Veronese, but much more serious,\r\nmore....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBehind them the little assistant was expounding to a new visitor the\r\nbeauties of the etchings. \xe2\x80\x9cVery intense,\xe2\x80\x9d he was saying, \xe2\x80\x9cthe feeling in\r\nthis passage.\xe2\x80\x9d The shadow, indeed, clung with an insistent affection\r\nround the stern of the boat. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what a fine, what a\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he hesitated\r\nfor an instant, and under his pale, oiled hair his face became suddenly\r\nvery red\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwhat a swirling composition.\xe2\x80\x9d He looked anxiously at the\r\nvisitor. The remark had been received without comment. He felt immensely\r\nrelieved.\r\n\r\nThey left the galleries together. Lypiatt set the pace, striding along\r\nat a great rate and with a magnificent brutality through the elegant and\r\nleisured crowd, gesticulating and loudly talking as he went. He carried\r\nhis hat in his hand; his tie was brilliantly orange. People turned to\r\nlook at him as he passed and he liked it. He had, indeed, a remarkable\r\nface\xe2\x80\x94a face that ought by rights to have belonged to a man of genius.\r\nLypiatt was aware of it. The man of genius, he liked to say, bears upon\r\nhis brow a kind of mark of Cain, by which men recognize him at once\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nhaving recognized, generally stone him,\xe2\x80\x9d he would add with that peculiar\r\nlaugh he always uttered whenever he said anything rather bitter or\r\ncynical; a laugh that was meant to show that the bitterness, the\r\ncynicism, justifiable as events might have made them, were really only a\r\nmask, and that beneath it the artist was still serenely and tragically\r\nsmiling. Lypiatt thought a great deal about the ideal artist. That\r\ntitanic abstraction stalked within his own skin. He was it\xe2\x80\x94a little too\r\nconsciously, perhaps.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis time,\xe2\x80\x9d he kept repeating, \xe2\x80\x9cthey\xe2\x80\x99ll be bowled over. This time....\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s going to be terrific.\xe2\x80\x9d And with the blood beating behind his eyes,\r\nwith the exultant consciousness and certainty of power growing and\r\ngrowing in him with every word he spoke, Lypiatt began to describe the\r\npictures there would be at his show; he talked about the preface he was\r\nwriting to the catalogue, the poems that would be printed in it by way\r\nof literary complement to the pictures. He talked, he talked.\r\n\r\nGumbril listened, not very attentively. He was wondering how any one\r\ncould talk so loud, could boast so extravagantly. It was as though the\r\nman had to shout in order to convince himself of his own existence. Poor\r\nLypiatt; after all these years, Gumbril supposed, he must have some\r\ndoubts about it. Ah, but this time, this time he was going to bowl them\r\nall over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re pleased, then, with what you\xe2\x80\x99ve done recently,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at the\r\nend of one of Lypiatt\xe2\x80\x99s long tirades.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPleased?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Lypiatt; \xe2\x80\x9cI should think I was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGumbril might have reminded him that he had been as well pleased in the\r\npast and that \xe2\x80\x98they\xe2\x80\x99 had by no means been bowled over. He preferred,\r\nhowever, to say nothing. Lypiatt went on about the size and universality\r\nof the old masters. He himself, it was tacitly understood, was one of\r\nthem.\r\n\r\nThey parted near the bottom of the Tottenham Court Road, Lypiatt to go\r\nnorthward to his studio off Maple Street, Gumbril to pay one of his\r\nsecret visits to those rooms of his in Great Russell Street. He had\r\ntaken them nearly a year ago now, two little rooms over a grocer\xe2\x80\x99s shop,\r\npromising himself goodness only knew what adventures in them. But\r\nsomehow there had been no adventures. Still, it had pleased him, all the\r\nsame, to be able to go there from time to time when he was in London and\r\nto think, as he sat in solitude before his gas fire, that there was\r\nliterally not a soul in the universe who knew where he was. He had an\r\nalmost childish affection for mysteries and secrets.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril, raising his hand to the salute. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99ll beat\r\nup some people for dinner on Friday.\xe2\x80\x9d (For they had agreed to meet\r\nagain.) He turned away, thinking that he had spoken the last words; but\r\nhe was mistaken.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, by the way,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lypiatt, who had also turned to go, but who now\r\ncame stepping quickly after his companion. \xe2\x80\x9cCan you, by any chance, lend\r\nme five pounds. Only till after the exhibition, you know. I\xe2\x80\x99m a bit\r\nshort.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoor old Lypiatt! But it was with reluctance that Gumbril parted from\r\nhis Treasury notes.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                               CHAPTER IV\r\n\r\n\r\nLypiatt had a habit, which some of his friends found rather trying\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nnot only friends, for Lypiatt was ready to let the merest acquaintances,\r\nthe most absolute strangers, even, into the secrets of his inspiration\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nhabit of reciting at every possible opportunity his own verses. He would\r\ndeclaim in a voice loud and tremulous, with an emotion that never seemed\r\nto vary with the varying subject-matter of his poems, for whole quarters\r\nof an hour at a stretch; would go on declaiming till his auditors were\r\noverwhelmed with such a confusion of embarrassment and shame, that the\r\nblood rushed to their cheeks and they dared not meet one another\xe2\x80\x99s eyes.\r\n\r\nHe was declaiming now; not merely across the dinner table to his own\r\nfriends, but to the whole restaurant. For at the first reverberating\r\nlines of his latest, \xe2\x80\x9cThe Conquistador,\xe2\x80\x9d there had been a startled\r\nturning of heads, a craning of necks from every corner of the room. The\r\npeople who came to this Soho restaurant because it was, notoriously, so\r\n\xe2\x80\x98artistic,\xe2\x80\x99 looked at one another significantly and nodded; they were\r\ngetting their money\xe2\x80\x99s worth, this time. And Lypiatt, with a fine air of\r\nrapt unconsciousness, went on with his recitation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook down on Mexico, Conquistador\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94that was the refrain.\r\n\r\nThe Conquistador, Lypiatt had made it clear, was the Artist, and the\r\nVale of Mexico on which he looked down, the towered cities of Tlacopan\r\nand Chalco, of Tenochtitlan and Iztapalapan symbolized\xe2\x80\x94well, it was\r\ndifficult to say precisely what. The universe, perhaps?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook down,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Lypiatt, with a quivering voice.\r\n\r\n            \xe2\x80\x9cLook down, Conquistador!\r\n            There on the valley\xe2\x80\x99s broad green floor,\r\n            There lies the lake; the jewelled cities gleam;\r\n            Chalco and Tlacopan\r\n            Awaiting the coming Man.\r\n            Look down on Mexico, Conquistador,\r\n            Land of your golden dream.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot \xe2\x80\x98dream,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril, putting down the glass from which he had\r\nbeen profoundly drinking. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t possibly say \xe2\x80\x98dream,\xe2\x80\x99 you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you interrupt me?\xe2\x80\x9d Lypiatt turned on him angrily. His wide mouth\r\ntwitched at the corners, his whole long face worked with excitement.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you let me finish?\xe2\x80\x9d He allowed his hand, which had hung\r\nawkwardly in the air above him, suspended, as it were, at the top of a\r\ngesture, to sink slowly to the table. \xe2\x80\x9cImbecile!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and once more\r\npicked up his knife and fork.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut really,\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril insisted, \xe2\x80\x9cyou can\xe2\x80\x99t say \xe2\x80\x98dream.\xe2\x80\x99 Can you now,\r\nseriously?\xe2\x80\x9d He had drunk the best part of a bottle of Burgundy and he\r\nfelt good-humoured, obstinate and a little bellicose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why not?\xe2\x80\x9d Lypiatt asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, because one simply can\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril leaned back in his chair, smiled\r\nand caressed his drooping blond moustache. \xe2\x80\x9cNot in this year of grace,\r\nnineteen twenty-two.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why?\xe2\x80\x9d Lypiatt repeated, with exasperation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause it\xe2\x80\x99s altogether _too_ late in the day,\xe2\x80\x9d declared precious Mr.\r\nMercaptan, rushing up to his emphasis with flutes and roaring, like a\r\ntrue Conquistador, to fall back, however, at the end of the sentence\r\nrather ignominiously into a breathless confusion. He was a sleek,\r\ncomfortable young man with smooth brown hair parted in the centre and\r\nconducted in a pair of flowing curves across the temples, to be looped\r\nin damp curls behind his ears. His face ought to have been rather more\r\nexquisite, rather more refinedly _dix-huiti\xc3\xa8me_ than it actually was. It\r\nhad a rather gross, snouty look, which was sadly out of harmony with Mr.\r\nMercaptan\xe2\x80\x99s inimitably graceful style. For Mr. Mercaptan had a style and\r\nused it, delightfully, in his middle articles for the literary weeklies.\r\nHis most precious work, however, was that little volume of essays, prose\r\npoems, vignettes and paradoxes, in which he had so brilliantly\r\nillustrated his favourite theme\xe2\x80\x94the pettiness, the simian limitations,\r\nthe insignificance and the absurd pretentiousness of _Homo_ soi-disant\r\n_Sapiens_. Those who met Mr. Mercaptan personally often came away with\r\nthe feeling that perhaps, after all, he was right in judging so severely\r\nof humanity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Too_ late in the day,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cTimes have changed. _Sunt lacrym\xc3\xa6\r\nrerum, nos et mutamur in illis._\xe2\x80\x9d He laughed his own applause.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Quot homines, tot disputandum est_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril, taking another sip\r\nof his Beaune Sup\xc3\xa9rieure. At the moment, he was all for Mercaptan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut _why_ is it too late?\xe2\x80\x9d Lypiatt insisted.\r\n\r\nMr. Mercaptan made a delicate gesture. \xe2\x80\x9c_\xc3\x87a se sent, mon cher ami_,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9c_\xc3\xa7a ne s\xe2\x80\x99explique pas._\xe2\x80\x9d Satan, it is said, carries hell in his\r\nheart; so it was with Mr. Mercaptan\xe2\x80\x94wherever he was, it was Paris.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDreams in nineteen twenty-two....\xe2\x80\x9d He shrugged his shoulders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter you\xe2\x80\x99ve accepted the war, swallowed the Russian famine,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nGumbril. \xe2\x80\x9cDreams!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey belonged to the _Rostand_ epoch,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mercaptan, with a\r\nlittle titter. \xe2\x80\x9c_Le R\xc3\xa8ve_\xe2\x80\x94ah!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLypiatt dropped his knife and fork with a clatter and leaned forward,\r\neager for battle. \xe2\x80\x9cNow I have you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cnow I have you on the hip.\r\nYou\xe2\x80\x99ve given yourselves away. You\xe2\x80\x99ve given away the secret of your\r\nspiritual poverty, your weakness and pettiness and impotence....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpotence? You malign me, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril.\r\n\r\nShearwater ponderously stirred. He had been silent all this time,\r\nsitting with hunched shoulders, his elbows on the table, his big round\r\nhead bent forward, absorbed, apparently, in the slow meticulous\r\ncrumbling of a piece of bread. Sometimes he put a piece of crust in his\r\nmouth and under the bushy brown moustache his jaw moved slowly,\r\nruminatively, with a sideways motion, like a cow\xe2\x80\x99s. He nudged Gumbril\r\nwith his elbow. \xe2\x80\x9cAss,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cbe quiet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLypiatt went on torrentially. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re afraid of ideals, that\xe2\x80\x99s what it\r\nis. You daren\xe2\x80\x99t admit to having dreams. Oh, I call them dreams,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nadded parenthetically. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mind being thought a fool and\r\nold-fashioned. The word\xe2\x80\x99s shorter and more English. Besides, it rhymes\r\nwith gleams. Ha, ha!\xe2\x80\x9d And Lypiatt laughed his loud Titan\xe2\x80\x99s laugh, the\r\nlaugh of cynicism which seems to belie, but which, for those who have\r\nunderstanding, reveals the high, positive spirit within. \xe2\x80\x9cIdeals\xe2\x80\x94they\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nnot sufficiently genteel for you civilized young men. You\xe2\x80\x99ve quite\r\noutgrown that sort of thing. No dream, no religion, no morality.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI glory in the name of earwig,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril. He was pleased with that\r\nlittle invention. It was felicitous; it was well chosen. \xe2\x80\x9cOne\xe2\x80\x99s an\r\nearwig in sheer self-protection,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained.\r\n\r\nBut Mr. Mercaptan refused to accept the name of earwig at any price.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_What_ there is to be ashamed of in being civilized, I _really_ don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, in a voice that was now the bull\xe2\x80\x99s, now the piping\r\nrobin\xe2\x80\x99s. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, if I glory in anything, it\xe2\x80\x99s in my little rococo boudoir,\r\nand the conversations across the polished mahogany, and the delicate,\r\nlascivious, _witty_ little flirtations on ample sofas inhabited by the\r\nsoul of Crebillon Fils. We needn\xe2\x80\x99t _all_ be Russians, I hope. These\r\nrevolting Dostoievskys.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mercaptan spoke with a profound feeling.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor all Utopians. Homo _au naturel_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mercaptan applied his thumb\r\nand forefinger to his, alas! too snout-like nose, \xe2\x80\x9c_\xc3\xa7a pue_. And as for\r\nHomo \xc3\xa0 la H. G. Wells\xe2\x80\x94_\xc3\xa7a ne pue pas assez_. What I glory in is the\r\ncivilized, middle way between stink and asepsis. Give me a little musk,\r\na little intoxicating feminine exhalation, the bouquet of old wine and\r\nstrawberries, a lavender bag under every pillow and pot-pourri in the\r\ncorners of the drawing-room. Readable books, amusing conversation,\r\ncivilized women, graceful art and dry vintage, music, with a quiet life\r\nand reasonable comfort\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s _all_ I ask for.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTalking about comfort,\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril put in, before Lypiatt had time to\r\nfling his answering thunders, \xe2\x80\x9cI must tell you about my new invention.\r\nPneumatic trousers,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained. \xe2\x80\x9cBlow them up. Perfect comfort. You\r\nsee the idea? You\xe2\x80\x99re a sedentary man, Mercaptan. Let me put you down for\r\na couple of pairs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Mercaptan shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cToo Wellsian,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cToo horribly\r\nUtopian. They\xe2\x80\x99d be ludicrously out of place in my boudoir. And besides,\r\nmy sofa is well enough sprung already, thank you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what about Tolstoy?\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Lypiatt, letting out his impatience in\r\na violent blast.\r\n\r\nMr. Mercaptan waved his hand. \xe2\x80\x9cRussian,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cRussian.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Michelangelo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlberti,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril, very seriously, giving them all a piece of his\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s mind\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cAlberti was much the better architect, I assure you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd pretentiousness for pretentiousness,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mercaptan, \xe2\x80\x9cI prefer\r\nold Borromini and the baroque.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat about Beethoven?\xe2\x80\x9d went on Lypiatt. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat about Blake? Where do\r\nthey come in under your scheme of things?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Mercaptan shrugged his shoulders. \xe2\x80\x9cThey stay in the hall,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t let them into the boudoir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou disgust me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lypiatt, with rising indignation, and making\r\nwider gestures. \xe2\x80\x9cYou disgust me\xe2\x80\x94you and your odious little sham\r\neighteenth-century civilization; your piddling little poetry; your art\r\nfor art\xe2\x80\x99s sake instead of for God\xe2\x80\x99s sake; your nauseating little\r\ncopulations without love or passion; your hoggish materialism; your\r\nbestial indifference to all that\xe2\x80\x99s unhappy and your yelping hatred of\r\nall that\xe2\x80\x99s great.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCharming, charming,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Mr. Mercaptan, who was pouring oil on his\r\nsalad.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can you ever hope to achieve anything decent or solid, when you\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t even believe in decency or solidity? I look about me,\xe2\x80\x9d and Lypiatt\r\ncast his eyes wildly round the crowded room, \xe2\x80\x9cand I find myself alone,\r\nspiritually alone. I strive on by myself, by myself.\xe2\x80\x9d He struck his\r\nbreast, a giant, a solitary giant. \xe2\x80\x9cI have set myself to restore\r\npainting and poetry to their rightful position among the great moral\r\nforces. They have been amusements, they have been mere games for too\r\nlong. I am giving my life for that. My life.\xe2\x80\x9d His voice trembled a\r\nlittle. \xe2\x80\x9cPeople mock me, hate me, stone me, deride me. But I go on, I go\r\non. For I know I\xe2\x80\x99m right. And in the end they too will recognize that\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve been right.\xe2\x80\x9d It was a loud soliloquy. One could fancy that Lypiatt\r\nhad been engaged in recognizing himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the same,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril with a cheerful stubbornness, \xe2\x80\x9cI persist\r\nthat the word \xe2\x80\x98dreams\xe2\x80\x99 is inadmissible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Inadmissible_,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Mr. Mercaptan, imparting to the word an\r\nadditional significance by giving it its French pronunciation. \xe2\x80\x9cIn the\r\nage of Rostand, well and good. But now....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril, \xe2\x80\x9cthe word merely connotes Freud.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a matter of literary tact,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Mr. Mercaptan. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you no\r\nliterary tact?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lypiatt, with emphasis, \xe2\x80\x9cthank God, I haven\xe2\x80\x99t. I have no tact\r\nof any kind. I do things straightforwardly, frankly, as the spirit moves\r\nme. I don\xe2\x80\x99t like compromises.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe struck the table. The gesture startlingly let loose a peal of cracked\r\nand diabolic laughter. Gumbril and Lypiatt and Mr. Mercaptan looked\r\nquickly up; even Shearwater lifted his great spherical head and turned\r\ntowards the sound the large disk of his face. A young man with a blond,\r\nfan-shaped beard stood by the table, looking down at them through a pair\r\nof bright blue eyes and smiling equivocally and disquietingly as though\r\nhis mind were full of some nameless and fantastic malice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Come sta la Sua Terribilt\xc3\xa0?_\xe2\x80\x9d he asked; and, taking off his\r\npreposterous bowler hat, he bowed profoundly to Lypiatt. \xe2\x80\x9cHow I\r\nrecognize my Buonarotti!\xe2\x80\x9d he added affectionately.\r\n\r\nLypiatt laughed, rather uncomfortably, and no longer on the Titanic\r\nscale. \xe2\x80\x9cHow I recognize my Coleman!\xe2\x80\x9d he echoed, rather feebly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the contrary,\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril corrected, \xe2\x80\x9chow almost completely I fail to\r\nrecognize. This beard\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he pointed to the blond fan\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwhy, may I ask?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore Russianism,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mercaptan, and shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, why indeed?\xe2\x80\x9d Coleman lowered his voice to a confidential whisper.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor religious reasons,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and made the sign of the cross.\r\n\r\n                      \xe2\x80\x9cChristlike in my behaviour,\r\n                      Like every good believer,\r\n                      I imitate the Saviour,\r\n                      And cultivate a beaver.\r\n\r\nThere be beavers which have made themselves beavers for the kingdom of\r\nheaven\xe2\x80\x99s sake. But there are some beavers, on the other hand, which were\r\nso born from their mother\xe2\x80\x99s womb.\xe2\x80\x9d He burst into a fit of outrageous\r\nlaughter which stopped as suddenly and as voluntarily as it had begun.\r\n\r\nLypiatt shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cHideous,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9chideous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMoreover,\xe2\x80\x9d Coleman went on, without paying any attention, \xe2\x80\x9cI have other\r\nand, alas! less holy reasons for this change of face. It enables one to\r\nmake such delightful acquaintances in the street. You hear some one\r\nsaying, \xe2\x80\x98Beaver,\xe2\x80\x99 as you pass, and you immediately have the right to\r\nrush up and get into conversation. I owe to this dear symbol,\xe2\x80\x9d and he\r\ncaressed the golden beard tenderly with the palm of his hand, \xe2\x80\x9cthe most\r\nadmirably dangerous relations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMagnificent,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril, drinking his own health. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall stop\r\nshaving at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShearwater looked round the table with raised eyebrows and a wrinkled\r\nforehead. \xe2\x80\x9cThis conversation is rather beyond me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said gravely.\r\nUnder the formidable moustache, under the thick, tufted eyebrows, the\r\nmouth was small and ingenuous, the mild grey eyes full of an almost\r\nchildish inquiry. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat does the word \xe2\x80\x98beaver\xe2\x80\x99 signify in this context?\r\nYou don\xe2\x80\x99t refer, I suppose, to the rodent, _Castor fiber_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut this is a very great man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coleman, raising his bowler. \xe2\x80\x9cTell\r\nme who he is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur friend Shearwater,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril, \xe2\x80\x9cthe physiologist.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColeman bowed. \xe2\x80\x9cPhysiological Shearwater,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAccept my homage.\r\nTo one who doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know what a beaver is, I resign all my claims to\r\nsuperiority. There\xe2\x80\x99s nothing else but beavers in all the papers. Tell\r\nme, do you never read the _Daily Express_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor the _Daily Mail_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShearwater shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor the _Mirror_? nor the _Sketch_? nor the _Graphic_? nor even (for I\r\nwas forgetting that physiologists must surely have Liberal\r\nopinions)\xe2\x80\x94even the _Daily News_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShearwater continued to shake his large spherical head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor any of the evening papers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColeman once more lifted his hat. \xe2\x80\x9cO eloquent, just and mighty Death!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe exclaimed, and replaced it on his head. \xe2\x80\x9cYou never read any papers at\r\nall\xe2\x80\x94not even our friend Mercaptan\xe2\x80\x99s delicious little middles in the\r\nweeklies? How is your delicious little middle, by the way?\xe2\x80\x9d Coleman\r\nturned to Mr. Mercaptan and with the point of his huge stick gave him a\r\nlittle prod in the stomach. \xe2\x80\x9c_\xc3\x87a marche\xe2\x80\x94les tripes_? Hein?\xe2\x80\x9d He turned\r\nback to Shearwater. \xe2\x80\x9cNot even those?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shearwater. \xe2\x80\x9cI have more serious things to think about\r\nthan newspapers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what serious thing, may I ask?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, at the present moment,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shearwater, \xe2\x80\x9cI am chiefly\r\npreoccupied with the kidneys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe kidneys!\xe2\x80\x9d In an ecstasy of delight, Coleman thumped the floor with\r\nthe ferrule of his stick. \xe2\x80\x9cThe kidneys! Tell me all about kidneys. This\r\nis of the first importance. This is really life. And I shall sit down at\r\nyour table without asking permission of Buonarotti here, and in the\r\nteeth of Mercaptan, and without so much as thinking about this species\r\nof Gumbril, who might as well not be there at all. I shall sit down\r\nand\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTalking of sitting,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril, \xe2\x80\x9cI wish I could persuade you to\r\norder a pair of my patent pneumatic trousers. They will\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColeman waved him away. \xe2\x80\x9cNot now, not now,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall sit down\r\nand listen to the physiologue talking about runions, while I myself\r\nactually eat them\xe2\x80\x94_saut\xc3\xa9s_. _Saut\xc3\xa9s_, mark my words.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLaying his hat and stick on the floor beside him, he sat down at the end\r\nof the table, between Lypiatt and Shearwater.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo believers,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, laying his hand for a moment on Lypiatt\xe2\x80\x99s arm,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand three black-hearted unbelievers\xe2\x80\x94confronted. Ha, ha!\xe2\x80\x9d He laughed his ferocious, artificial laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s an end to any civilized conversation,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mercaptan complained,\r\nhissing on the _c_, labiating lingeringly on the _v_ of \xe2\x80\x98civilized\xe2\x80\x99 and\r\ngiving the first two _i_\xe2\x80\x99s their fullest value. This is most important;\r\nBuonarotti shares my opinion about this, I know. Has the Archetypal Man\r\na _boyau rectum_, as Mercaptan would say again, or not? Everything\r\ndepends on this, as Voltaire realized ages ago. \xe2\x80\x98His feet,\xe2\x80\x99 as we know\r\nalready on inspired authority, \xe2\x80\x98were straight feet; and the sole of his\r\nfeet were like the sole of a calf\xe2\x80\x99s foot.\xe2\x80\x99 But the viscera, you must\r\ntell us something about the viscera. And where\r\nare my _rognons saut\xc3\xa9s_?\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted at the waiter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou revolt me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lypiatt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot mortually, I \xe2\x80\x99ope?\xe2\x80\x9d Coleman turned with solicitude to his\r\nneighbour; then shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cMortually I fear. Kiss me \xe2\x80\x99Ardy, and I\r\ndie happy.\xe2\x80\x9d He blew a kiss into the air. \xe2\x80\x9cBut why is the physiologue so\r\nslow? Up, pachyderm, up! Answer. You hold the key to everything. The\r\nkey, I tell you, the key. I remember, when I used to hang about the\r\nbiological laboratories at school, eviscerating frogs\xe2\x80\x94crucified with\r\npins, they were, belly upwards, like little green Christs\xe2\x80\x94I remember\r\nonce, when I was sitting there, quietly poring over the entrails, in\r\ncame the laboratory boy and said to the stinks usher: \xe2\x80\x98Please, sir, may\r\nI have the key of the Absolute?\xe2\x80\x99 And, would you believe it, that usher\r\ncalmly put his hand in his trouser pocket and fished out a small Yale\r\nkey and gave it him without a word. What a gesture! The key of the\r\nAbsolute. But it was only the absolute alcohol the urchin wanted\xe2\x80\x94to\r\npickle some loathsome f\xc5\x93tus in, I suppose. God rot his soul in peace!\r\nAnd now, Castor Fiber, out with your key. Tell us about the Archetypal\r\nMan, tell us about the primordial Adam. Tell us all about the _boyau\r\nrectum_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPonderously, Shearwater moved his clumsy frame; leaning back in his\r\nchair he scrutinized Coleman with a large, benevolent curiosity. The\r\neyes under the savage eyebrows were mild and gentle; behind the fearful\r\ndisguise of the moustache he smiled poutingly, like a baby who sees the\r\napproaching bottle. The broad, domed forehead was serene. He ran his\r\nhand through his thick brown hair, scratched his head meditatively and\r\nthen, when he had thoroughly examined, had comprehended and duly\r\nclassified the strange phenomenon of Coleman, opened his mouth and\r\nuttered a little good-natured laugh of amusement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVoltaire\xe2\x80\x99s question,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last, in his slow, deep voice, \xe2\x80\x9cseemed\r\nat the time he asked it an unanswerable piece of irony. It would have\r\nseemed almost equally ironic to his contemporaries, if he had asked\r\nwhether God had a pair of kidneys. We know a little more about the\r\nkidneys nowadays. If he had asked me, I should answer: why not? The\r\nkidneys are so beautifully organized; they do their work of regulation\r\nwith such a miraculous\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s hard to find another word\xe2\x80\x94such a positively\r\ndivine precision, such knowledge and wisdom, that there\xe2\x80\x99s no reason why\r\nyour archetypal man, whoever he is, or any one else, for that matter,\r\nshould be ashamed of owning a pair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColeman clapped his hands. \xe2\x80\x9cThe key,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, \xe2\x80\x9cthe key. Out of the\r\ntrouser pocket of babes and sucklings it comes. The genuine, the unique\r\nYale. How right I was to come here to-night! But, holy Sephiroth,\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s my trollop.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe picked up his stick, jumped from his chair and threaded his way\r\nbetween the tables. A woman was standing near the door. Coleman came up\r\nto her, pointed without speaking to the table, and returned, driving her\r\nalong in front of him, tapping her gently over the haunches with his\r\nstick, as one might drive a docile animal to the slaughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAllow me to introduce,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coleman. \xe2\x80\x9cThe sharer of my joys and\r\nsorrows. _La compagne de mes nuits blanches et de mes jours plut\xc3\xb4t\r\nsales._ In a word, Zoe. _Qui ne comprend pas le fran\xc3\xa7ais, qui me d\xc3\xa9teste\r\navec une passion \xc3\xa9gale \xc3\xa0 la mienne, et qui mangera, ma foi, des rognons\r\npour faire honneur au physiologue._\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave some Burgundy?\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril proffered the bottle.\r\n\r\nZoe nodded and pushed forward her glass. She was dark-haired, had a pale\r\nskin and eyes like round blackberries. Her mouth was small and floridly\r\ncurved. She was dressed, rather depressingly, like a picture by Augustus\r\nJohn, in blue and orange. Her expression was sullen and ferocious, and\r\nshe looked about her with an air of profound contempt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShearwater\xe2\x80\x99s no better than a mystic,\xe2\x80\x9d fluted Mr. Mercaptan. \xe2\x80\x9cA\r\nmystical scientist; really, one hadn\xe2\x80\x99t reckoned on that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike a Liberal Pope,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor Metternich, you remember? Pio\r\nNono.\xe2\x80\x9d And he burst into a fit of esoteric laughter. \xe2\x80\x9cOf less than\r\naverage intelligence,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured delightedly, and refilled his glass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s only the deliberately blind who wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t reckon on the\r\ncombination,\xe2\x80\x9d Lypiatt put in, indignantly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are science and art,\r\nwhat are religion and philosophy but so many expressions in human terms\r\nof some reality more than human? Newton and Boehme and Michelangelo\xe2\x80\x94what\r\nare they doing but expressing, in different ways, different aspects of\r\nthe same thing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlberti, I beg you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril. \xe2\x80\x9cI assure you he was the better\r\narchitect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Fi donc!_\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mercaptan. \xe2\x80\x9cSan Carlo alle Quatro Fontane\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d But\r\nhe got no further. Lypiatt abolished him with a gesture.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne reality,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, \xe2\x80\x9cthere is only one reality.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne reality,\xe2\x80\x9d Coleman reached out a hand across the table and caressed\r\nZoe\xe2\x80\x99s bare white arm, \xe2\x80\x9cand that is callipygous.\xe2\x80\x9d Zoe jabbed at his hand\r\nwith her fork.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are all trying to talk about it,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Lypiatt. \xe2\x80\x9cThe physicists\r\nhave formulated their laws, which are after all no more than stammering\r\nprovisional theories about a part of it. The physiologists are\r\npenetrating into the secrets of life, psychologists into the mind. And\r\nwe artists are trying to say what is revealed to us about the moral\r\nnature, the personality of that reality, which is the universe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Mercaptan threw up his hands in affected horror. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, _barbaridad,\r\nbarbaridad_!\xe2\x80\x9d Nothing less than the pure Castilian would relieve his\r\nfeelings. \xe2\x80\x9cBut all this is meaningless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite right about the chemists and physicists,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shearwater.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re always trying to pretend that they\xe2\x80\x99re nearer the truth than we\r\nare. They take their crude theories as facts and try to make us accept\r\nthem when we\xe2\x80\x99re dealing with life. Oh, they are sacred, their theories.\r\nLaws of Nature they call them; and they talk about their known truths\r\nand our romantic biological fancies. What a fuss they make when we talk\r\nabout life! Bloody fools!\xe2\x80\x9d said Shearwater, mild and crushing. \xe2\x80\x9cNobody\r\nbut a fool could talk of mechanism in face of the kidneys. And there are\r\nactually imbeciles who talk about the mechanism of heredity and\r\nreproduction.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the same,\xe2\x80\x9d began Mr. Mercaptan very earnestly, anxious to deny his\r\nown life, \xe2\x80\x9cthere are eminent authorities. I can only quote what they\r\nsay, of course. I can\xe2\x80\x99t pretend to know anything about it myself. But\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReproduction, reproduction,\xe2\x80\x9d Coleman murmured the word to himself\r\necstatically. \xe2\x80\x9cDelightful and horrifying to think they all come to that,\r\neven the most virginal; that they were all made for that, little\r\nshe-dogs, in spite of their china blue eyes. What sort of a mandrake\r\nshall we produce, Zoe and I?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, turning to Shearwater. \xe2\x80\x9cHow I\r\nshould like to have a child,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on without waiting for an answer.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t teach it anything; no language, nothing at all. Just a\r\nchild of nature. I believe it would really be the devil. And then what\r\nfun it would be if it suddenly started to say \xe2\x80\x98Bekkos,\xe2\x80\x99 like the\r\nchildren in Herodotus. And Buonarotti here would paint an allegorical\r\npicture of it and write an epic called \xe2\x80\x98The Ignoble Savage.\xe2\x80\x99 And Castor\r\nFiber would come and sound its kidneys and investigate its sexual\r\ninstincts. And Mercaptan would write one of his inimitable middle\r\narticles about it. And Gumbril would make it a pair of patent trousers.\r\nAnd Zoe and I would look parentally on and fairly swell with pride.\r\nShouldn\xe2\x80\x99t we, Zoe?\xe2\x80\x9d Zoe preserved her expression of sullen, unchanging\r\ncontempt and did not deign to answer. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, how delightful it would be! I\r\nlong for posterity. I live in hopes. I stope against Stopes. I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nZoe threw a piece of bread, which caught him on the cheek, a little\r\nbelow the eye. Coleman leaned back and laughed and laughed till the\r\ntears rolled down his face.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                               CHAPTER V\r\n\r\n\r\nOne after another, they engaged themselves in the revolving doors of the\r\nrestaurant, trotted round in the moving cage of glass and ejected\r\nthemselves into the coolness and darkness of the street. Shearwater\r\nlifted up his large face and took two or three deep breaths. \xe2\x80\x9cToo much\r\ncarbon dioxide and ammonia in there,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is unfortunate that when two or three are gathered together in God\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nname, or even in the more civilized name of Mercaptan of the delicious\r\nmiddle,\xe2\x80\x9d Mercaptan dexterously parried the prod which Coleman aimed at\r\nhim, \xe2\x80\x9cit is altogether deplorable that they should necessarily empest\r\nthe air.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLypiatt had turned his eyes heavenwards. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat stars,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nwhat prodigious gaps between the stars!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA real light opera summer night.\xe2\x80\x9d And Mercaptan began to sing, in\r\nfragmentary German, the \xe2\x80\x98Barcarolle\xe2\x80\x99 from the _Tales of Hoffmann_.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLiebe Nacht, du sch\xc3\xb6ne Nacht, oh stille mein tumpty-tum. Te, tum, Te\r\ntum.... Delicious Offenbach. Ah, if only we could have a third Empire!\r\nAnother comic Napoleon! That would make Paris look like Paris again.\r\nTiddy, tumpty-ti-tum.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey walked along without any particular destination, but simply for the\r\nsake of walking through this soft cool night. Coleman led the way,\r\ntapping the pavement at every step with the ferrule of his stick. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nblind leading the blind,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, if only there were a ditch,\r\na crevasse, a great hole full of stinging centipedes and dung. How\r\ngleefully I should lead you all into it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you would do well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shearwater gravely, \xe2\x80\x9cto go and see a\r\ndoctor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColeman gave vent to a howl of delight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes it occur to you,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cthat at this moment we are walking\r\nthrough the midst of seven million distinct and separate individuals,\r\neach with distinct and separate lives and all completely indifferent to\r\nour existence? Seven million people, each one of whom thinks himself\r\nquite as important as each of us does. Millions of them are now sleeping\r\nin an empested atmosphere. Hundreds of thousands of couples are at this\r\nmoment engaged in mutually caressing one another in a manner too hideous\r\nto be thought of, but in no way differing from the manner in which each\r\nof us performs, delightfully, passionately and beautifully, his similar\r\nwork of love. Thousands of women are now in the throes of parturition,\r\nand of both sexes thousands are dying of the most diverse and appalling\r\ndiseases, or simply because they have lived too long. Thousands are\r\ndrunk, thousands have over-eaten, thousands have not had enough to eat.\r\nAnd they are all alive, all unique and separate and sensitive, like you\r\nand me. It\xe2\x80\x99s a horrible thought. Ah, if I could lead them all into that\r\ngreat hole of centipedes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe tapped and tapped on the pavement in front of him, as though\r\nsearching for the crevasse. At the top of his voice he began to chant:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO all ye Beasts and Cattle, curse ye the Lord: curse him and vilify him\r\nfor ever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll this religion,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Mercaptan. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat with Lypiatt on one side,\r\nbeing a muscular Christian artist, and Coleman on the other, howling the\r\nblack mass.... Really!\xe2\x80\x9d He elaborated an Italianate gesture, and turned\r\nto Zoe. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think of it all?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\nZoe jerked her head in Coleman\xe2\x80\x99s direction. \xe2\x80\x9cI think e\xe2\x80\x99s a bloody\r\nswine,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. They were the first words she had spoken since she had\r\njoined the party.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear, hear!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Coleman, and he waved his stick.\r\n\r\nIn the warm yellow light of the coffee-stall at Hyde Park Corner\r\nloitered a little group of people. Among the peaked caps and the\r\nchauffeurs\xe2\x80\x99 dust-coats, among the weather-stained workmen\xe2\x80\x99s jackets and\r\nthe knotted handkerchiefs, there emerged an alien elegance. A tall tubed\r\nhat and a silk-faced overcoat, a cloak of flame-coloured satin, and in\r\nbright, coppery hair a great Spanish comb of carved tortoiseshell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99m damned,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril as they approached. \xe2\x80\x9cI believe it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nMyra Viveash.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lypiatt, peering in his turn. He began suddenly to walk\r\nwith an affected swagger, kicking his heels at every step. Looking at\r\nhimself from outside, his divining eyes pierced through the veil of\r\ncynical _je-m\xe2\x80\x99en-fichisme_ to the bruised heart beneath. Besides, he\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t want any one to guess.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Viveash is it?\xe2\x80\x9d Coleman quickened his rapping along the pavement.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd who is the present incumbent?\xe2\x80\x9d He pointed at the top hat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan it be Bruin Opps?\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril dubiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOpps!\xe2\x80\x9d Coleman yelled out the name. \xe2\x80\x9cOpps!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe top hat turned, revealing a shirt front, a long grey face, a glitter\r\nof circular glass over the left eye. \xe2\x80\x9cWho the devil are you?\xe2\x80\x9d The voice\r\nwas harsh and arrogantly offensive.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am that I am,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coleman. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I have with me\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he pointed to\r\nShearwater, to Gumbril, to Zoe\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9ca physiologue, a pedagogue and a\r\npriapagogue; for I leave out of account mere artists and journalists\r\nwhose titles do not end with the magic syllable. And finally,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nindicating himself, \xe2\x80\x9cplain Dog, which being interpreted kabbalistically\r\nbackwards, signifies God. All at your service.\xe2\x80\x9d He took off his hat and\r\nbowed.\r\n\r\nThe top hat turned back towards the Spanish comb. \xe2\x80\x9cWho is this horrible\r\ndrunk?\xe2\x80\x9d it inquired.\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash did not answer him, but stepped forward to meet the\r\nnewcomers. In one hand she held a peeled, hard-boiled egg and a thick\r\nslice of bread and butter in the other, and between her sentences she\r\nbit at them alternately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cColeman!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, and her voice, as she spoke, seemed always on\r\nthe point of expiring, as though each word were the last, utterly\r\nfaintly and breakingly from a death-bed\xe2\x80\x94the last, with all the profound\r\nand nameless significance of the ultimate word. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a very long time\r\nsince I heard you raving last. And you, Theodore darling, why do I never\r\nsee you now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGumbril shrugged his shoulders. \xe2\x80\x9cBecause you don\xe2\x80\x99t want to, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said.\r\n\r\nMyra laughed and took another bite at her bread and butter.... She laid\r\nthe back of her hand\xe2\x80\x94for she was still holding the butt end of her\r\nhard-boiled egg\xe2\x80\x94on Lypiatt\xe2\x80\x99s arm. The Titan, who had been looking at the\r\nsky, seemed to be surprised to find her standing there. \xe2\x80\x9cYou?\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\nsmiling and wrinkling up his forehead interrogatively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s to-morrow I\xe2\x80\x99m sitting for you, Casimir, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you remembered.\xe2\x80\x9d The veil parted for a moment. Poor Lypiatt! \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nhappy Mercaptan? Always happy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGallantly Mercaptan kissed the back of the hand which held the egg. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nmight be happier,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured, rolling up at her from the snouty face a\r\npair of small brown eyes. \xe2\x80\x9c_Puis-je esp\xc3\xa9rer?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash laughed expiringly from her inward death-bed and turned on\r\nhim, without speaking, her pale unwavering glance. Her eyes had a\r\nformidable capacity for looking and expressing nothing; they were like\r\nthe pale blue eyes which peer out of the Siamese cat\xe2\x80\x99s black velvet\r\nmask.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBellissima,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Mercaptan, flowering under their cool light.\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash addressed herself to the company at large. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have had the\r\nmost appalling evening,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t we, Bruin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruin Opps said nothing, but only scowled. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t like these damned\r\nintruders. The skin of his contracted brows oozed over the rim of his\r\nmonocle, on to the shining glass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought it would be fun,\xe2\x80\x9d Myra went on, \xe2\x80\x9cto go to that place at\r\nHampton Court, where you have dinner on an island and dance....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is there about islands,\xe2\x80\x9d put in Mercaptan, in a deliciously\r\nwhimsical parenthesis, \xe2\x80\x9cthat makes them so peculiarly voluptuous?\r\nCythera, Monkey Island, Capri. _Je me demande._\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnother charming middle.\xe2\x80\x9d Coleman pointed his stick menacingly; Mr.\r\nMercaptan stepped quickly out of range.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo we took a cab,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Viveash continued, \xe2\x80\x9cand set out. And what a\r\ncab, my God! A cab with only one gear and that the lowest. A cab as old\r\nas the century, a museum specimen, a collector\xe2\x80\x99s piece.\xe2\x80\x9d They had been\r\nhours and hours on the way. And when they got there, the food they were\r\noffered to eat, the wine they were expected to drink! From her eternal\r\ndeath-bed Mrs. Viveash cried out in unaffected horror. Everything tasted\r\nas though it has been kept soaking for a week in the river before being\r\nserved up\xe2\x80\x94rather weedy, with that delicious typhoid flavour of Thames\r\nwater. There was Thames even in the champagne. They had not been able to\r\neat so much as a crust of bread. Hungry and thirsty, they had\r\nre-embarked in their antique taxi, and here, at last, they were, at the\r\nfirst outpost of civilization, eating for dear life.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, a terrible evening,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Viveash concluded. \xe2\x80\x9cThe only thing which\r\nkept up my spirits was the spectacle of Bruin\xe2\x80\x99s bad temper. You\xe2\x80\x99ve no\r\nidea, Bruin, what an incomparable comic you can be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruin ignored the remark. With an expression of painfully repressed\r\ndisgust he was eating a hard-boiled egg. Myra\xe2\x80\x99s caprices were becoming\r\nmore and more impossible. That Hampton Court business had been bad\r\nenough; but when it came to eating in the street, in the middle of a lot\r\nof filthy workmen\xe2\x80\x94well, really, that was rather too much.\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash looked about her. \xe2\x80\x9cAm I never to know who this mysterious\r\nperson is?\xe2\x80\x9d She pointed to Shearwater, who was standing a little apart\r\nfrom the group, his back leaning against the Park railings and staring\r\nthoughtfully at the ground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe physiologue,\xe2\x80\x9d Coleman explained, \xe2\x80\x9cand he has the key. The key, the\r\nkey!\xe2\x80\x9d He hammered the pavement with his stick.\r\n\r\nGumbril performed the introduction in more commonplace style.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to take much interest in us, Mr. Shearwater,\xe2\x80\x9d Myra\r\ncalled expiringly. Shearwater looked up; Mrs. Viveash regarded him\r\nintently through pale, unwavering eyes, smiling as she looked that\r\nqueer, downward-turning smile which gave to her face, through its mask\r\nof laughter, a peculiar expression of agony. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to take\r\nmuch interest in us,\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated.\r\n\r\nShearwater shook his heavy head. \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should I? There\xe2\x80\x99s not time to be interested in everything. One can\r\nonly be interested in what\xe2\x80\x99s worth while.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd we\xe2\x80\x99re not worth while?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot to me personally,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Shearwater with candour. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Great Wall\r\nof China, the political situation in Italy, the habits of Trematodes\xe2\x80\x94all\r\nthese are most interesting in themselves. But they aren\xe2\x80\x99t interesting to\r\nme; I don\xe2\x80\x99t permit them to be. I haven\xe2\x80\x99t the leisure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what do you allow yourself to be interested in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall we go?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruin impatiently; he had succeeded in swallowing\r\nthe last fragment of his hard-boiled egg. Mrs. Viveash did not answer,\r\ndid not even look at him.\r\n\r\nShearwater, who had hesitated before replying, was about to speak. But\r\nColeman answered for him. \xe2\x80\x9cBe respectful,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Mrs. Viveash.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a great man. He reads no papers, not even those in which our\r\nMercaptan so beautifully writes. He does not know what a beaver is. And\r\nhe lives for nothing but the kidneys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash smiled her smile of agony. \xe2\x80\x9cKidneys? But what a _memento\r\nmori_. There are other portions of the anatomy.\xe2\x80\x9d She threw back her\r\ncloak revealing an arm, a bare shoulder, a slant of pectoral muscle. She\r\nwas wearing a white dress that, leaving her back and shoulders bare,\r\ncame up, under either arm, to a point in front and was held there by a\r\ngolden thread about the neck. \xe2\x80\x9cFor example,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and twisted her\r\nhand several times over and over, making the slender arm turn at the\r\nelbow, as though to demonstrate the movement of the articulations and\r\nthe muscular play.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Memento vivere_,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mercaptan aptly commented. \xe2\x80\x9c_Vivamus, mea\r\nLesbia, atque amemus._\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash dropped her arm and pulled the cloak back into place. She\r\nlooked at Shearwater, who had followed all her movements with\r\nconscientious attention, and who now nodded with an expression of\r\ninterrogation on his face, as though to ask: what next?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe all know that you\xe2\x80\x99ve got beautiful arms,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruin angrily.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s no need for you to make an exhibition of them in the street, at\r\nmidnight. Let\xe2\x80\x99s get out of this.\xe2\x80\x9d He laid his hand on her shoulder and\r\nmade as if to draw her away. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99d better be going. Goodness knows\r\nwhat\xe2\x80\x99s happening behind us.\xe2\x80\x9d He indicated with a little movement of the\r\nhead the loiterers round the coffee-stall. \xe2\x80\x9cSome disturbance among the\r\n_canaille_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash looked round. The cab-drivers and the other consumers of\r\nmidnight coffee had gathered in an interested circle, curious and\r\nsympathetic, round the figure of a woman who was sitting, like a limp\r\nbundle tied up in black cotton and mackintosh, on the stall-keeper\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhigh stool, leaning wearily against the wall of the booth. A man stood\r\nbeside her drinking tea out of a thick white cup. Every one was talking\r\nat once.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMayn\xe2\x80\x99t the poor wretches talk?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Viveash, turning back to\r\nBruin. \xe2\x80\x9cI never knew any one who had the lower classes on the brain as\r\nmuch as you have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI loathe them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruin. \xe2\x80\x9cI hate every one poor, or ill, or old.\r\nCan\xe2\x80\x99t abide them; they make me positively sick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Quelle \xc3\xa2me bien-n\xc3\xa9e_,\xe2\x80\x9d piped Mr. Mercaptan. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd how well and frankly\r\nyou express what we all feel and lack the courage to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLypiatt gave vent to indignant laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI remember when I was a little boy,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruin went on, \xe2\x80\x9cmy old grandfather\r\nused to tell me stories about his childhood. He told me that when he was\r\nabout five or six, just before the passing of the Reform Bill of\r\n\xe2\x80\x99thirty-two, there was a song which all right-thinking people used to\r\nsing, with a chorus that went like this: \xe2\x80\x98Rot the People, blast the\r\nPeople, damn the Lower Classes.\xe2\x80\x99 I wish I knew the rest of the words and\r\nthe tune. It must have been a good song.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColeman was enraptured with the song. He shouldered his walking-stick\r\nand began marching round and round the nearest lamp-post chanting the\r\nwords to a stirring march tune. \xe2\x80\x9cRot the People, blast the People....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHe marked the rhythm with heavy stamps of his feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, if only they\xe2\x80\x99d invent servants with internal combustion engines,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Bruin, almost pathetically. \xe2\x80\x9cHowever well trained they are, they\r\nalways betray their humanity occasionally. He had been most horribly in love.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you think,\xe2\x80\x9d he asked abruptly, \xe2\x80\x9cof Myra Viveash?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink?\xe2\x80\x9d said Shearwater. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I thought very much about\r\nher. Not a case for ratiocination exactly, is she? She seemed to me\r\nentertaining enough, as women go. I said I\xe2\x80\x99d lunch with her on\r\nThursday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGumbril felt, all of a sudden, the need to speak confidentially. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\r\nwas a time,\xe2\x80\x9d he said in a tone that was quite unreally airy, off-hand\r\nand disengaged, \xe2\x80\x9cyears ago, when I totally lost my head about her.\r\nTotally.\xe2\x80\x9d Those tear-wet patches on his pillow, cold against his cheek\r\nin the darkness; and oh, the horrible pain of weeping, vainly, for\r\nsomething that was nothing, that was everything in the world! \xe2\x80\x9cTowards\r\nthe end of the war it was. I remember walking up this dismal street one\r\nnight, in the pitch darkness, writhing with jealousy.\xe2\x80\x9d He was silent.\r\nSpectrally, like a dim, haunting ghost, he had hung about her; dumbly,\r\ndumbly imploring, appealing. \xe2\x80\x9cThe weak, silent man,\xe2\x80\x9d she used to call\r\nhim. And once for two or three days, out of pity, out of affection, out\r\nof a mere desire, perhaps, to lay the tiresome ghost, she had given him\r\nwhat his mournful silence implored\xe2\x80\x94only to take it back, almost as soon\r\nas accorded. That other night, when he had walked up this street before,\r\ndesire had eaten out his vitals and his body seemed empty, sickeningly\r\nand achingly void; jealousy was busily reminding him, with an unflagging\r\nmalice, of her beauty\xe2\x80\x94of her beauty and the hateful, ruffian hands which\r\nnow caressed, the eyes which looked on it. That was all long ago.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is certainly handsome,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shearwater, commenting, at one or two\r\nremoves, on Gumbril\xe2\x80\x99s last remark. \xe2\x80\x9cI can see that she might make any\r\none who got involved in her decidedly uncomfortable.\xe2\x80\x9d After a day or\r\ntwo\xe2\x80\x99s continuous sweating, it suddenly occurred to him, one might\r\nperhaps find sea-water more refreshing than fresh water. That would be\r\nqueer.\r\n\r\nGumbril burst out ferociously laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cBut there were other times,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nwent on jauntily, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen other people were jealous of me.\xe2\x80\x9d Ah, revenge,\r\nrevenge. In the better world of the imagination it was possible to get\r\none\xe2\x80\x99s own back. What fiendish vendettas were there carried to successful\r\nends! \xe2\x80\x9cI remember once writing her a quatrain in French.\xe2\x80\x9d (He had\r\nwritten it years after the whole thing was over, he had never sent it to\r\nany one at all; but that was all one.) \xe2\x80\x9cHow did it go? Ah, yes.\xe2\x80\x9d And he\r\nrecited, with suitable gestures:\r\n\r\n                  \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Puisque nous sommes l\xc3\xa0, je dois,\r\n                    Vous avertir, sans trop de honte,\r\n                  Que je n\xe2\x80\x99\xc3\xa9gale pas le Comte\r\n                    Casanovesque de Sixfois.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nRather prettily turned, I flatter myself. Rather elegantly gross.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGumbril\xe2\x80\x99s laughter went hooting past the Marble Arch. It stopped rather\r\nsuddenly, however, at the corner of the Edgware Road. He had suddenly\r\nremembered Mr. Mercaptan, and the thought depressed him.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                               CHAPTER VI\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was between Whitefield Street and the Tottenham Court Road, in a\r\n\xe2\x80\x98heavenly Mews,\xe2\x80\x99 as he liked to call it (for he had a characteristic\r\nweakness for philosophical paronomasia), that Casimir Lypiatt lived and\r\nworked. You passed under an archway of bald and sooty brick\xe2\x80\x94and at\r\nnight, when the green gas-lamp underneath the arch threw livid lights\r\nand enormous architectural shadows, you could fancy yourself at the\r\nentrance of one of Piranesi\xe2\x80\x99s prisons\xe2\x80\x94and you found yourself in a long\r\ncul-de-sac, flanked on either side by low buildings, having stabling for\r\nhorses below and, less commodiously, stabling for human beings in the\r\nattics above. An old-fashioned smell of animals mingled with the more\r\nprogressive stink of burnt oil. The air was a little thicker here, it\r\nseemed, than in the streets outside; looking down the mews on even the\r\nclearest day, you could see the forms of things dimming and softening,\r\nthe colours growing richer and deeper with every yard of distance. It\r\nwas the best place in the world, Lypiatt used to say, for studying\r\naerial perspective; that was why he lived there. But you always felt\r\nabout poor Lypiatt that he was facing misfortune with a jest a little\r\ntoo self-consciously.\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash\xe2\x80\x99s taxi drove in under the Piranesian arch, drove in slowly\r\nand as though with a gingerly reluctance to soil its white wheels on\r\npavements so sordid. The cabman looked round inquiringly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis right?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\nWith a white-gloved finger Mrs. Viveash prodded the air two or three\r\ntimes, indicating that he was to drive straight on. Half-way down the\r\nmews she rapped the glass; the man drew up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever been down _\xe2\x80\x99ere_ before,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, for the sake of making a\r\nlittle conversation, while Mrs. Viveash fumbled for her money. He looked\r\nat her with a polite and slightly ironic curiosity that was frankly\r\nmingled with admiration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re lucky,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash. \xe2\x80\x9cWe poor decayed gentlewomen\xe2\x80\x94you see\r\nwhat we\xe2\x80\x99re reduced to.\xe2\x80\x9d And she handed him a florin.\r\n\r\nSlowly the taxi-man unbuttoned his coat and put the coin away in an\r\ninner pocket. He watched her as she crossed the dirty street, placing\r\nher feet with a meticulous precision one after the other in the same\r\nstraight line, as though she were treading a knife edge between goodness\r\nonly knew what invisible gulfs. Floating she seemed to go, with a little\r\nspring at every step and the skirt of her summery dress\xe2\x80\x94white it was,\r\nwith a florid pattern printed in black all over it\xe2\x80\x94blowing airily out\r\naround her swaying march. Decayed gentlewomen indeed! The driver started\r\nhis machine with an unnecessary violence; he felt, for some reason,\r\npositively indignant.\r\n\r\nBetween the broad double-doors through which the horses passed to their\r\nfodder and repose were little narrow human doors\xe2\x80\x94for the Yahoos, Lypiatt\r\nused to say in his large allusive way; and when he said it he laughed\r\nwith the loud and bell-mouthed cynicism of one who sees himself as a\r\nmisunderstood and embittered Prometheus. At one of these little Yahoo\r\ndoors Mrs. Viveash halted and rapped as loudly as a small and\r\nstiff-hinged knocker would permit. Patiently she waited; several small\r\nand dirty children collected to stare at her. She knocked again and\r\nagain waited. More children came running up from the farther end of the\r\nmews; two young girls of fifteen or sixteen appeared at a neighbouring\r\ndoorway and immediately gave tongue in whoops of mirthless, hyena-like\r\nlaughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you ever read about the pied piper of Hamelin?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Viveash asked\r\nthe nearest child. Terrified, it shrank away. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought not,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\nand knocked again.\r\n\r\nThere was a sound, at last, of heavy feet slowly descending steep\r\nstairs; the door opened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWelcome to the palazzo!\xe2\x80\x9d It was Lypiatt\xe2\x80\x99s heroic formula of\r\nhospitality.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWelcome at last,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Viveash corrected, and followed him up a narrow,\r\ndark staircase that was as steep as a ladder. He was dressed in a\r\nvelveteen jacket and linen trousers that should have been white, but\r\nneeded washing. He was dishevelled and his hands were dirty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you knock more than once?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, looking back over his\r\nshoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore than twenty times,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Viveash justifiably exaggerated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m infinitely sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Lypiatt. \xe2\x80\x9cI get so deeply absorbed in\r\nmy work, you know. Did you wait long?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe children enjoyed it, at any rate.\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Viveash was irritated by a\r\nsuspicion, which was probably, after all, quite unjustified, that\r\nCasimir had been rather consciously absorbed in his work; that he had\r\nheard her first knock and plunged the more profoundly into those depths\r\nof absorption where the true artist always dwells, or at any rate ought\r\nto dwell; to rise at her third appeal with a slow, pained reluctance,\r\ncursing, perhaps, at the importunity of a world which thus noisily\r\ninterrupted the flow of his inspiration. \xe2\x80\x9cQueer, the way they stare at\r\none,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, with a note in her dying voice of a petulance that\r\nthe children had not inspired. \xe2\x80\x9cDoes one look such a guy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLypiatt threw open the door at the head of the stairs and stood there on\r\nthe threshold, waiting for her. \xe2\x80\x9cQueer?\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cNot a bit.\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nas she moved past him into the room, he laid his hand on her shoulder\r\nand fell into step with her, leaving the door to slam behind them.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMerely an example of the mob\xe2\x80\x99s instinctive dislike of the aristocratic\r\nindividual. That\xe2\x80\x99s all. \xe2\x80\x98Oh, why was I born with a different face?\xe2\x80\x99\r\nThank God I was, though. And so were you. But the difference has its\r\ndisadvantages; the children throw stones.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey didn\xe2\x80\x99t throw stones.\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Viveash was too truthful, this time.\r\n\r\nThey halted in the middle of the studio. It was not a very large room\r\nand there were too many things in it. The easel stood near the centre of\r\nthe studio; round it Lypiatt kept a space permanently cleared. There was\r\na broad fairway leading to the door, and another, narrower and\r\ntortuously winding between boxes and piled-up furniture and tumbled\r\nbooks, gave access to his bed. There was a piano and a table permanently\r\nset with dirty plates and strewed with the relics of two or three meals.\r\nBookshelves stood on either side of the fireplace and lying on the floor\r\nwere still more books, piles on dusty piles. Mrs. Viveash stood looking\r\nat the picture on the easel (abstract again\xe2\x80\x94she didn\xe2\x80\x99t like it), and\r\nLypiatt, who had dropped his hand from her shoulder, had stepped back\r\nthe better to see her, stood earnestly looking at Mrs. Viveash.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I kiss you?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked after a silence.\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash turned towards him, smiling agonizingly, her eyebrows\r\nironically lifted, her eyes steady and calm and palely, brightly\r\ninexpressive. \xe2\x80\x9cIf it really gives you any pleasure,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t, I may say, to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou make me suffer a great deal,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lypiatt, and said it so quietly\r\nand unaffectedly, that Myra was almost startled; she was accustomed,\r\nwith Casimir, to noisier and more magniloquent protestations.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m very sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; and, really, she felt sorry. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhelp it, can I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you can\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated and his voice\r\nhad now become the voice of Prometheus in his bitterness. \xe2\x80\x9cNor can\r\ntigresses.\xe2\x80\x9d He had begun to pace up and down the unobstructed fairway\r\nbetween his easel and the door; Lypiatt liked pacing while he talked.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou like playing with the victim,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on; \xe2\x80\x9che must die slowly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nReassured, Mrs. Viveash faintly smiled. This was the familiar Casimir.\r\nSo long as he could talk like this, could talk like an old-fashioned\r\nFrench novel, it was all right; he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t really be so very unhappy.\r\nShe sat down on the nearest unencumbered chair. Lypiatt continued to\r\nwalk back and forth, waving his arms as he walked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut perhaps it\xe2\x80\x99s good for one to suffer,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cperhaps it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nunavoidable and necessary. Perhaps I ought to thank you. Can an artist\r\ndo anything if he\xe2\x80\x99s happy? Would he ever want to do anything? What is\r\nart, after all, but a protest against the horrible inclemency of life?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHe halted in front of her, with arms extended in a questioning gesture.\r\nMrs. Viveash slightly shrugged her shoulders. She really didn\xe2\x80\x99t know;\r\nshe couldn\xe2\x80\x99t answer. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, but that\xe2\x80\x99s all nonsense,\xe2\x80\x9d he burst out again,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9call rot. I want to be happy and contented and successful; and of course\r\nI should work better if I were. And I want, oh, above everything,\r\neverything, I want you: to possess you completely and exclusively and\r\njealously and for ever. And the desire is like rust corroding my heart,\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s like moth eating holes in the fabric of my mind. And you merely\r\nlaugh.\xe2\x80\x9d He threw up his hands and let them limply fall again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t laugh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash. On the contrary, she was very\r\nsorry for him; and, what was more, he rather bored her. For a few days,\r\nonce, she had thought she might be in love with him. His impetuosity had\r\nseemed a torrent strong enough to carry her away. She had found out her\r\nmistake very soon. After that he had rather amused her: and now he\r\nrather bored her. No, decidedly, she never laughed. She wondered why she\r\nstill went on seeing him. Simply because one must see some one? or why?\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you going to go on with my portrait?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\nLypiatt sighed. \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I\xe2\x80\x99d better be getting on with\r\nmy work. Work\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s the only thing. \xe2\x80\x98Portrait of a Tigress.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d The cynical\r\nTitan spoke again. \xe2\x80\x9cOr shall I call it, \xe2\x80\x98Portrait of a Woman who has\r\nnever been in Love?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat would be a very stupid title,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr, \xe2\x80\x98Portrait of the Artist\xe2\x80\x99s Heart Disease\xe2\x80\x99? That would be good, that\r\nwould be damned good!\xe2\x80\x9d Lypiatt laughed very loudly and slapped his\r\nthighs. He looked, Mrs. Viveash thought, peculiarly ugly when he\r\nlaughed. His face seemed to go all to pieces; not a corner of it but was\r\nwrinkled and distorted by the violent grimace of mirth. Even the\r\nforehead was ruined when he laughed. Foreheads are generally the human\r\npart of people\xe2\x80\x99s faces. Let the nose twitch and the mouth grin and the\r\neyes twinkle as monkeyishly as you like; the forehead can still be calm\r\nand serene, the forehead still knows how to be human. But when Casimir\r\nlaughed, his forehead joined in the general disintegrating grimace. And\r\nsometimes even when he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t laughing, when he was just vivaciously\r\ntalking, his forehead seemed to lose its calm and would twitch and\r\nwrinkle itself in a dreadful kind of agitation. \xe2\x80\x98Portrait of the\r\nArtist\xe2\x80\x99s Heart Disease\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94she didn\xe2\x80\x99t find it so very funny.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe critics would think it was a problem picture,\xe2\x80\x9d Lypiatt went on.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so it would be, by God, so it would be. You _are_ a problem. You\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nthe Sphinx. I wish I were \xc5\x92dipus and could kill you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll this mythology! Mrs. Viveash shook her head.\r\n\r\nHe made his way through the intervening litter and picked up a canvas\r\nthat was leaning with averted face against the wall near the window. He\r\nheld it out at arm\xe2\x80\x99s length and examined it, his head critically cocked\r\non one side. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s good,\xe2\x80\x9d he said softly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s good. Look at it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd, stepping out once more into the open, he propped it up against the\r\ntable so that Mrs. Viveash could see it without moving from her chair.\r\n\r\nIt was a stormy vision of her; it was Myra seen, so to speak, through a\r\ntornado. He had distorted her in the portrait, had made her longer and\r\nthinner than she really was, had turned her arms into sleek tubes and\r\nput a bright, metallic polish on the curve of her cheek. The figure in\r\nthe portrait seemed to be leaning backwards a little from the surface of\r\nthe canvas, leaning sideways too, with the twist of an ivory statuette\r\ncarved out of the curving tip of a great tusk. Only somehow in Lypiatt\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nportrait the curve seemed to lack grace, it was without point, it had no\r\nsense.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve made me look,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash at last, \xe2\x80\x9cas though I were\r\nbeing blown out of shape by the wind.\xe2\x80\x9d All this show of violence\xe2\x80\x94what\r\nwas the point of it? She didn\xe2\x80\x99t like it, she didn\xe2\x80\x99t like it at all. But\r\nCasimir was delighted with her comment. He slapped his thighs and once\r\nmore laughed his restless, sharp-featured face to pieces.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, by God,\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted, \xe2\x80\x9cby God, that\xe2\x80\x99s right! Blown out of shape by\r\nthe wind. That\xe2\x80\x99s it: you\xe2\x80\x99ve said it.\xe2\x80\x9d He began stamping up and down the\r\nroom again, gesticulating. \xe2\x80\x9cThe wind, the great wind that\xe2\x80\x99s in me.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nstruck his forehead. \xe2\x80\x9cThe wind of life, the wild west wind. I feel it\r\ninside me, blowing, blowing. It carries me along with it; for though\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s inside me, it\xe2\x80\x99s more than I am, it\xe2\x80\x99s a force that comes from\r\nsomewhere else, it\xe2\x80\x99s Life itself, it\xe2\x80\x99s God. It blows me along in the\r\nteeth of opposing fate, it makes me work on, fight on.\xe2\x80\x9d He was like a\r\nman who walks along a sinister road at night and sings to keep up his\r\nown spirits, to emphasize and magnify his own existence. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd when I\r\npaint, when I write or improvise my music, it bends the things I have in\r\nmy mind, it pushes them in one direction, so that everything I do has\r\nthe look of a tree that streams north-east with all its branches and all\r\nits trunk from the root upwards, as though it were trying to run from\r\nbefore the Atlantic gale.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLypiatt stretched out his two hands and, with fingers splayed out to the\r\nwidest and trembling in the excessive tension of the muscles, moved them\r\nslowly upwards and sideways, as though he were running his palms up the\r\nstem of a little wind-wizened tree on a hilltop above the ocean.\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash continued to look at the unfinished portrait. It was as\r\nnoisy and easy and immediately effective as a Vermouth advertisement in\r\nthe streets of Padua.
