Cinzano, Bonomelli, Campari\xe2\x80\x94illustrious names.\r\nGiotto and Mantegna mouldered meanwhile in their respective chapels.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd look at this,\xe2\x80\x9d Lypiatt went on. He took down the canvas that was\r\nclamped to the easel and held it out for her inspection. It was one of\r\nCasimir\xe2\x80\x99s abstract paintings: a procession of machine-like forms rushing\r\nup diagonally from right to left across the canvas, with as it were a\r\nspray of energy blowing back from the crest of the wave towards the top\r\nright-hand corner. \xe2\x80\x9cIn this painting,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI symbolize the\r\nArtist\xe2\x80\x99s conquering spirit\xe2\x80\x94rushing on the universe, making it its own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHe began to declaim:\r\n\r\n            \xe2\x80\x9cLook down, Conquistador,\r\n            There on the valley\xe2\x80\x99s broad green floor,\r\n            There lies the lake, the jewelled cities gleam,\r\n            Chalco and Tlacopan\r\n            Awaiting the coming Man;\r\n            Look down on Mexico, Conquistador,\r\n            Land of your golden dream.\r\n\r\nOr the same idea in terms of music\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d and Lypiatt dashed to the piano\r\nand evoked a distorted ghost of Scriabin. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked\r\nfeverishly, when the ghost was laid again and the sad cheap jangling had\r\nfaded again into silence. \xe2\x80\x9cYou _feel_? The artist rushes on the world,\r\nconquers it, gives it beauty, imposes a moral significance.\xe2\x80\x9d He returned\r\nto the picture. \xe2\x80\x9cThis will be fine when it\xe2\x80\x99s finished,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTremendous. You feel the wind blowing there, too.\xe2\x80\x9d And with a pointing\r\nfinger he followed up the onrush of the forms. \xe2\x80\x9cThe great southwester\r\ndriving them on. \xe2\x80\x98Like leaves from an enchanter fleeing.\xe2\x80\x99 Only not\r\nchaotically, not in disorder. They\xe2\x80\x99re blown, so to speak, in column of\r\nfour\xe2\x80\x94by a conscious wind.\xe2\x80\x9d He leaned the canvas against the table and\r\nwas free again to march and brandish his conquering fists.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLife,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9clife\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the great, essential thing. You\xe2\x80\x99ve got to\r\nget life into your art, otherwise it\xe2\x80\x99s nothing. And life only comes out\r\nof life, out of passion and feeling; it can\xe2\x80\x99t come out of theories.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s the stupidity of all this chatter about art for art\xe2\x80\x99s sake and\r\nthe \xc3\xa6sthetic emotions and purely formal values and all that. It\xe2\x80\x99s only\r\nthe formal relations that matter; one subject is just as good as\r\nanother\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the theory. You\xe2\x80\x99ve only got to look at the pictures of\r\nthe people who put it into practice to see that it won\xe2\x80\x99t do. Life comes\r\nout of life. You must paint with passion and the passion will stimulate\r\nyour intellect to create the right formal relations. And to paint with\r\npassion, you must paint things that passionately interest you, moving\r\nthings, human things. Nobody, except a mystical pantheist, like Van\r\nGogh, can seriously be as much interested in napkins, apples and bottles\r\nas in his lover\xe2\x80\x99s face, or the resurrection, or the destiny of man.\r\nCould Mantegna have devised his splendid compositions if he had painted\r\narrangements of Chianti flasks and cheeses instead of Crucifixions,\r\nmartyrs and triumphs of great men? Nobody but a fool could believe it.\r\nAnd could I have painted that portrait if I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t loved you, if you\r\nweren\xe2\x80\x99t killing me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAh, Bonomelli and illustrious Cinzano!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPassionately I paint passion. I draw life out of life. And I wish them\r\njoy of their bottles and their Canadian apples and their muddy table\r\nnapkins with the beastly folds in them that look like loops of tripe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nOnce more Lypiatt disintegrated himself with laughter; then was silent.\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash nodded, slowly and reflectively. \xe2\x80\x9cI think you\xe2\x80\x99re right,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe said. Yes, he was surely right; there must be life, life was the\r\nimportant thing. That was precisely why his paintings were so bad\xe2\x80\x94she\r\nsaw now; there was no life in them. Plenty of noise there was, and\r\ngesticulation and a violent galvanized twitching; but no life, only the\r\ntheatrical show of it. There was a flaw in the conduit; somewhere\r\nbetween the man and his work life leaked out. He protested too much. But\r\nit was no good; there was no disguising the deadness. Her portrait was a\r\ndancing mummy. He bored her now. Did she even positively dislike him?\r\nBehind her unchanging pale eyes Mrs. Viveash wondered. But in any case,\r\nshe reflected, one needn\xe2\x80\x99t always like the people with whom one\r\nassociates. There are music halls as well as confidential boudoirs; some\r\npeople are admitted to the tea-party and the _t\xc3\xaate-\xc3\xa0-t\xc3\xaate_, others, on a\r\nstage invisible, poor things! to themselves, do their little\r\nsong-and-dance, roll out their characteristic patter, and having\r\nprovided you with your entertainment are dismissed with their due share\r\nof applause. But then, what if they become boring?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lypiatt at last\xe2\x80\x94he had stood there, motionless, for a long\r\ntime, biting his nails, \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose we\xe2\x80\x99d better begin our sitting.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\npicked up the unfinished portrait and adjusted it on the easel. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nwasted a lot of time,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand there isn\xe2\x80\x99t, after all, so much of\r\nit to waste.\xe2\x80\x9d He spoke gloomily, and his whole person had become, all of\r\na sudden, curiously shrunken and deflated. \xe2\x80\x9cThere isn\xe2\x80\x99t so much of it,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe repeated, and sighed. \xe2\x80\x9cI still think of myself as a young man, young\r\nand promising, don\xe2\x80\x99t you know. Casimir Lypiatt\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s a young, promising\r\nsort of name, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it? But I\xe2\x80\x99m not young, I\xe2\x80\x99ve passed the age of\r\npromise. Every now and then I realize it, and it\xe2\x80\x99s painful, it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndepressing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash stepped up on to the model\xe2\x80\x99s dais and took her seat. \xe2\x80\x9cIs\r\nthat right?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\nLypiatt looked first at her, then at his picture. Her beauty, his\r\npassion\xe2\x80\x94were they only to meet on the canvas? Opps was her lover. Time\r\nwas passing; he felt tired. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99ll do,\xe2\x80\x9d he said and began painting.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow young are you?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked after a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwenty-five, I should imagine,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwenty-five? Good Lord, it\xe2\x80\x99s nearly fifteen years since I was\r\ntwenty-five. Fifteen years, fighting all the time. God, how I hate\r\npeople sometimes! Everybody. It\xe2\x80\x99s not their malignity I mind; I can give\r\nthem back as good as they give me. It\xe2\x80\x99s their power of silence and\r\nindifference, it\xe2\x80\x99s their capacity for making themselves deaf. Here am I\r\nwith something to say to them, something important and essential. And\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve been saying it for more than fifteen years, I\xe2\x80\x99ve been shouting it.\r\nThey pay no attention. I bring them my head and heart on a charger, and\r\nthey don\xe2\x80\x99t even notice that the things are there. I sometimes wonder how\r\nmuch longer I can manage to go on.\xe2\x80\x9d His voice had become very low, and\r\nit trembled. \xe2\x80\x9cOne\xe2\x80\x99s nearly forty, you know....\xe2\x80\x9d The voice faded huskily\r\naway into silence. Languidly and as though the business exhausted him,\r\nhe began mixing colours on his palette.\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash looked at him. No, he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t young; at the moment, indeed,\r\nhe seemed to have become much older than he really was. An old man was\r\nstanding there, peaked and sharp and worn. He had failed, he was\r\nunhappy. But the world would have been unjuster, less discriminating if\r\nit had given him success.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome people believe in you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; there was nothing else for her\r\nto say.\r\n\r\nLypiatt looked up at her. \xe2\x80\x9cYou?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash nodded, deliberately. It was a lie. But was it possible to\r\ntell the truth? \xe2\x80\x9cAnd then there is the future,\xe2\x80\x9d she reassured him, and\r\nher faint death-bed voice seemed to prophesy with a perfect certainty.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not forty yet; you\xe2\x80\x99ve got twenty, thirty years of work in front\r\nof you. And there were others, after all, who had to wait\xe2\x80\x94a long\r\ntime\xe2\x80\x94sometimes till after they were dead. Great men; Blake, for\r\ninstance....\xe2\x80\x9d She felt positively ashamed; it was like a little talk by\r\nDoctor Frank Crane. But she felt still more ashamed, when she saw that\r\nCasimir had begun to cry and that the tears were rolling, one after\r\nanother, slowly down his face.\r\n\r\nHe put down his palette, he stepped on to the dais, he came and knelt at\r\nMrs. Viveash\xe2\x80\x99s feet. He took one of her hands between his own and he\r\nbent over it, pressing it to his forehead, as though it were a charm\r\nagainst unhappy thoughts, sometimes kissing it; soon it was wet with\r\ntears. He wept almost in silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all right,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Viveash kept repeating, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s all right,\xe2\x80\x9d and she\r\nlaid her free hand on his bowed head, she patted it comfortingly as one\r\nmight pat the head of a large dog that comes and thrusts its muzzle\r\nbetween one\xe2\x80\x99s knees. She felt, even as she made it, how meaningless and\r\nunintimate the gesture was. If she had liked him, she would have run her\r\nfingers through his hair; but somehow his hair rather disgusted her.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all right, all right.\xe2\x80\x9d But, of course, it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t all right; and\r\nshe was comforting him under false pretences and he was kneeling at the\r\nfeet of somebody who simply wasn\xe2\x80\x99t there\xe2\x80\x94so utterly detached, so far\r\naway she was from all this scene and all his misery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re the only person,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last, \xe2\x80\x9cwho cares or understands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash could almost have laughed.\r\n\r\nHe began once more to kiss her hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeautiful and enchanting Myra\xe2\x80\x94you were always that. But now you\xe2\x80\x99re good\r\nand dear as well, now I know you\xe2\x80\x99re kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor Casimir!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. Why was it that people always got involved in\r\none\xe2\x80\x99s life? If only one could manage things on the principle of the\r\nrailways! Parallel tracks\xe2\x80\x94that was the thing. For a few miles you\xe2\x80\x99d be\r\nrunning at the same speed. There\xe2\x80\x99d be delightful conversation out of the\r\nwindows; you\xe2\x80\x99d exchange the omelette in your restaurant car for the\r\nvol-au-vent in theirs. And when you\xe2\x80\x99d said all there was to say, you\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nput on a little more steam, wave your hand, blow a kiss and away you\xe2\x80\x99d\r\ngo, forging ahead along the smooth, polished rails. But instead of that,\r\nthere were these dreadful accidents; the points were wrongly set, the\r\ntrains came crashing together; or people jumped on as you were passing\r\nthrough the stations and made a nuisance of themselves and wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nallow themselves to be turned off. Poor Casimir! But he irritated her,\r\nhe was a horrible bore. She ought to have stopped seeing him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t wholly dislike me, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut of course not, my poor Casimir!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you knew how horribly I loved you!\xe2\x80\x9d He looked up at her\r\ndespairingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what\xe2\x80\x99s the good?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you ever known what it\xe2\x80\x99s like to love some one so much that you\r\nfeel you could die of it? So that it hurts all the time. As though there\r\nwere a wound. Have you ever known that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash smiled her agonizing smile, nodded slowly and said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps. And one doesn\xe2\x80\x99t die, you know. One doesn\xe2\x80\x99t die.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLypiatt was leaning back, staring fixedly up at her. The tears were dry\r\non his face, his cheeks were flushed. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you know what it is,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nasked, \xe2\x80\x9cto love so much, that you begin to long for the anodyne of\r\nphysical pain to quench the pain in the soul? You don\xe2\x80\x99t know that.\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nsuddenly, with his clenched fist, he began to bang the wooden dais on\r\nwhich he was kneeling, blow after blow, with all his strength.\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash leant forward and tried to arrest his hand. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re mad,\r\nCasimir,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re mad. Don\xe2\x80\x99t do that.\xe2\x80\x9d She spoke with anger.\r\n\r\nLypiatt laughed till his face was all broken up with the grimace, and\r\nproffered for her inspection his bleeding knuckles. The skin hung in\r\nlittle white tags and tatters, and from below the blood was slowly\r\noozing up to the surface. \xe2\x80\x9cLook,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and laughed again. Then\r\nsuddenly, with an extraordinary agility, he jumped to his feet, bounded\r\nfrom the dais and began once more to stride up and down the fairway\r\nbetween his easel and the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy God,\xe2\x80\x9d he kept repeating, \xe2\x80\x9cby God, by God. I feel it in me. I can\r\nface the whole lot of you; the whole damned lot. Yes, and I shall get\r\nthe better of you yet. An Artist\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he called up that traditional ghost\r\nand it comforted him; he wrapped himself with a protective gesture\r\nwithin the ample folds of its bright mantle\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9can Artist doesn\xe2\x80\x99t fail\r\nunder unhappiness. He gets new strength from it. The torture makes him\r\nsweat new masterpieces....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe began to talk about his books, his poems and pictures; all the great\r\nthings in his head, the things he had already done. He talked about his\r\nexhibition\xe2\x80\x94ah, by God, that would astonish them, that would bowl them\r\nover, this time. The blood mounted to his face; there was a flush over\r\nthe high projecting cheek-bones. He could feel the warm blood behind his\r\neyes. He laughed aloud; he was a laughing lion. He stretched out his\r\narms; he was enormous, his arms reached out like the branches of a\r\ncedar. The Artist walked across the world and the mangy dogs ran yelping\r\nand snapping behind him. The great wind blew and blew, driving him on;\r\nit lifted him and he began to fly.\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash listened. It didn\xe2\x80\x99t look as though he would get much\r\nfurther with the portrait.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER VII\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was Press Day. The critics had begun to arrive; Mr. Albemarle\r\ncirculated among them with a ducal amiability. The young assistant\r\nhovered vaguely about, straining to hear what the great men had to say\r\nand trying to pretend that he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t eavesdropping. Lypiatt\xe2\x80\x99s pictures\r\nhung on the walls, and Lypiatt\xe2\x80\x99s catalogue, thick with its preface and\r\nits explanatory notes, was in all hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery strong,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Albemarle kept repeating, \xe2\x80\x9cvery strong indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d It\r\nwas his password for the day.\r\n\r\nLittle Mr. Clew, who represented the _Daily Post_, was inclined to be\r\nenthusiastic. \xe2\x80\x9cHow well he writes!\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Mr. Albemarle, looking up\r\nfrom the catalogue. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd how well he paints! What _impasto_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n_Impasto, impasto_\xe2\x80\x94the young assistant sidled off unobtrusively to the\r\ndesk and made a note of it. He would look the word up in Grubb\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n_Dictionary of Art and Artists_ later on. He made his way back,\r\ncircuitously and as though by accident, into Mr. Clew\xe2\x80\x99s neighbourhood.\r\n\r\nMr. Clew was one of those rare people who have a real passion for art.\r\nHe loved painting, all painting, indiscriminately. In a picture gallery\r\nhe was like a Turk in a harem; he adored them all. He loved Memling as\r\nmuch as Raphael, he loved Gr\xc3\xbcnewald and Michelangelo, Holman Hunt and\r\nManet, Romney and Tintoretto; how happy he could be with all of them!\r\nSometimes, it is true, he hated; but that was only when familiarity had\r\nnot yet bred love. At the first Post-Impressionist Exhibition, for\r\nexample, in 1911, he had taken a very firm stand. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is an obscene\r\nfarce,\xe2\x80\x9d he had written then. Now, however, there was no more passionate\r\nadmirer of Matisse\xe2\x80\x99s genius. As a connoisseur and _kunstforscher_, Mr.\r\nClew was much esteemed. People would bring him dirty old pictures to\r\nlook at, and he would exclaim at once: Why, it\xe2\x80\x99s an El Greco, a\r\nPiazetta, or some other suitable name. Asked how he knew, he would shrug\r\nhis shoulders and say: But it\xe2\x80\x99s signed all over. His certainty and his\r\nenthusiasm were infectious. Since the coming of El Greco into fashion,\r\nhe had discovered dozens of early works by that great artist. For Lord\r\nPetersfield\xe2\x80\x99s collection alone he had found four early El Grecos, all by\r\npupils of Bassano. Lord Petersfield\xe2\x80\x99s confidence in Mr. Clew was\r\nunbounded; not even that affair of the Primitives had shaken it. It was\r\na sad affair: Lord Petersfield\xe2\x80\x99s Duccio had shown signs of cracking; the\r\nestate carpenter was sent for to take a look at the panel; he had\r\nlooked. \xe2\x80\x9cA worse-seasoned piece of Illinois hickory,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nnever seen.\xe2\x80\x9d After that he looked at the Simone Martini; for that, on\r\nthe contrary, he was full of praise. Smooth-grained, well-seasoned\xe2\x80\x94it\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t crack, no, not in a hundred years. \xe2\x80\x9cA nicer slice of board\r\nnever came out of America.\xe2\x80\x9d He had a hyperbolical way of speaking. Lord\r\nPetersfield was extremely angry; he dismissed the estate carpenter on\r\nthe spot. After that he told Mr. Clew that he wanted a Giorgione, and\r\nMr. Clew went out and found him one which was signed all over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like this very much,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Clew, pointing to one of the thoughts\r\nwith which Lypiatt had prefaced his catalogue. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Genius,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d he adjusted\r\nhis spectacles and began to read aloud, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98is life. Genius is a force of\r\nnature. In art, nothing else counts. The modern impotents, who are\r\nafraid of genius and who are envious of it, have invented in\r\nself-defence the notion of the Artist. The Artist with his sense of\r\nform, his style, his devotion to pure beauty, et cetera, et cetera. But\r\nGenius includes the Artist; every Genius has, among very many others,\r\nthe qualities attributed by the impotents to the Artist. The Artist\r\nwithout genius is a carver of fountains through which no water flows.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nVery true,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Clew, \xe2\x80\x9cvery true indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d He marked the passage\r\nwith his pencil.\r\n\r\nMr. Albemarle produced the password. \xe2\x80\x9cVery strongly put,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have always felt that myself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Clew. \xe2\x80\x9cEl Greco, for\r\nexample....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood morning, what about El Greco?\xe2\x80\x9d said a voice, all in one breath.\r\nThe thin, long, skin-covered skeleton of Mr. Mallard hung over them like\r\na guilty conscience. Mr. Mallard wrote every week in the _Hebdomadal\r\nDigest_. He had an immense knowledge of art, and a sincere dislike of\r\nall that was beautiful. The only modern painter whom he really admired\r\nwas Hodler. All others were treated by him with a merciless savagery; he\r\ntore them to pieces in his weekly articles with all the holy gusto of a\r\nCalvinist iconoclast smashing images of the Virgin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat about El Greco?\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. He had a peculiarly passionate\r\nloathing of El Greco.\r\n\r\nMr. Clew smiled up at him propitiatingly; he was afraid of Mr. Mallard.\r\nHis enthusiasms were no match for Mr. Mallard\xe2\x80\x99s erudite and logical\r\ndisgusts. \xe2\x80\x9cI was merely quoting him as an example,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn example, I hope, of incompetent drawing, baroque composition,\r\ndisgusting forms, garish colouring and hysterical subject-matter.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr.\r\nMallard showed his old ivory teeth in a menacing smile. \xe2\x80\x9cThose are the\r\nonly things which El Greco\xe2\x80\x99s work exemplifies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Clew gave a nervous little laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think of these?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nasked, pointing to Lypiatt\xe2\x80\x99s canvases.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey look to me very ordinarily bad,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mr. Mallard.\r\n\r\nThe young assistant listened appalled. In a business like this, how was\r\nit possible to make good?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the same,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Clew courageously, \xe2\x80\x9cI like that bowl of roses\r\nin the window with the landscape behind. Number twenty-nine.\xe2\x80\x9d He looked\r\nin the catalogue. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd there\xe2\x80\x99s a really charming little verse about it:\r\n\r\n             \xe2\x80\x98O beauty of the rose,\r\n             Goodness as well as perfume exhaling!\r\n             Who gazes on these flowers,\r\n             On this blue hill and ripening field\xe2\x80\x94he knows\r\n             Where duty leads and that the nameless Powers\r\n             In a rose can speak their will.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nReally charming!\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Clew made another mark with his pencil.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut commonplace, commonplace.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mallard shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd in any\r\ncase a verse can\xe2\x80\x99t justify a bad picture. What an unsubtle harmony of\r\ncolour! And how uninteresting the composition is! That receding\r\ndiagonal\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s been worked to death.\xe2\x80\x9d He too made a mark in his\r\ncatalogue\xe2\x80\x94a cross and a little circle, arranged like the skull and\r\ncross-bones on a pirate\xe2\x80\x99s flag. Mr. Mallard\xe2\x80\x99s catalogues were always\r\ncovered with these little marks: they were his symbols of condemnation.\r\n\r\nMr. Albemarle, meanwhile, had moved away to greet the new arrivals. To\r\nthe critic of the _Daily Cinema_ he had to explain that there were no\r\nportraits of celebrities. The reporter from the _Evening Planet_ had to\r\nbe told which were the best pictures.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Lypiatt,\xe2\x80\x9d he dictated, \xe2\x80\x9cis a poet and philosopher as well as a\r\npainter. His catalogue is a\xe2\x80\x94h\xe2\x80\x99m\xe2\x80\x94declaration of faith.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe reporter took it down in shorthand. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd very nice too,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m most grateful to you, sir, most grateful.\xe2\x80\x9d And he hurried away, to\r\nget to the Cattle Show before the King should arrive. Mr. Albemarle\r\naffably addressed himself to the critic of the _Morning Globe_.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _al_ways regard this gallery,\xe2\x80\x9d said a loud and cheerful voice, full\r\nof bulls and canaries in chorus, \xe2\x80\x9cas positively a _mauvais lieu_. Such\r\nexhibitions!\xe2\x80\x9d And Mr. Mercaptan shrugged his shoulders expressively. He\r\nhalted to wait for his companion.\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash had lagged behind, reading the catalogue as she slowly\r\nwalked along. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a complete book,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cfull of poems and\r\nessays and short stories even, so far as I can see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the usual cracker mottoes.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mercaptan laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cI know the sort\r\nof thing. \xe2\x80\x98Look after the past and the future will look after itself.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\xe2\x80\x98God squared minus Man squared equals Art-plus-life times\r\nArt-minus-Life.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98The Higher the Art the fewer the morals\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94only that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntoo nearly good sense to have been invented by Lypiatt. But I know the\r\nsort of thing. I could go on like that for ever.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mercaptan was\r\ndelighted with himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll read you one of them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98A picture is a\r\nchemical combination of plastic form and spiritual significance.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCrikey!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mercaptan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Those who think that a picture is a matter of nothing but plastic form\r\nare like those who imagine that water is made of nothing but hydrogen.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Mercaptan made a grimace. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat writing!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed; \xe2\x80\x9c_le style\r\nc\xe2\x80\x99est l\xe2\x80\x99homme_. Lypiatt hasn\xe2\x80\x99t got a style. Argal\xe2\x80\x94inexorable\r\nconclusion\xe2\x80\x94Lypiatt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t exist. My word, though. Look at those\r\nhorrible great nudes there. Like Carracis with cubical muscles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSampson and Delilah,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash. \xe2\x80\x9cWould you like me to read\r\nabout them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash did not press the matter. Casimir, she thought, must have\r\nbeen thinking of her when he wrote this little poem about Poets and\r\nWomen, crossed genius, torments, the sweating of masterpieces. She\r\nsighed. \xe2\x80\x9cThose leopards are rather nice,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and looked at the\r\ncatalogue again. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98An animal is a symbol and its form is significant. In\r\nthe long process of adaptation, evolution has refined and simplified and\r\nshaped, till every part of the animal expresses one desire, a single\r\nidea. Man, who has become what he is, not by specialization, but by\r\ngeneralization, symbolizes with his body no one thing. He is a symbol of\r\neverything from the most hideous and ferocious bestiality to godhead.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mercaptan.\r\n\r\nA canvas of mountains and enormous clouds like nascent sculptures\r\npresented itself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Aerial Alps\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Viveash began to read.\r\n\r\n               \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Aerial Alps of amber and of snow,\r\n               Junonian flesh, and bosomy alabaster\r\n               Carved by the wind\xe2\x80\x99s uncertain hands....\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Mercaptan stopped his ears. \xe2\x80\x9cPlease, please,\xe2\x80\x9d he begged.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNumber seventeen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash, \xe2\x80\x9cis called \xe2\x80\x98Woman on a Cosmic\r\nbackground,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d A female figure stood leaning against a pillar on a\r\nhilltop, and beyond was a blue night with stars. \xe2\x80\x9cUnderneath is written:\r\n\xe2\x80\x98For one at least, she is more than the starry universe.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Viveash\r\nremembered that Lypiatt had once said very much that sort of thing to\r\nher. \xe2\x80\x9cSo many of Casimir\xe2\x80\x99s things remind me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cof those\r\nItalian vermouth advertisements. You know\xe2\x80\x94Cinzano, Bonomelli and all\r\nthese. I wish they didn\xe2\x80\x99t. This woman in white with her head in the\r\nGreat Bear....\xe2\x80\x9d She shook her head. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor Casimir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Mercaptan roared and squealed with laughter. \xe2\x80\x9cBonomelli,\xe2\x80\x9d he said;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s precisely it. What a critic, Myra! I take off my hat.\xe2\x80\x9d They\r\nmoved on. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what\xe2\x80\x99s this grand transformation scene?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash looked at the catalogue. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s called \xe2\x80\x98The Sermon on the\r\nMount,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd really, do you know, I rather like it. All that\r\ncrowd of figures slanting up the hill and the single figure on the\r\ntop\xe2\x80\x94it seems to me very dramatic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy _dear_,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Mr. Mercaptan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd in spite of everything,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash, feeling suddenly and\r\nuncomfortably that she had somehow been betraying the man, \xe2\x80\x9che\xe2\x80\x99s really\r\nvery nice, you know. Very nice, indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d Her expiring voice sounded very\r\ndecidedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, _ces femmes_,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mr. Mercaptan, \xe2\x80\x9c_ces femmes_! They\xe2\x80\x99re all\r\nPasiphaes and Ledas. They all in their hearts prefer beasts to men,\r\nsavages to civilized beings. _Even_ you, Myra, I _really_ believe.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nshook his head.\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash ignored the outburst. \xe2\x80\x9cVery nice,\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated\r\nthoughtfully. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly rather a bore....\xe2\x80\x9d Her voice expired altogether.\r\n\r\nThey continued their round of the gallery.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER VIII\r\n\r\n\r\nCritically, in the glasses of Mr. Bojanus\xe2\x80\x99s fitting-room, Gumbril\r\nexamined his profile, his back view. Inflated, the Patent Small-Clothes\r\nbulged, bulged decidedly, though with a certain gracious opulence that\r\nmight, in a person of the other sex, have seemed only deliciously\r\nnatural. In him, however, Gumbril had to admit, the opulence seemed a\r\nlittle misplaced and paradoxical. Still, if one has to suffer in order\r\nto be beautiful, one must also expect to be ugly in order not to suffer.\r\nPractically, the trousers were a tremendous success. He sat down heavily\r\non the hard wooden bench of the fitting-room and was received as though\r\non a lap of bounding resiliency; the Patent Small-Clothes, there was no\r\ndoubt, would be proof even against marble. And the coat, he comforted\r\nhimself, would mask with its skirts the too decided bulge. Or if it\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t, well, there was no help for it. One must resign oneself to\r\nbulging, that was all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery nice,\xe2\x80\x9d he declared at last.\r\n\r\nMr. Bojanus, who had been watching his client in silence and with a\r\npolite but also, Gumbril could not help feeling, a somewhat ironical\r\nsmile, coughed. \xe2\x80\x9cIt depends,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cprecisely what you mean by\r\n\xe2\x80\x98nice.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d He cocked his head on one side, and the fine waxed end of his\r\nmoustache was like a pointer aimed up at some remote star.\r\n\r\nGumbril said nothing, but catching sight once more of his own side view,\r\nnodded a dubious agreement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf by nice,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Mr. Bojanus, \xe2\x80\x9cyou mean comfortable, well and\r\ngood. If, however, you mean elegant, then, Mr. Gumbril, I fear I must\r\ndisagree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut elegance,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril, feebly playing the philosopher, \xe2\x80\x9cis only\r\nrelative, Mr. Bojanus. There are certain African negroes, among whom it\r\nis considered elegant to pierce the lips and distend them with wooden\r\nplates, until the mouth looks like a pelican\xe2\x80\x99s beak.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Bojanus placed his hand in his bosom and slightly bowed. \xe2\x80\x9cVery\r\npossibly, Mr. Gumbril,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied. \xe2\x80\x9cBut if you\xe2\x80\x99ll pardon my saying so,\r\nwe are not African negroes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGumbril was crushed, deservedly. He looked at himself again in the\r\nmirrors. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you object,\xe2\x80\x9d he asked after a pause, \xe2\x80\x9cto all eccentricities\r\nin dress, Mr. Bojanus? Would you put us all into your elegant uniform?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly not,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mr. Bojanus. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are certain walks of life\r\nin which eccentricity in appearance is positively a _sine qua non_, Mr.\r\nGumbril, and I might almost say _de rigueur_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd which walks of life, Mr. Bojanus, may I ask? You refer, perhaps, to\r\nthe artistic walks? Sombreros and Byronic collars and possibly velveteen\r\ntrousers? Though all that sort of thing is surely a little out of date,\r\nnowadays.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEnigmatically Mr. Bojanus smiled, a playful Sphinx. He thrust his right\r\nhand deeper into his bosom and with his left twisted to a finer needle\r\nthe point of his moustache. \xe2\x80\x9cNot artists, Mr. Gumbril.\xe2\x80\x9d He shook his\r\nhead. \xe2\x80\x9cIn practice they may show themselves a little eccentric and\r\nnegleejay. But they have no need to look unusual on principle. It\xe2\x80\x99s only\r\nthe politicians who need do it on principle. It\xe2\x80\x99s only _de rigueur_, as\r\none might say, in the political walks, Mr. Gumbril.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou surprise me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril. \xe2\x80\x9cI should have thought that it was to\r\nthe politician\xe2\x80\x99s interest to look respectable and normal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it is still more to his interest as a leader of men to look\r\ndistinguished,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Bojanus replied. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, not precisely\r\ndistinguished,\xe2\x80\x9d he corrected himself, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause that implies that\r\npoliticians look _distangay_, which I regret to say, Mr. Gumbril, they\r\nvery often don\xe2\x80\x99t. Distinguishable, is more what I mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEccentricity is their badge of office?\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Gumbril. He sat down\r\nluxuriously on the Patent Small-Clothes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s more like it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Bojanus, tilting his moustaches. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nleader has got to look different from the other ones. In the good old\r\ndays they always wore their official badges. The leader \xe2\x80\x99ad his livery,\r\nlike every one else, to show who he was. That was sensible, Mr. Gumbril.\r\nNowadays he has no badge\xe2\x80\x94at least not for ordinary occasions\xe2\x80\x94for I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncount Privy Councillors\xe2\x80\x99 uniforms and all that sort of once-a-year fancy\r\ndress. \xe2\x80\x99E\xe2\x80\x99s reduced to dressing in some eccentric way or making the most\r\nof the peculiarities of \xe2\x80\x99is personal appearance. A very \xe2\x80\x99apazard method\r\nof doing things, Mr. Gumbril, very \xe2\x80\x99apazard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGumbril agreed.\r\n\r\nMr. Bojanus went on, making small, neat gestures as he spoke. \xe2\x80\x9cSome of\r\nthem,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cwear \xe2\x80\x99uge collars, like Mr. Gladstone. Some wear\r\norchids and eyeglasses, like Joe Chamberlain. Some let their \xe2\x80\x99air grow,\r\nlike Lloyd George. Some wear curious \xe2\x80\x99ats, like Winston Churchill. Some\r\nput on black shirts, like this Mussolini, and some put on red ones, like\r\nGaribaldi. Some turn up their moustaches, like the German Emperor. Some\r\nturn them down, like Clemenceau. Some grow whiskers, like Tirpitz. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t speak of all the uniforms, orders, ornaments, \xe2\x80\x99ead-dresses,\r\nfeathers, crowns, buttons, tattooings, ear-rings, sashes, swords,\r\ntrains, tiaras, urims, thummims and what not, Mr. Gumbril, that \xe2\x80\x99ave\r\nbeen used in the past and in other parts of the world to distinguish the\r\nleader. We, \xe2\x80\x99oo know our \xe2\x80\x99istory, Mr. Gumbril, we know all about that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGumbril made a deprecating gesture. \xe2\x80\x9cYou speak for yourself, Mr.\r\nBojanus,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nMr. Bojanus bowed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray continue,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril.\r\n\r\nMr. Bojanus bowed again. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, Mr. Gumbril,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthe point of all\r\nthese things, as I\xe2\x80\x99ve already remarked, is to make the leader look\r\ndifferent, so that \xe2\x80\x99e can be recognized at the first _coop d\xe2\x80\x99oil_, as\r\nyou might say, by the \xe2\x80\x99erd \xe2\x80\x99e \xe2\x80\x99appens to be leading. For the \xe2\x80\x99uman \xe2\x80\x99erd,\r\nMr. Gumbril, is an \xe2\x80\x99erd which can\xe2\x80\x99t do without a leader. Sheep, for\r\nexample: I never noticed that they \xe2\x80\x99ad a leader; nor rooks. Bees, on the\r\nother \xe2\x80\x99and, I take it, \xe2\x80\x99ave. At least when they\xe2\x80\x99re swarming. Correct me,\r\nMr. Gumbril, if I\xe2\x80\x99m wrong. Natural \xe2\x80\x99istory was never, as you might say,\r\nmy _forty_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor mine,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Gumbril.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs for elephants and wolves, Mr. Gumbril, I can\xe2\x80\x99t pretend to speak of\r\nthem with first-\xe2\x80\x99and knowledge. Nor llamas, nor locusts, nor squab\r\npigeons, nor lemmings. But \xe2\x80\x99uman beings, Mr. Gumbril, those I can claim\r\nto talk of with authority, if I may say so in all modesty, and not as\r\nthe scribes. I \xe2\x80\x99ave made a special study of them, Mr. Gumbril. And my\r\nprofession \xe2\x80\x99as brought me into contact with very numerous specimens.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGumbril could not help wondering where precisely in Mr. Bojanus\xe2\x80\x99s museum\r\nhe himself had his place.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe \xe2\x80\x99uman \xe2\x80\x99erd,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Bojanus went on, \xe2\x80\x9cmust have a leader. And a leader\r\nmust have something to distinguish him from the \xe2\x80\x99erd. It\xe2\x80\x99s important for\r\n\xe2\x80\x99is interests that he should be recognized easily. See a baby reaching\r\nout of a bath and you immediately think of Pears\xe2\x80\x99 Soap; see the white\r\n\xe2\x80\x99air waving out behind and think of Lloyd George. That\xe2\x80\x99s the secret. But\r\nin my opinion, Mr. Gumbril, the old system was much more sensible, give\r\nthem regular uniforms and badges, I say; make Cabinet Ministers wear\r\nfeathers in their \xe2\x80\x99air. Then the people will be looking to a real fixed\r\nsymbol of leadership, not to the peculiarities of the mere individuals.\r\nBeards and \xe2\x80\x99air and funny collars change; but a good uniform is always\r\nthe same. Give them feathers, that\xe2\x80\x99s what I say, Mr. Gumbril. Feathers\r\nwill increase the dignity of the State and lessen the importance of the\r\nindividual. And that,\xe2\x80\x9d concluded Mr. Bojanus with emphasis, \xe2\x80\x9cthat, Mr.\r\nGumbril, will be all to the good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to tell me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril, \xe2\x80\x9cthat if I chose to show\r\nmyself to the multitude in my inflated trousers, I could become a\r\nleader\xe2\x80\x94do you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, no,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Bojanus. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99ave to \xe2\x80\x99ave the talent for talking\r\nand ordering people about, to begin with. Feathers wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t give the\r\ngenius, but they\xe2\x80\x99d magnify the effect of what there was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGumbril got up and began to divest himself of the Small-Clothes. He\r\nunscrewed the valve and the air whistled out, dyingly. He too sighed.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCurious,\xe2\x80\x9d he said pensively, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I\xe2\x80\x99ve never felt the need for a\r\nleader. I\xe2\x80\x99ve never met any one I felt I could whole-heartedly admire or\r\nbelieve in, never any one I wanted to follow. It must be pleasant, I\r\nshould think, to hand oneself over to somebody else. It must give you a\r\nwarm, splendid, comfortable feeling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Bojanus smiled and shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cYou and I, Mr. Gumbril,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x99re not the sort of people to be impressed with feathers or\r\neven by talking and ordering about. We may not be leaders ourselves. But\r\nat any rate we aren\xe2\x80\x99t the \xe2\x80\x99erd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot the main herd, perhaps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot any \xe2\x80\x99erd,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Bojanus insisted proudly.\r\n\r\nGumbril shook his head dubiously and buttoned up his trousers. He was\r\nnot sure, now he came to think of it, that he didn\xe2\x80\x99t belong to all the\r\nherds\xe2\x80\x94by a sort of honorary membership and temporarily, as occasion\r\noffered, as one belongs to the Union at the sister university or to the\r\nNaval and Military Club while one\xe2\x80\x99s own is having its annual clean-out.\r\nShearwater\xe2\x80\x99s herd, Lypiatt\xe2\x80\x99s herd, Mr. Mercaptan\xe2\x80\x99s herd, Mrs. Viveash\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nherd, the architectural herd of his father, the educational herd (but\r\nthat, thank God! was now bleating on distant pastures), the herd of Mr.\r\nBojanus\xe2\x80\x94he belonged to them all a little, to none of them completely.\r\nNobody belonged to his herd. How could they? No chameleon can live with\r\ncomfort on a tartan. He put on his coat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll send the garments this evening,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Bojanus. Gumbril left\r\nthe shop. At the theatrical wig-maker\xe2\x80\x99s in Leicester Square he ordered a\r\nblond fan-shaped beard to match his own hair and moustache. He would, at\r\nany rate, be his own leader; he would wear a badge, a symbol of\r\nauthority. And Coleman had said that there were dangerous relations to\r\nbe entered into by the symbol\xe2\x80\x99s aid.\r\n\r\nAh, now he was provisionally a member of Coleman\xe2\x80\x99s herd. It was all very\r\ndepressing.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                               CHAPTER IX\r\n\r\n\r\nFan-shaped, blond, mounted on gauze and guaranteed undetectable, it\r\narrived from the wig-maker, preciously packed in a stout cardboard box\r\nsix times too large for it and accompanied by a quarter of a pint of the\r\nchoicest spirit gum. In the privacy of his bedroom Gumbril uncoffined\r\nit, held it out for his own admiration, caressed its silkiness and\r\nfinally tried it on, holding it provisionally to his chin, in front of\r\nthe looking-glass. The effect, he decided immediately, was stunning, was\r\ngrandiose. From melancholy and all too mild he saw himself transformed\r\non the instant into a sort of jovial Henry the Eighth, into a massive\r\nRabelaisian man, broad and powerful and exuberant with vitality and\r\nhair.\r\n\r\nThe proportions of his face were startlingly altered. The podium, below\r\nthe mouth, had been insufficiently massive to carry the stately order of\r\nthe nose; and the ratiocinative attic of the forehead, noble enough, no\r\ndoubt, in itself, had been disproportionately high. The beard now\r\nsupplied the deficiencies in the stylobate, and planted now on a firm\r\nbasement of will, the order of the senses, the aerial attic of ideas,\r\nreared themselves with a more classical harmoniousness of proportion. It\r\nonly remained for him to order from Mr. Bojanus an American coat, padded\r\nout at the shoulders as squarely and heroically as a doublet of the\r\nCinquecento, and he would look the complete Rabelaisian man. Great\r\neater, deep drinker, stout fighter, prodigious lover; clear thinker,\r\ncreator of beauty, seeker of truth and prophet of heroic grandeurs.\r\nFitted out with coat and beard, he could qualify for the next vacancy\r\namong the c\xc5\x93nobites of Thelema.\r\n\r\nHe removed his beard\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cput his beaver up,\xe2\x80\x9d as they used to say in the\r\nfine old days of chivalry; he would have to remember that little joke\r\nfor Coleman\xe2\x80\x99s benefit. He put his beaver up\xe2\x80\x94ha, ha!\xe2\x80\x94and stared ruefully\r\nat the far from Rabelaisian figure which now confronted him. The\r\nmoustache\xe2\x80\x94that was genuine enough\xe2\x80\x94which had looked, in conjunction with\r\nthe splendid work of art below, so fierce and manly, served by itself,\r\nhe now perceived, only droopily to emphasize his native mildness and\r\nmelancholy.\r\n\r\nIt was a dismal affair, which might have belonged to Maurice Barr\xc3\xa8s in\r\nyouth; a slanting, flagging, sagging thing, such as could only grow on\r\nthe lip of an assiduous Cultivator of the Me, and would become, as one\r\ngrew older, ludicrously out of place on the visage of a roaring\r\nNationalist. If it weren\xe2\x80\x99t that it fitted in so splendidly with the\r\nbeard, if it weren\xe2\x80\x99t that it became so marvellously different in the new\r\ncontext he had now discovered for it, he would have shaved it off then\r\nand there.\r\n\r\nMournful appendage. But now he would transform it, he would add to it\r\nits better half. Zadig\xe2\x80\x99s quatrain to his mistress, when the tablet on\r\nwhich it was written was broken in two, became a treasonable libel on\r\nthe king. So this moustache, thought Gumbril, as gingerly he applied the\r\nspirit gum to his cheeks and chin, this moustache which by itself serves\r\nonly to betray me, becomes, as soon as it is joined to its missing\r\ncontext, an amorous arm for the conquest of the fair sex.\r\n\r\nA little far-fetched, he decided; a little ponderous. And besides, as so\r\nfew people had read Zadig, not much use in conversation. Cautiously and\r\nwith neat, meticulous finger-tips he adjusted the transformation to his\r\ngummed face, pressed it firmly, held it while it stuck fast. The portals\r\nof Thelema opened before him; he was free of those rich orchards, those\r\nhalls and courts, those broad staircases winding in noble spirals within\r\nthe flanks of each of the fair round towers. And it was Coleman who had\r\npointed out the way; he felt duly grateful. One last look at the\r\nComplete Man, one final and definitive constatation that the Mild and\r\nMelancholy one was, for the time at least, no more; and he was ready in\r\nall confidence to set out. He selected a loose, light great-coat\xe2\x80\x94not\r\nthat he needed a coat at all, for the day was bright and warm; but until\r\nMr. Bojanus had done his labour of padding he would have to broaden\r\nhimself out in this way, even if it did mean that he might be\r\nuncomfortably hot. To fall short of Complete Manhood for fear of a\r\nlittle inconvenience would be absurd. He slipped, therefore, into his\r\nlight coat\xe2\x80\x94a toga, Mr. Bojanus called it, a very neat toga in real West\r\nCountry whipcord. He put on his broadest and blackest felt hat, for\r\nbreadth above everything was what he needed to give him completeness,\r\nbreadth of stature, breadth of mind, breadth of human sympathy, breadth\r\nof smile, breadth of humour, breadth of everything. The final touch was\r\na massive and antique Malacca cane belonging to his father. If he had\r\npossessed a bulldog, he would have taken it out on a leash. But he did\r\nnot. He issued into the sunshine, unaccompanied.\r\n\r\nBut unaccompanied he did not mean to remain for long. These warm, bright\r\nMay days were wonderful days for being in love on. And to be alone on\r\nsuch days was like a malady. It was a malady from which the Mild and\r\nMelancholy Man suffered all too frequently. And yet there were millions\r\nof superfluous women in the country; millions of them. Every day, in the\r\nstreets, one saw thousands of them passing; and some were exquisite,\r\nwere ravishing, the only possible soul-mates. Thousands of unique\r\nsoul-mates every day. The Mild and Melancholy one allowed them to\r\npass\xe2\x80\x94for ever. But to-day\xe2\x80\x94to-day he was the complete and Rabelaisian\r\nman; he was bearded to the teeth; the imbecile game was at its height;\r\nthere would be opportunities, and the Complete Man could know how to\r\ntake them. No, he would not be unaccompanied for long.\r\n\r\nOutside in the square the fourteen plane trees glowed in their young,\r\nunsullied green. At the end of every street the golden muslin of the\r\nhaze hung in an unwrinkled curtain that thinned away above the sky\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngauzy horizon to transparent nothing against the intenser blue. The dim,\r\nconch-like murmur that in a city is silence seemed hazily to identify\r\nitself with the golden mistiness of summer, and against this dim, wide\r\nbackground the yells of the playing children detached themselves,\r\ndistinct and piercing. \xe2\x80\x9cBeaver\xe2\x80\x9d they shouted, \xe2\x80\x9cbeaver!\xe2\x80\x9d and, \xe2\x80\x9cIs it cold\r\nup there?\xe2\x80\x9d Full of playful menace, the Complete Man shook at them his\r\nborrowed Malacca. He accepted their prompt hail as the most favourable\r\nof omens.\r\n\r\nAt the first tobacconist\xe2\x80\x99s Gumbril bought the longest cigar he could\r\nfind, and trailing behind him expiring blue wreaths of Cuban smoke, he\r\nmade his way slowly and with an ample swagger towards the Park. It was\r\nthere, under the elms, on the shores of the ornamental waters, that he\r\nexpected to find his opportunity, that he intended\xe2\x80\x94how confidently\r\nbehind his Gargantuan mask!\xe2\x80\x94to take it.\r\n\r\nThe opportunity offered itself sooner than he expected.\r\n\r\nHe had just turned into the Queen\xe2\x80\x99s Road and was sauntering past\r\nWhiteley\xe2\x80\x99s with the air of one who knows that he has a right to a good\r\nplace, to two or three good places even, in the sun, when he noticed\r\njust in front of him, peering intently at the New Season\xe2\x80\x99s Models, a\r\nyoung woman whom in his mild and melancholy days he would have only\r\nhopelessly admired, but who now, to the Complete Man, seemed a destined\r\nand accessible prey. She was fairly tall, but seemed taller than she\r\nactually was, by reason of her remarkable slenderness. Not that she\r\nlooked disagreeably thin, far from it. It was a rounded slenderness. The\r\nComplete Man decided to consider her as tubular\xe2\x80\x94flexible and tubular,\r\nlike a section of boa constrictor, should one say. She was dressed in\r\nclothes that emphasized this serpentine slimness, in a close-fitting\r\ngrey jacket that buttoned up to the neck and a long, narrow grey skirt\r\nthat came down to her ankles. On her head was a small, sleek black hat,\r\nthat looked almost as though it were made of metal. It was trimmed on\r\none side with a bunch of dull golden foliage.\r\n\r\nThose golden leaves were the only touch of ornament in all the severe\r\nsmoothness and unbroken tubularity of her person. As for her face, that\r\nwas neither strictly beautiful nor strictly ugly, but combined elements\r\nof both beauty and ugliness into a whole that was unexpected, that was\r\noddly and somehow unnaturally attractive.\r\n\r\nPretending, he too, to take an interest in the New Season\xe2\x80\x99s Models,\r\nGumbril made, squinting sideways over the burning tip of his cigar, an\r\ninventory of her features. The forehead, that was mostly hidden by her\r\nhat; it might be pensively and serenely high, it might be of that degree\r\nof lowness which in men is villainous, but in women is only another\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nrather rustic one perhaps, rather _canaille_ even, but definitely\r\nanother\xe2\x80\x94attraction. There was no telling. As for her eyes, they were\r\ngreen, and limpid; set wide apart in her head they looked out from under\r\nheavy lids and through openings that slanted up towards the outer\r\ncorners. Her nose was slightly aquiline. Her mouth was full-lipped, but\r\nstraight and unexpectedly wide. Her chin was small, round and firm. She\r\nhad a pale skin, a little flushed over the cheek-bones, which were\r\nprominent.\r\n\r\nOn the left cheek, close under the corner of the slanting eye, she had a\r\nbrown mole. Such hair as Gumbril could see beneath her hat was pale and\r\ninconspicuously blond. When she had finished looking at the New Season\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nModels she moved slowly on, halting for a moment before the travelling\r\ntrunks and the fitted picnic baskets; dwelling for a full minute over\r\nthe corsets, passing the hats, for some reason, rather contemptuously,\r\nbut pausing, which seemed strange, for a long pensive look at the cigars\r\nand wine. As for the tennis rackets and cricket bats, the school outfits\r\nand the gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s hosiery\xe2\x80\x94she hadn\xe2\x80\x99t so much as a look for one of\r\nthem. But how lovingly she lingered before the boots and shoes! Her own\r\nfeet, the Complete Man noticed with satisfaction, had an elegance of\r\nflorid curves. And while other folk walked on neat\xe2\x80\x99s leather she was\r\ncontent to be shod with nothing coarser than mottled serpent\xe2\x80\x99s skin.\r\n\r\nSlowly they drifted up Queen\xe2\x80\x99s Road, lingering before every jeweller\xe2\x80\x99s,\r\nevery antiquarian\xe2\x80\x99s, every milliner\xe2\x80\x99s on the way. The stranger gave him\r\nno opportunity, and indeed, Gumbril reflected, how should she? For the\r\nimbecile game on which he was relying is a travelling piquet for two\r\nplayers, not a game of patience. No sane human being could play it in\r\nsolitude. He would have to make the opportunity himself.\r\n\r\nAll that was mild in him, all that was melancholy, shrank with a\r\nsickened reluctance from the task of breaking\xe2\x80\x94with what consequences\r\ndelicious and perilous in the future or, in the case of the deserved\r\nsnub, immediately humiliating?\xe2\x80\x94a silence which, by the tenth or twelfth\r\nshop window, had become quite unbearably significant. The Mild and\r\nMelancholy one would have drifted to the top of the road, sharing, with\r\nthat community of tastes which is the basis of every happy union, her\r\nenthusiasm for brass candlesticks and toasting-forks, imitation\r\nChippendale furniture, gold watch-bracelets and low-waisted summer\r\nfrocks; would have drifted to the top of the road and watched her,\r\ndumbly, disappearing for ever into the green Park or along the blank\r\npavements of the Bayswater Road; would have watched her for ever\r\ndisappear and then, if the pubs had happened to be open, would have gone\r\nand ordered a glass of port, and sitting at the bar would have savoured,\r\nstill dumbly, among the other drinkers, the muddy grapes of the Douro,\r\nand his own unique loneliness.\r\n\r\nThat was what the Mild and Melancholy one would have done. But the\r\nsight, as he gazed earnestly into an antiquary\xe2\x80\x99s window, of his own\r\npowerful bearded face reflected in a sham Heppelwhite mirror, reminded\r\nhim that the Mild and Melancholy one was temporarily extinct, and that\r\nit was the Complete Man who now dawdled, smoking his long cigar, up the\r\nQueen\xe2\x80\x99s Road towards the Abbey of Thelema.\r\n\r\nHe squared his shoulders; in that loose toga of Mr. Bojanus\xe2\x80\x99s he looked\r\nas copious as Fran\xc3\xa7ois Premier. The time, he decided, had come.\r\n\r\nIt was at this moment that the reflection of the stranger\xe2\x80\x99s face joined\r\nitself in the little mirror, as she made a little movement away from the\r\nOld Welsh dresser in the corner, to that of his own. She looked at the\r\nspurious Heppelwhite. Their eyes met in the hospitable glass. Gumbril\r\nsmiled. The corners of the stranger\xe2\x80\x99s wide mouth seemed faintly to move;\r\nlike petals of the magnolia, her eyelids came slowly down over her\r\nslanting eyes. Gumbril turned from the reflection to the reality.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you want to say Beaver,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou may.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Complete Man had made his first speech.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to say nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said the stranger. She spoke with a charming\r\nprecision and distinctness, lingering with a pretty emphasis on the _n_\r\nof nothing. \xe2\x80\x9cN\xe2\x80\x94n\xe2\x80\x94nothing\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94it sounded rather final. She turned away, she\r\nmoved on.\r\n\r\nBut the Complete Man was not one to be put off by a mere ultimatum.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, falling into step with her, \xe2\x80\x9cnow I\xe2\x80\x99ve had it\xe2\x80\x94the\r\ndeserved snub. Honour is saved, prestige duly upheld. Now we can get on\r\nwith our conversation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Mild and Melancholy one stood by, gasping with astonished\r\nadmiration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are v\xe2\x80\x94very impertinent,\xe2\x80\x9d said the stranger, smiling and looking up\r\nfrom under the magnolia petals.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is in my character,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Complete Man. \xe2\x80\x9cYou mustn\xe2\x80\x99t blame me.\r\nOne cannot escape from one\xe2\x80\x99s heredity; that\xe2\x80\x99s one\xe2\x80\x99s share of original\r\nsin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is always grace,\xe2\x80\x9d said the stranger.\r\n\r\nGumbril caressed his beard. \xe2\x80\x9cTrue,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI advise you to pr\xe2\x80\x94ray for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis prayer, the Mild and Melancholy one reflected, had already been\r\nanswered. The original sin in him had been self-corrected.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere is another antique shop,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril. \xe2\x80\x9cShall we stop and have a\r\nlook at it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe stranger glanced at him doubtfully. But he looked quite serious.\r\nThey stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow revolting this sham cottage furniture is,\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril remarked. The\r\nshop, he noticed, was called \xe2\x80\x98Ye Olde Farme House.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe stranger, who had been on the point of saying how much she liked\r\nthose lovely Old Welsh dressers, gave him her heartiest agreement. \xe2\x80\x9cSo\r\nv\xe2\x80\x94vulgar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo horribly refined. So refined and artistic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe laughed on a descending chromatic scale. This was excitingly new.\r\nPoor Aunt Aggie with her Arts and Crafts, and her old English furniture.\r\nAnd to think she had taken them so seriously! She saw in a flash the\r\nfastidious lady that she now was\xe2\x80\x94with Louis whatever-it-was furniture at\r\nhome, and jewels, and young poets to tea, and real artists. In the past,\r\nwhen she had imagined herself entertaining real artists, it had always\r\nbeen among really artistic furniture. Aunt Aggie\xe2\x80\x99s furniture. But\r\nnow\xe2\x80\x94no, oh no. This man was probably an artist. His beard; and that big\r\nblack hat. But not poor; very well dressed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s funny to think that there are people who call that sort of\r\nthing artistic. One\xe2\x80\x99s quite s\xe2\x80\x94sorry for them,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, with a little\r\nhiss.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have a kind heart,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad to see that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot v\xe2\x80\x94very kind, I\xe2\x80\x99m af\xe2\x80\x94fraid.\xe2\x80\x9d She looked at him sideways, and\r\nsignificantly as the fastidious lady would have looked at one of the\r\npoets.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, kind enough, I hope,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Complete Man. He was delighted\r\nwith his new acquaintance.\r\n\r\nTogether they disembogued into the Bayswater Road. It was here, Gumbril\r\nreflected, that the Mild and Melancholy one would dumbly have slunk away\r\nto his glass of port and his loneliness among the alien topers at the\r\nbar. But the Complete Man took his new friend by the elbow, and steered\r\nher into the traffic. Together they crossed the road, together entered\r\nthe park.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI still think you are v\xe2\x80\x94very impertinent,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lady. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat induced\r\nyou to follow me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a single comprehensive gesture, Gumbril indicated the sun, the sky,\r\nthe green trees airily glittering, the grass, the emerald lights and\r\nviolet shadows of the rustic distance. \xe2\x80\x9cOn a day like this,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9chow could I help it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOriginal sin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d the Complete Man modestly shook his head, \xe2\x80\x9cI lay no claim to\r\noriginality in this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe stranger laughed. This was nearly as good as a young poet at the\r\ntea-table. She was very glad that she\xe2\x80\x99d decided, after all, to put on\r\nher best suit this afternoon, even if it was a little stuffy for the\r\nwarmth of the day. He, too, she noticed, was wearing a great-coat; which\r\nseemed rather odd.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it original,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cto go and tumble stupidly like an\r\nelephant into a pitfall, head over ears, at first sight...?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at him sideways, then closed down the magnolia petals, and\r\nsmiled. This was going to be the real thing\xe2\x80\x94one of those long, those\r\ninterminable, or, at any rate, indefinitely renewable conversations\r\nabout love; witty, subtle, penetrating and bold, like the conversations\r\nin books, like the conversations across the tea-table between brilliant\r\nyoung poets and ladies of quality, grown fastidious through an excessive\r\nexperience, fastidious and a little weary, but still, in their subtle\r\nway, insatiably curious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuppose we sit down,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Gumbril, and he pointed to a couple of\r\ngreen iron chairs, standing isolated in the middle of the grass close\r\ntogether and with their fronts slanting inwards a little towards one\r\nanother in a position that suggested a confidential intimacy. At the\r\nprospect of the conversation that, inevitably, was about to unroll\r\nitself, he felt decidedly less elated than did his new friend. If there\r\nwas anything he disliked it was conversations about love. It bored him,\r\noh, it bored him most horribly, this minute analysis of the passion that\r\nyoung women always seemed to expect one, at some point or other in one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nrelation with them, to make. How love alters the character for both good\r\nand bad; how physical passion need not be incompatible with the\r\nspiritual; how a hateful and tyrannous possessiveness can be allied in\r\nlove with the most unselfish solicitude for the other party\xe2\x80\x94oh, he knew\r\nall this and much more, so well, so well. And whether one can be in love\r\nwith more than one person at a time, whether love can exist without\r\njealousy, whether pity, affection, desire can in any way replace the\r\nfull and genuine passion\xe2\x80\x94how often he had had to thrash out these dreary\r\nquestions!\r\n\r\nAnd all the philosophic speculations were equally familiar, all the\r\nphysiological and anthropological and psychological facts. In the theory\r\nof the subject he had ceased to take any interest. Unhappily, a\r\ndiscussion of the theory always seemed to be an essential preliminary to\r\nthe practice of it. He sighed a little wearily as he took his seat on\r\nthe green iron chair. But then, recollecting that he was now the\r\nComplete Man, and that the Complete Man must do everything with a\r\nflourish and a high hand, he leaned forward and, smiling with a charming\r\ninsolence through his beard, began:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTiresias, you may remember, was granted the singular privilege of\r\nliving both as a man and a woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAh, this was the genuine young poet. Supporting an elbow on the back of\r\nher chair and leaning her cheek against her hand, she disposed herself\r\nto listen and, where necessary, brilliantly to interpellate; it was\r\nthrough half-closed eyes that she looked at him, and she smiled faintly\r\nin a manner which she knew, from experience, to be enigmatic, and though\r\na shade haughty, though a tiny bit mocking and ironical, exceedingly\r\nattractive.\r\n\r\nAn hour and a half later they were driving towards an address in Bloxam\r\nGardens, Maida Vale. The name seemed vaguely familiar to Gumbril. Bloxam\r\nGardens\xe2\x80\x94perhaps one of his aunts had lived there once?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a dr\xe2\x80\x94dreadful little maisonnette,\xe2\x80\x9d she explained. \xe2\x80\x9cFull of awful\r\nthings. We had to take it furnished. It\xe2\x80\x99s so impossible to find anything\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGumbril leaned back in his corner, wondering, as he studied that averted\r\nprofile, who or what this young woman could be. She seemed to be in the\r\nobvious movement, to like the sort of things one would expect people to\r\nlike; she seemed to be as highly civilized, in Mr. Mercaptan\xe2\x80\x99s rather\r\ntechnical sense of the term, as free of all prejudices as the great\r\nexponent of civilization himself.\r\n\r\nShe seemed, from her coolly dropped hints, to possess all the dangerous\r\nexperience, all the assurance and easy ruthlessness of a great lady\r\nwhose whole life is occupied in the interminable affairs of the heart,\r\nthe senses and the head. But, by a strange contradiction she seemed to\r\nfind her life narrow and uninteresting. She had complained in so many\r\nwords that her husband misunderstood and neglected her, had complained,\r\nby implication, that she knew very few interesting people.\r\n\r\nThe maisonnette in Bloxam Gardens was certainly not very splendid\xe2\x80\x94six\r\nrooms on the second and third floors of a peeling stucco house. And the\r\nfurniture\xe2\x80\x94decidedly Hire Purchase. And the curtains and\r\ncretonnes\xe2\x80\x94brightly \xe2\x80\x98modern,\xe2\x80\x99 positively \xe2\x80\x98futurist.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat one has to put up with in furnished flats!\xe2\x80\x9d The lady made a\r\ngrimace as she ushered him into the sitting-room. And while she spoke\r\nthe words, she really managed to persuade herself that the furniture\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t theirs, that they had found all this sordid stuff cluttering up\r\nthe rooms, not chosen it, oh with pains! themselves, not doggedly paid\r\nfor it, month by month.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur own things,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured vaguely, \xe2\x80\x9care stored. In the Riviera.\xe2\x80\x9d It\r\nwas there, under the palms, among the gaudy melon flowers and the\r\ncroupiers that the fastidious lady had last held her salon of young\r\npoets. In the Riviera\xe2\x80\x94that would explain, now she came to think of it, a\r\nlot of things, if explanation ever became necessary.\r\n\r\nThe Complete Man nodded sympathetically. \xe2\x80\x9cOther people\xe2\x80\x99s tastes,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nheld up his hands, they both laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cBut why do we think of other\r\npeople?\xe2\x80\x9d he added. And coming forward with a conquering impulsiveness he\r\ntook both her long, fine hands in his and raised them to his bearded\r\nmouth.\r\n\r\nShe looked at him for a second, then dropped her eyelids, took back her\r\nhands. \xe2\x80\x9cI must go and make the tea,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe servants\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the plural\r\nwas a pardonable exaggeration\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9care out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGallantly, the Complete Man offered to come and help her. These scenes\r\nof intimate life had a charm all their own. But she would not allow it.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no,\xe2\x80\x9d she was very firm, \xe2\x80\x9cI simply forbid you. You must stay here. I\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t be a moment,\xe2\x80\x9d and she was gone, closing the door carefully behind\r\nher.\r\n\r\nLeft to himself, Gumbril sat down and filed his nails.\r\n\r\nAs for the young lady, she hurried along to her dingy little kitchen,\r\nlit the gas, put the kettle on, set out the teapot and the cups on a\r\ntray, and from the biscuit-box, where it was stored, took out the\r\nremains of a chocolate cake, which had already seen service at the\r\nday-before-yesterday\xe2\x80\x99s tea-party. When all was ready here, she tiptoed\r\nacross to her bedroom and sitting down at her dressing-table, began with\r\nhands that trembled a little with excitement to powder her nose, and\r\nheighten the colour of her cheeks. Even after the last touch had been\r\ngiven, she still sat there, looking at her image in the glass.\r\n\r\nThe lady and the poet, she was thinking, the _grande dame_ and the\r\nbrilliant young man of genius. She liked young men with beards. But he\r\nwas not an artist, in spite of the beard, in spite of the hat. He was a\r\nwriter of sorts. So she gathered; but he was reticent, he was\r\ndelightfully mysterious. She too, for that matter. The great lady slips\r\nout, masked, into the street; touches the young man\xe2\x80\x99s sleeve: Come with\r\nme. She chooses, does not let herself passively be chosen. The young\r\npoet falls at her feet; she lifts him up. One is accustomed to this sort\r\nof thing.\r\n\r\nShe opened her jewel box, took out all her rings\xe2\x80\x94there were not many of\r\nthem, alas!\xe2\x80\x94and put them on. Two or three of them, on second thoughts,\r\nshe took off again; they were a little, she suspected with a sudden\r\nqualm, in other people\xe2\x80\x99s taste.\r\n\r\nHe was very clever, very artistic\xe2\x80\x94only that seemed to be the wrong word\r\nto use; he seemed to know all the new things, all the interesting\r\npeople. Perhaps he would introduce her to some of them. And he was so\r\nmuch at ease behind his knowledge, so well assured. But for her part,\r\nshe felt pretty certain, she had made no stupid mistakes. She too had\r\nbeen, had looked at any rate\xe2\x80\x94which was the important thing\xe2\x80\x94very much at\r\nease.\r\n\r\nShe liked young men with beards. They looked so Russian. Catherine of\r\nRussia had been one of the great ladies with caprices. Masked in the\r\nstreets. Young poet, come with me. Or even, Young butcher\xe2\x80\x99s boy. But\r\nthat, no, that was going too far, too low. Still, life, life\xe2\x80\x94it was\r\nthere to be lived\xe2\x80\x94life\xe2\x80\x94to be enjoyed. And now, and now? She was still\r\nwondering what would happen next, when the kettle, which was one of\r\nthose funny ones which whistle when they come to the boil, began,\r\nfitfully, at first, then, under full steam, unflaggingly, to sound its\r\nmournful, other-worldly note. She sighed and bestirred herself to attend\r\nto it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me help you.\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril jumped up as she came into the room. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\r\ncan I do?\xe2\x80\x9d He hovered rather ineptly round her.\r\n\r\nThe lady put down her tray on the little table. \xe2\x80\x9cN\xe2\x80\x94nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cN\xe2\x80\x94nothing?\xe2\x80\x9d he imitated her with a playful mockery. \xe2\x80\x9cAm I good for\r\nn\xe2\x80\x94nothing at all?\xe2\x80\x9d He took one of her hands and kissed it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing that\xe2\x80\x99s of the l\xe2\x80\x94least importance.\xe2\x80\x9d She sat down and began to\r\npour out the tea.\r\n\r\nThe Complete Man also sat down. \xe2\x80\x9cSo to adore at first sight,\xe2\x80\x9d he asked,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cis not of the l\xe2\x80\x94least importance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe shook her head, smiled, raised and lowered her eyelids. One was so\r\nwell accustomed to this sort of thing; it had no importance. \xe2\x80\x9cSugar?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe asked. The young poet was safely there, sparkling across the\r\ntea-table. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you? I\r\nthink it\xe2\x80\x99s one\xe2\x80\x99s first duty.\xe2\x80\x9d She became quite grave. \xe2\x80\x9cOne ought to\r\nenjoy every moment of it,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, passionately, adventurously,\r\nnewly, excitingly, uniquely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Complete Man laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cA conscientious hedonist. I see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe felt uncomfortably that the fastidious lady had not quite lived up\r\nto her character. She had spoken more like a young woman who finds life\r\ntoo dull and daily, and would like to get on to the cinema. \xe2\x80\x9cI am very\r\nconscientious,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, making significant play with the magnolia\r\npetals and smiling her riddling smile. She must retrieve the Great\r\nCatherine\xe2\x80\x99s reputation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could see that from the first,\xe2\x80\x9d mocked the Complete Man with a\r\ntriumphant insolence. \xe2\x80\x9cConscience doth make cowards of us all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe fastidious lady only contemptuously smiled. \xe2\x80\x9cHave a little chocolate\r\ncake,\xe2\x80\x9d she suggested. Her heart was beating. She wondered, she wondered.\r\n\r\nThere was a long silence. Gumbril finished his chocolate cake, gloomily\r\ndrank his tea and did not speak. He found, all at once, that he had\r\nnothing to say. His jovial confidence seemed, for the moment, to have\r\ndeserted him. He was only the Mild and Melancholy one foolishly\r\ndisguised as a Complete Man; a sheep in beaver\xe2\x80\x99s clothing. Perhaps he was thinking\r\nof her\xe2\x80\x94suspecting her, seeing through the fastidious lady and feeling\r\nangry at her attempted deception. Or\r\nperhaps he was just made like that\xe2\x80\x94a moody young poet; that seemed, on\r\nthe whole, the most likely explanation; it was also the most pleasing\r\nand romantic. She waited. He must do something, he told himself; he must recover\r\nthe Complete Man\xe2\x80\x99s lost _morale_. Desperately he came to a halt in\r\nfront of the one decent picture hanging on the walls. It was an\r\neighteenth-century engraving of Raphael\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98Transfiguration\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94better, he\r\nalways thought, in black and white than in its bleakly-coloured\r\noriginal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a nice engraving,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cVery nice.\xe2\x80\x9d The mere fact of having\r\nuttered at all was a great comfort to him, a real relief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cThat belongs to me. It has\r\nmade it possible to reproduce pictures so easily and cheaply, that all\r\nthe bad artists who were well occupied in the past, making engravings of\r\ngood men\xe2\x80\x99s paintings, are now free to do bad original work of their\r\nown.\xe2\x80\x9d All this was terribly impersonal, he told himself, terribly off\r\nthe point. But what?\r\n\r\nShe came to his rescue. \xe2\x80\x9cI bought another at the same time,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98The Last Communion of St. Jerome,\xe2\x80\x99 by\xe2\x80\x94who is it? But if you don\xe2\x80\x99t mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe bowed. \xe2\x80\x9cIf _you_ don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe smiled graciously to him and got up. \xe2\x80\x9cThis way,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and\r\nopened the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a lovely picture,\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril went on, loquaciously now, behind her,\r\nas they walked down the dark corridor. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd besides, I have a\r\nsentimental attachment to it. There used to be a copy of an engraving of\r\nit at home, when I was a child. And I remember wondering and\r\nwondering\xe2\x80\x94oh, it went on for years\xe2\x80\x94every time I saw the picture;\r\nwondering why on earth that old bishop (for I did know it was a bishop)\r\nshould be handing the naked old man a five-shilling piece.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe opened a door; they were in her very pink room. Grave in its solemn\r\nand subtly harmonious beauty, the picture hung over the mantelpiece,\r\nhung there, among the photographs of the little friends of her own age,\r\nlike some strange object from another world. From within that chipped\r\ngilt frame all the beauty, all the grandeur of religion looked darkly\r\nout upon the pink room. The little friends of her own age, all\r\ndeliciously nubile, sweetly smiled, turned up their eyes, clasped\r\nPersian cats or stood jauntily, feet apart, hand in the breeches pocket\r\nof the land-girl\xe2\x80\x99s uniform; the pink roses on the wallpaper, the pink\r\nand white curtains, the pink bed, the strawberry-coloured carpet, filled\r\nall the air with the rosy reflections of nakedness and life.\r\n\r\nAnd utterly remote, absorbed in their grave, solemn ecstasy, the robed\r\nand mitred priest held out, the dying saint yearningly received, the\r\nbody of the Son of God. A bishop giving a naked old\r\nman five shillings in a church, with angels fluttering overhead, and a\r\nlion sleeping in the foreground. It was obscure, it was horribly\r\nobscure.\xe2\x80\x9d He turned away from the picture and confronted his hostess,\r\nwho was standing a little way behind him smiling enigmatically and\r\ninvitingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cObscure,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cBut so is everything. It was; he had heard Shearwater\r\ntalk about it.\r\n\r\nDid she make a habit of this sort of thing!\r\n\r\nCould Shearwater be wholly unaware of what she was really like? I take my rabbit,\xe2\x80\x9d the friend was saying\xe2\x80\x94he was a young man with\r\ndark, protruding eyes, and staring, doggy nostrils; very eager, lively\r\nand loud. \xe2\x80\x9cI take my rabbit and I inject into it the solution of eyes,\r\npulped eyes of another dead rabbit. There are many ways, he reflected, of spending an\r\nafternoon.\r\n\r\nThe doggy young man refused to come in, he had to get in his game of\r\ntennis before dinner. Shearwater climbed the stairs alone. He was taking\r\noff his hat in the little hall of his own apartment, when Rosie came out\r\nof the sitting-room with a trayful of tea-things.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, kissing her affectionately on the forehead. \xe2\x80\x9cWell?\r\nPeople to tea?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly one,\xe2\x80\x9d Rosie replied. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go and make you a fresh cup.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe glided off, rustling in her pink kimono towards the kitchen.\r\n\r\nShearwater sat down in the sitting-room. He had brought home with him\r\nfrom the library the fifteenth volume of the _Biochemical Journal_.\r\nThere was something in it he wanted to look up. He turned over the\r\npages. Ah, here it was. He began reading. Rosie came back again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s your tea,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nHe thanked her without looking up. The tea grew cold on the little table\r\nat his side.\r\n\r\nLying on the sofa, Rosie pondered and remembered. Had the events of the\r\nafternoon, she asked herself, really happened? They seemed very\r\nimprobable and remote, now, in this studious silence. She couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help\r\nfeeling a little disappointed. Was it only this? So simple and obvious?\r\nShe tried to work herself up into a more exalted mood. She even tried to\r\nfeel guilty; but there she failed completely. She tried to feel\r\nrapturous; but without much more success. Still, he certainly had been a\r\nmost extraordinary man. Such impudence, and at the same time such\r\ndelicacy and tact.\r\n\r\nIt was a pity she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t afford to change the furniture. She saw now\r\nthat it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do at all. She would go and tell Aunt Aggie about the\r\ndreadful middle-classness of her Art and Craftiness.\r\n\r\nShe ought to have an Empire _chaise longue_. Like Madame R\xc3\xa9camier.
