(_She goes out._)\r\n\r\n    THE MONSTER (_solus_): Somewhere there must be love like music.\r\n    Love harmonious and ordered: two spirits, two bodies moving\r\n    contrapuntally together. Somewhere, the stupid brutish act must be\r\n    made to make sense, must be enriched, must be made significant.\r\n    Lust, like Diabelli\xe2\x80\x99s waltz, a stupid air, turned by a genius into\r\n    three-and-thirty fabulous variations. Somewhere....\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh dear!\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Mrs. Viveash.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCharming!\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril protested.\r\n\r\n  ... love like sheets of silky flame; like landscapes brilliant in\r\n  the sunlight against a background of purple thunder; like the\r\n  solution of a cosmic problem; like faith....\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCrikey!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash.\r\n\r\n  ... Somewhere, somewhere. But in my veins creep the maggots of the\r\n  pox....\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, really!\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Viveash shook her head. \xe2\x80\x9cToo medical!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n  ... crawling towards the brain, crawling into the mouth, burrowing\r\n  into the bones. Insatiably.\r\n\r\n  The Monster threw himself to the ground, and the curtain came down.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd about time too!\xe2\x80\x9d declared Mrs. Viveash.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCharming!\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril stuck to his guns. \xe2\x80\x9cCharming! charming!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a disturbance near the door. Mrs. Viveash looked round to see\r\nwhat was happening. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd now on top of it all,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9chere comes\r\nColeman, raving, with an unknown drunk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave we missed it?\xe2\x80\x9d Coleman was shouting. \xe2\x80\x9cHave we missed all the\r\nlovely bloody farce?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLovely bloody!\xe2\x80\x9d his companion repeated with drunken raptures, and he\r\nwent into fits of uncontrollable laughter. We\xe2\x80\x99re making a round\r\ntour\xe2\x80\x94or, rather, a descending spiral tour of hell. But we\xe2\x80\x99re only at the\r\nfirst circle so far. Milton called them that; he\r\n  should have known. Somewhere there must be men, however. The\r\n  variations on Diabelli prove it. Brunelleschi\xe2\x80\x99s dome is more than\r\n  the magnification of Cl\xc3\xa9o de M\xc3\xa9rode\xe2\x80\x99s breast. Somewhere there are\r\n  men with power, living reasonably. Like our mythical Greeks and\r\n  Romans. Living cleanly. The images of the gods are their portraits.\r\n  They walk under their own protection. (_The_ MONSTER _climbs on to a\r\n  chair and stands in the posture of a statue_.) Jupiter, father of\r\n  gods, a man, I bless myself, I throw bolts at my own disobedience, I\r\n  answer my own prayers, I pronounce oracles to satisfy the questions\r\n  I myself propound. I abolish all tetters, poxes, blood-spitting,\r\n  rotting of bones. With love I recreate the world from within. Europa\r\n  puts an end to squalor, Leda does away with tyranny, Danae tempers\r\n  stupidity. After establishing these reforms in the social sewer, I\r\n  climb, I climb, up through the manhole, out of the manhole, beyond\r\n  humanity. For the manhole, even the manhole, is dark; though not so\r\n  dingy as the doghole it was before I altered it. Up through the\r\n  manhole, towards the air. Up, up! (_And the_ MONSTER, _suiting the\r\n  action to his words, climbs up the runged back of his chair and\r\n  stands, by a miraculous feat of acrobacy, on the topmost bar_.) I\r\n  begin to see the stars through other eyes than my own. More than dog\r\n  already, I become more than man. I begin to have inklings of the\r\n  shape and sense of things. Upwards, upwards I strain, I peer, I\r\n  reach aloft. (_The balanced_ MONSTER _reaches, strains and peers_.)\r\n  And I seize, I seize! (_As he shouts these words, the_ MONSTER\r\n  _falls heavily, head foremost, to the floor. He lies there quite\r\n  still. (_He\r\n  examines the_ MONSTER.)\r\n\r\n  THE WARDER: He was for ever climbing on to his chair.\r\n\r\n  THE DOCTOR: Well, he won\xe2\x80\x99t any more. His neck\xe2\x80\x99s broken.\r\n\r\n  THE WARDER: You don\xe2\x80\x99t say so?\r\n\r\n  THE DOCTOR: I do.\r\n\r\n  THE WARDER: Well, I never!\r\n\r\n  THE DOCTOR: Have it carried down to the dissecting-room.\r\n\r\n  THE WARDER: I\xe2\x80\x99ll send for the porters at once.\r\n\r\n  (_Exeunt severally, and_ CURTAIN.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad that\xe2\x80\x99s over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe music struck up again, saxophone and \xe2\x80\x99cello, with the thin draught\r\nof the violin to cool their ecstasies and the thumping piano to remind\r\nthem of business. Gumbril and Mrs. Viveash slid out into the dancing\r\ncrowd, revolving as though by force of habit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese substitutes for the genuine copulative article,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coleman to\r\nhis disciple, \xe2\x80\x9care beneath the dignity of hell-hounds like you and me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharmed, the young man laughed; he was attentive as though at the feet\r\nof Socrates. Coleman had found him in a night club, where he had gone in\r\nsearch of Zoe, found him very drunk in the company of two formidable\r\nwomen fifteen or twenty years his senior, who were looking after him,\r\nhalf maternally out of pure kindness of heart, half professionally; for\r\nhe seemed to be carrying a good deal of money. He was incapable of\r\nlooking after himself. Coleman had pounced on him at once, claimed an\r\nold friendship which the youth was too tipsy to be able to deny, and\r\ncarried him off. There was something, he always thought, peculiarly\r\ninteresting about the spectacle of children tobogganing down into the\r\ncesspools.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like this place,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTastes differ!\xe2\x80\x9d Coleman shrugged his shoulders. \xe2\x80\x9cThe German professors\r\nhave catalogued thousands of people whose whole pleasure consists in\r\neating dung.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man smiled and nodded, rather vaguely. \xe2\x80\x9cIs there anything to\r\ndrink here?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo respectable,\xe2\x80\x9d Coleman answered, shaking his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think this is a bloody place,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! but some people like blood. And some like boots. And some like long\r\ngloves and corsets. And some like birch-rods. And some like sliding down\r\nslopes and can\xe2\x80\x99t look at Michelangelo\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98Night\xe2\x80\x99 on the Medici Tombs\r\nwithout dying the little death, because the statue seems to be sliding.\r\nAnd some....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I want something to drink,\xe2\x80\x9d insisted the young man.\r\n\r\nColeman stamped his feet, waved his arms. \xe2\x80\x9c_\xc3\x80 boire! \xc3\xa0 boire!_\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nshouted, like the newborn Gargantua. Nobody paid any attention.\r\n\r\nThe music came to an end. Gumbril and Mrs. Viveash reappeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDante,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coleman, \xe2\x80\x9ccalls for drink. We must leave the building.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Anything to get out of this,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\ntime?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGumbril looked at his watch. \xe2\x80\x9cHalf-past one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash sighed. \xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t possibly go to bed,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cfor another\r\nhour at least.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey walked out into the street. The stars were large and brilliant\r\noverhead. There was a little wind that almost seemed to come from the\r\ncountry. Gumbril thought so, at any rate; he thought of the country.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe question is, where?\xe2\x80\x9d said Coleman. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can come to my bordello, if\r\nyou like; but it\xe2\x80\x99s a long way off and Zoe hates us all so much, she\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nprobably set on us with the meat-chopper. If she\xe2\x80\x99s back again, that is.\r\nThough she may be out all night. _Zoe mou, sas agapo._ Shall we risk\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo me it\xe2\x80\x99s quite indifferent,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash faintly, as though\r\nwholly preoccupied with expiring.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr there\xe2\x80\x99s my place,\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril said abruptly, as though shaking himself\r\nawake out of some dream.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you live still farther, don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d said Coleman. \xe2\x80\x9cWith venerable\r\nparents, and so forth. One foot in the grave and all that. Shall we\r\nmingle hornpipes with funerals?\xe2\x80\x9d He began to hum Chopin\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98Funeral\r\nMarch\xe2\x80\x99 at three times its proper speed, and seizing the young stranger\r\nin his arms, two-stepped two or three turns on the pavement, then\r\nreleased his hold and let him go reeling against the area railings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean the family mansion,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril. \xe2\x80\x9cI mean my own\r\nrooms. They\xe2\x80\x99re quite near. In Great Russell Street.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never knew you had any rooms, Theodore,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody did.\xe2\x80\x9d Why should they know now? Because the wind seemed almost a\r\ncountry wind? \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s drink there,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSplendid!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the young man. They were all splendid people.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s some gin,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCapital aphrodisiac!\xe2\x80\x9d Coleman commented.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome light white wine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDiuretic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd some whisky.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe great emetic,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coleman. \xe2\x80\x9cCome on.\xe2\x80\x9d And he struck up the March\r\nof the Fascisti. \xe2\x80\x9c_Giovinezza, giovinezza, primavera di bellezza_....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThe noise went fading down the dark, empty streets.\r\n\r\nThe gin, the white wine, and even, for the sake of the young stranger,\r\nwho wanted to sample everything, the emetic whisky, were produced.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like your rooms,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash, looking round her. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I resent\r\nyour secrecy about them, Theodore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrink, puppy!\xe2\x80\x9d Coleman refilled the boy\xe2\x80\x99s glass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s to secrecy,\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril proposed. Shut it tightly, keep it dark,\r\ncover it up. Be silent, prevaricate, lie outright. He laughed and drank.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you remember,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cthose instructive advertisements of\r\nEno\xe2\x80\x99s Fruit Salt they used to have when we were young? There was one\r\nlittle anecdote about a doctor who advised the hypochondriacal patient\r\nwho had come to consult him, to go and see Grimaldi, the clown; and the\r\npatient answered, \xe2\x80\x98I am Grimaldi.\xe2\x80\x99 Do you remember?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd why do you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. Or rather, I do know,\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril corrected himself, and\r\nlaughed again.\r\n\r\nThe young man suddenly began to boast. \xe2\x80\x9cI lost two hundred pounds\r\nyesterday playing _chemin de fer_,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and looked round for\r\napplause.\r\n\r\nColeman patted his curly head. \xe2\x80\x9cDelicious child!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re\r\npositively Hogarthian.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAngrily, the boy pushed him away. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you doing?\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted; then\r\nturned and addressed himself once more to the others. \xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t afford\r\nit, you know\xe2\x80\x94not a bloody penny of it. Not my money, either.\xe2\x80\x9d He seemed\r\nto find it exquisitely humorous. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd that two hundred wasn\xe2\x80\x99t all,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nadded, almost expiring with mirth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell Coleman how you borrowed his beard, Theodore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGumbril was looking intently into his glass, as though he hoped to see\r\nin its pale mixture of gin and Sauterne visions, as in a crystal, of the\r\nfuture. Mrs. Viveash touched him on the arm and repeated her injunction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that!\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril rather irritably. \xe2\x80\x9cNo. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t an interesting\r\nstory.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, it is! I insist,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Viveash, commanding peremptorily\r\nfrom her death-bed.\r\n\r\nGumbril drank his gin and Sauterne. \xe2\x80\x9cVery well then,\xe2\x80\x9d he said\r\nreluctantly, and began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what my governor will say,\xe2\x80\x9d the young man put in once or\r\ntwice. But nobody paid any attention to him. He relapsed into a sulky\r\nand, it seemed to him, very dignified silence. Under the warm, jolly\r\ntipsiness he felt a chill of foreboding. He poured out some more whisky.\r\n\r\nGumbril warmed to his anecdote. Expiringly Mrs. Viveash laughed from\r\ntime to time, or smiled her agonizing smile. Coleman whooped like a\r\nRedskin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd after the concert to these rooms,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril.\r\n\r\nWell, let everything go. Into the mud. Leave it there, and let the dogs\r\nlift their hind legs over it as they pass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! the genuine platonic fumblers,\xe2\x80\x9d commented Coleman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am Grimaldi,\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril laughed. Further than this it was difficult to\r\nsee where the joke could go. There, on the couch, where Mrs. Viveash and\r\nColeman were now sitting, she had lain sleeping in his arms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTowsing, in Elizabethan,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coleman.\r\n\r\nUnreal, eternal in the secret darkness. A night that was an eternal\r\nparenthesis among the other nights and days.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel I\xe2\x80\x99m going to be sick,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man suddenly. He had\r\nwanted to go on silently and haughtily sulking; but his stomach declined\r\nto take part in the dignified game.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood Lord!\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril, and jumped up. But before he could do\r\nanything effective, the young man had fulfilled his own prophecy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe real charm about debauchery,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coleman philosophically, \xe2\x80\x9cis its\r\ntotal pointlessness, futility, and above all its incredible tediousness.\r\nIf it really were all roses and exhilaration, as these poor children\r\nseem to imagine, it would be no better than going to church or studying\r\nthe higher mathematics. I should never touch a drop of wine or another\r\nharlot again. It would be against my principles. I told you it was\r\nemetic,\xe2\x80\x9d he called to the young man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what are your principles?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Viveash.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, strictly ethical,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coleman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re responsible for this creature,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril, pointing to the\r\nyoung man, who was sitting on the floor near the fireplace, cooling his\r\nforehead against the marble of the mantelpiece. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must take him away.\r\nReally, what a bore!\xe2\x80\x9d His nose and mouth were all wrinkled up with\r\ndisgust.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d the young man whispered. He kept his eyes shut and his face\r\nwas exceedingly pale.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut with pleasure,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coleman. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s your name?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked the\r\nyoung man, \xe2\x80\x9cand where do you live?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy name is Porteous,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the young man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood lord!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Gumbril, letting himself fall on to the couch beside\r\nMrs. Viveash. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the last straw!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER XVII\r\n\r\n\r\nThe two o\xe2\x80\x99clock snorted out of Charing Cross, but no healths were drunk,\r\nthis time, to Viscount Lascelles. A desiccating sobriety made arid the\r\ncorner of the third-class carriage in which Gumbril was sitting. His\r\nthoughts were an interminable desert of sand, with not a palm in sight,\r\nnot so much as a comforting mirage. Once again he fumbled in his\r\nbreast-pocket, brought out and unfolded the flimsy paper. Once more he\r\nread. How many times had he read before?\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cYour telegram made me very unhappy. Not merely because of the\r\n  accident\xe2\x80\x94though it made me shudder to think that something terrible\r\n  might have happened, poor darling\xe2\x80\x94but also, selfishly, my own\r\n  disappointment. I had looked forward so much. I had made a picture\r\n  of it all so clearly. I should have met you at the station with the\r\n  horse and trap from the Chequers, and we\xe2\x80\x99d have driven back to the\r\n  cottage\xe2\x80\x94and you\xe2\x80\x99d have loved the cottage. We\xe2\x80\x99d have had tea and I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\n  have made you eat an egg with it after your journey. Then we\xe2\x80\x99d have\r\n  gone for a walk; through the most heavenly wood I found yesterday to\r\n  a place where there\xe2\x80\x99s a wonderful view\xe2\x80\x94miles and miles of it. And\r\n  we\xe2\x80\x99d have wandered on and on, and sat down under the trees, and the\r\n  sun would have set, and the twilight would slowly have come to an\r\n  end, and we\xe2\x80\x99d have gone home again and found the lamps lighted and\r\n  supper ready\xe2\x80\x94not very grand, I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid, for Mrs. Vole isn\xe2\x80\x99t the\r\n  best of cooks. And then the piano; for there is a piano, and I had\r\n  the tuner come specially from Hastings yesterday, so that it isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n  _so_ bad now. And you\xe2\x80\x99d have played; and perhaps I would have made\r\n  my noises on it. And at last it would have been time for candles and\r\n  bed. When I heard you were coming, Theodore, I told Mrs. Vole a lie\r\n  about you. I said you were my husband, because she\xe2\x80\x99s fearfully\r\n  respectable, of course; and it would dreadfully disturb her if you\r\n  weren\xe2\x80\x99t. But I told myself that, too. I meant that you should be.\r\n  You see, I tell you everything. I\xe2\x80\x99m not ashamed. I wanted to give\r\n  you everything I could, and then we should always be together,\r\n  loving one another. And I should have been your slave, I should have\r\n  been your property and lived inside your life. But you would always\r\n  have had to love me.\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cAnd then, just as I was getting ready to go and call at the\r\n  Chequers for the horse and trap, your telegram came. I saw the word\r\n  \xe2\x80\x98accident,\xe2\x80\x99 and I imagined you all bleeding and smashed\xe2\x80\x94oh,\r\n  dreadful, dreadful. But then, when you seemed to make rather a joke\r\n  of it\xe2\x80\x94why did you say \xe2\x80\x98a little indisposed?\xe2\x80\x99 that seemed, somehow,\r\n  so stupid, I thought\xe2\x80\x94and said you were coming to-morrow, it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n  that which upset me; it was the dreadful, dreadful disappointment.\r\n  It was like a stab, that disappointment; it hurt so terribly, so\r\n  unreasonably much. It made me cry and cry, so that I thought I\r\n  should never be able to stop. And then, gradually, I began to see\r\n  that the pain of the disappointment wasn\xe2\x80\x99t unreasonably great. It\r\n  wasn\xe2\x80\x99t merely a question of your coming being put off for a day; it\r\n  was a question of its being put off for ever, of my never seeing you\r\n  again. I saw that that accident had been something really arranged\r\n  by Providence. It was meant to warn me and show me what I ought to\r\n  do. I saw how hopelessly impracticable the happiness I had been\r\n  imagining really was. I saw that you didn\xe2\x80\x99t, you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t love me in\r\n  anything like the same way as I loved you. I was only a curious\r\n  adventure, a new experience, a means to some other end. Mind, I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\n  not blaming you in the least. I\xe2\x80\x99m only telling you what is true,\r\n  what I gradually came to realize as true. If you\xe2\x80\x99d come\xe2\x80\x94what then?\r\n  I\xe2\x80\x99d have given you everything, my body, my mind, my soul, my whole\r\n  life. I\xe2\x80\x99d have twisted myself into the threads of your life. And\r\n  then, when in due course you wanted to make an end to this curious\r\n  little adventure, you would have had to cut the tangle and it would\r\n  have killed me; it would also have hurt you. At least I think it\r\n  would. In the end, I thanked God for the accident which had\r\n  prevented you coming. In this way, Providence lets us off very\r\n  lightly\xe2\x80\x94you with a bruise or two (for I do hope it really is\r\n  nothing, my precious darling), and me with a bruise inside, round\r\n  the heart. But both will get well quite soon. And all our lives, we\r\n  shall have an afternoon under the trees, an evening of music and in\r\n  the darkness, a night, an eternity of happiness, to look back on. I\r\n  shall go away from Robertsbridge at once. Good-bye, Theodore. What a\r\n  long letter! The last you\xe2\x80\x99ll ever get from me. The last\xe2\x80\x94what a\r\n  dreadful hurting word that is. I shall take it to post at once, for\r\n  fear, if I leave it, I may be weak enough to change my mind and let\r\n  you come to-morrow. I shall take it at once, then I shall come home\r\n  again and pack up and tell some new fib to Mrs. Vole. And after\r\n  that, perhaps I shall allow myself to cry again. Good-bye.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAridly, the desert of sand stretched out with not a tree and not even a\r\nmirage, except perhaps the vague and desperate hope that he might get\r\nthere before she started, that she might conceivably have changed her\r\nmind. Ah, if only he\xe2\x80\x99d read the letter a little earlier! But he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwoken up before eleven, he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been down before half-past. Sitting at\r\nthe breakfast-table, he had read the letter through.\r\n\r\nThe eggs and bacon had grown still colder, if that was possible, than\r\nthey were. He had read it through, he had rushed to the A.B.C. There was\r\nno practicable train before the two o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\r\n\r\nIf he had taken the seven-twenty-seven he would certainly have got there\r\nbefore she started. Ah, if only he had woken up a little earlier! But\r\nthen he would have had to go to bed a little earlier. And in order to go\r\nto bed earlier, he would have had to abandon Mrs. Viveash before she had\r\nbored herself to that ultimate point of fatigue at which she did at last\r\nfeel ready for repose. And to abandon Mrs. Viveash\xe2\x80\x94ah, that was really\r\nimpossible, she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t allow herself to be left alone. If only he\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t gone to the London Library yesterday! A wanton, unnecessary visit\r\nit had been. For after all, the journey was short; he didn\xe2\x80\x99t need a book\r\nfor the train. And the _Life of Beckford_, for which he had asked,\r\nproved, of course, to be out\xe2\x80\x94and he had been utterly incapable of\r\nthinking of any other book, among the two or three hundred thousand on\r\nthe shelves, that he wanted to read. And, in any case, what the devil\r\ndid he want with a _Life of Beckford_? Hadn\xe2\x80\x99t he his own life, the life\r\nof Gumbril, to attend to? Wasn\xe2\x80\x99t one life enough, without making\r\nsuperfluous visits to the London Library in search of other lives? And\r\nthen what a stroke of bad luck to have run into Mrs. Viveash at that\r\nvery moment! What an abject weakness to have let himself be bullied into\r\nsending that telegram. \xe2\x80\x9cA little indisposed....\xe2\x80\x9d Oh, my God! Gumbril\r\nshut his eyes and ground his teeth together; he felt himself blushing\r\nwith a retrospective shame.\r\n\r\nAnd of course it was quite useless taking the train, like this, to\r\nRobertsbridge. She\xe2\x80\x99d be gone, of course. Still, there was always the\r\ndesperate hope. There was the mirage across the desiccated plains, the\r\nmirage one knew to be deceptive and which, on a second glance, proved\r\nnot even to be a mirage, but merely a few livery spots behind the eyes.\r\nStill, it was amply worth doing\xe2\x80\x94as a penance, and to satisfy the\r\nconscience and to deceive oneself with an illusion of action. And then\r\nthe fact that he was to have spent the afternoon with Rosie and had put\r\nher off\xe2\x80\x94that too was highly satisfying. And not merely put her off,\r\nbut\xe2\x80\x94ultimate clownery in the worst of deliriously bad taste\xe2\x80\x94played a\r\njoke on her. \xe2\x80\x9cImpossible come to you, meet me 213 Sloane Street, second\r\nfloor, a little indisposed.\xe2\x80\x9d He wondered how she\xe2\x80\x99d get on with Mr.\r\nMercaptan; for it was to his rococo boudoir and Cr\xc3\xa9billon-souled sofa\r\nthat he had on the spur of the clownish moment, as he dashed into the\r\npost office on the way to the station, sent her.\r\n\r\nAridly, the desiccated waste extended. Had she been right in her letter?\r\nWould it really have lasted no more than a little while, and ended as\r\nshe prophesied, with an agonizing cutting of the tangle? Or could it be\r\nthat she had held out the one hope of happiness? Wasn\xe2\x80\x99t she perhaps the\r\none unique being with whom he might have learned to await in quietness\r\nthe final coming of that lovely terrible thing, from before the sound of\r\nwhose secret footsteps more than once and oh! ignobly he had fled? He\r\ncould not decide, it was impossible to decide until he had seen her\r\nagain, till he had possessed her, mingled his life with hers. And now\r\nshe had eluded him; for he knew very well that he would not find her. He\r\nsighed and looked out of the window.\r\n\r\nThe train pulled up at a small suburban station. Suburban, for though\r\nLondon was already some way behind, the little sham half-timbered houses\r\nnear the station, the newer tile and rough-cast dwellings farther out on\r\nthe slope of the hill proclaimed with emphasis the presence of the\r\nbusiness man, the holder of the season ticket. Gumbril looked at them\r\nwith a pensive disgust which must have expressed itself on his features;\r\nfor the gentleman sitting in the corner of the carriage facing his,\r\nsuddenly leaned forward, tapped him on the knee, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cI see you\r\nagree with me, sir, that there are too many people in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGumbril, who up till now had merely been aware that somebody was sitting\r\nopposite him, now looked with more attention at the stranger. He was a\r\nlarge, square old gentleman of robust and flourishing appearance, with a\r\nface of wrinkled brown parchment and a white moustache that merged, in a\r\nhandsome curve, with a pair of side whiskers, in a manner which reminded\r\none of the photographs of the Emperor Francis Joseph.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI perfectly agree with you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril answered. If he had been\r\nwearing his beard, he would have gone on to suggest that loquacious old\r\ngentlemen in trains are among the supernumeraries of the planet. As it\r\nwas, however he spoke with courtesy, and smiled in his most engaging\r\nfashion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I look at all these revolting houses,\xe2\x80\x9d the old gentleman\r\ncontinued, shaking his fist at the snuggeries of the season-ticket\r\nholders, \xe2\x80\x9cI am filled with indignation. I feel my spleen ready to burst,\r\nsir, ready to burst.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can sympathize with you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril. \xe2\x80\x9cThe architecture is\r\ncertainly not very soothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not the architecture I mind so much,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted the old gentleman,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s merely a question of art, and all nonsense so far as I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nconcerned. What disgusts me is the people inside the architecture, the\r\nnumber of them, sir. And the way they breed. Like maggots, sir, like\r\nmaggots. Millions of them, creeping about the face of the country,\r\nspreading blight and dirt wherever they go; ruining everything. It\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\npeople I object to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril, \xe2\x80\x9cif you will have sanitary conditions that\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t allow plagues to flourish properly; if you will tell mothers how\r\nto bring up their children, instead of allowing nature to kill them off\r\nin her natural way; if you will import unlimited supplies of corn and\r\nmeat: what can you expect? Of course the numbers go up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old gentleman waved all this away. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care what the causes\r\nare,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all one to me. What I do object to, sir, is the\r\neffects. Why sir, I am old enough to remember walking through the\r\ndelicious meadows beyond Swiss Cottage, I remember seeing the cows\r\nmilked in West Hampstead, sir. And now, what do I see now, when I go\r\nthere? Hideous red cities pullulating with Jews, sir. Pullulating with\r\nprosperous Jews. Am I right in being indignant, sir? Do I do well, like\r\nthe prophet Jonah, to be angry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril, with growing enthusiasm, \xe2\x80\x9cand the more so\r\nsince this frightful increase in population is the world\xe2\x80\x99s most\r\nformidable danger at the present time. With populations that in Europe\r\nalone expand by millions every year, no political foresight is possible.\r\nA few years of this mere bestial propagation will suffice to make\r\nnonsense of the wisest schemes of to-day\xe2\x80\x94or would suffice,\xe2\x80\x9d he hastened\r\nto correct himself, \xe2\x80\x9cif any wise schemes were being matured at the\r\npresent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery possibly, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old gentleman, \xe2\x80\x9cbut what I object to is\r\nseeing good cornland being turned into streets, and meadows, where cows\r\nused to graze, covered with houses full of useless and disgusting human\r\nbeings. I resent seeing the country parcelled out into back gardens.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is there any prospect,\xe2\x80\x9d Gumbril earnestly asked, \xe2\x80\x9cof our ever being\r\nable in the future to support the whole of our population? Will\r\nunemployment ever decrease?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d the old gentleman replied. \xe2\x80\x9cBut the families of the\r\nunemployed will certainly increase.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are right, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril, \xe2\x80\x9cthey will. And the families of the\r\nemployed and the prosperous will as steadily grow smaller. It is\r\nregrettable that birth control should have begun at the wrong end of the\r\nscale. There seems to be a level of poverty below which it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem\r\nworth while practising birth control, and a level of education below\r\nwhich birth control is regarded as morally wrong. Strange, how long it\r\nhas taken for the ideas of love and procreation to dissociate themselves\r\nin the human mind. In the majority of minds they are still, even in this\r\nso-called twentieth century, indivisibly wedded. Still,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued\r\nhopefully, \xe2\x80\x9cprogress is being made, progress is certainly, though\r\nslowly, being made. It is gratifying to find, for example, in the latest\r\nstatistics, that the clergy, as a class, are now remarkable for the\r\nsmallness of their families. The old jest is out of date. Is it too much\r\nto hope that these gentlemen may bring themselves in time to preach what\r\nthey already practise?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt _is_ too much to hope, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d the old gentleman answered with\r\ndecision.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are probably right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf we were all to preach all the things we all practise,\xe2\x80\x9d continued the\r\nold gentleman, \xe2\x80\x9cthe world would soon be a pretty sort of bear-garden, I\r\ncan tell you. Yes, and a monkey-house. And a wart-hoggery. As it is,\r\nsir, it is merely a place where there are too many human beings. Vice\r\nmust pay its tribute to virtue, or else we are all undone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI admire your wisdom, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril.\r\n\r\nThe old gentleman was delighted. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I have been much impressed by your\r\nphilosophical reflections,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cTell me, are you at all interested\r\nin old brandy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, not philosophically,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril. \xe2\x80\x9cAs a mere empiric only.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs a mere empiric!\xe2\x80\x9d The old gentleman laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cThen let me beg you to\r\naccept a case. I have a cellar which I shall never drink dry, alas!\r\nbefore I die. My only wish is that what remains of it shall be\r\ndistributed among those who can really appreciate it. In you, sir, I see\r\na fitting recipient of a case of brandy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou overwhelm me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gumbril. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are too kind, and, I may add, too\r\nflattering.\xe2\x80\x9d The train, which was a mortally slow one, came grinding for\r\nwhat seemed the hundredth time to a halt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old gentleman. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you have a card, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGumbril searched his pockets. \xe2\x80\x9cI have come without one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old gentleman. \xe2\x80\x9cI think I have a pencil. If you\r\nwill give me your name and address, I will have the case sent to you at\r\nonce.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLeisurely, he hunted for the pencil, he took out a notebook. The train\r\ngave a jerk forward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nGumbril began dictating. \xe2\x80\x9cTheodore,\xe2\x80\x9d he said slowly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe\xe2\x80\x94o\xe2\x80\x94dore,\xe2\x80\x9d the old gentleman repeated, syllable by syllable.\r\n\r\nThe train crept on, with slowly gathering momentum, through the station.\r\nHappening to look out of the window at this moment, Gumbril saw the name\r\nof the place painted across a lamp. It was Robertsbridge. He made a\r\nloud, inarticulate noise, flung open the door of the compartment,\r\nstepped out on to the footboard and jumped. He landed safely on the\r\nplatform, staggered forward a few paces with his acquired momentum and\r\ncame at last to a halt. A hand reached out and closed the swinging door\r\nof his compartment and, an instant afterwards, through the window, a\r\nface that, at a distance, looked more than ever like the face of the\r\nEmperor Francis Joseph, looked back towards the receding platform. The\r\nmouth opened and shut; no words were audible. Standing on the platform,\r\nGumbril made a complicated pantomime, signifying his regret by shrugging\r\nhis shoulders and placing his hand on his heart; urging in excuse for\r\nhis abrupt departure the necessity under which he laboured of alighting\r\nat this particular station\xe2\x80\x94which he did by pointing at the name on the\r\nboards and lamps, then at himself, then at the village across the\r\nfields. The old gentleman waved his hand, which still held, Gumbril\r\nnoticed, the notebook in which he had been writing. Then the train\r\ncarried him out of sight. There went the only case of old brandy he was\r\never likely to possess, thought Gumbril sadly, as he turned away.\r\nSuddenly, he remembered Emily again; for a long time he had quite\r\nforgotten her.\r\n\r\nThe cottage, when at last he found it, proved to be fully as picturesque\r\nas he had imagined. And Emily, of course, had gone, leaving, as might\r\nhave been expected, no address. He took the evening train back to\r\nLondon. The aridity was now complete, and even the hope of a mirage had\r\nvanished. There was no old gentleman to make a diversion. The size of\r\nclergymen\xe2\x80\x99s families, even the fate of Europe, seemed unimportant now,\r\nwere indeed perfectly indifferent to him.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                             CHAPTER XVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nTwo hundred and thirteen Sloane Street. The address, Rosie reflected, as\r\nshe vaporized synthetic lilies of the valley over all her sinuous\r\nperson, was decidedly a good one. It argued a reasonable prosperity,\r\nattested a certain distinction. The knowledge of his address confirmed\r\nher already high opinion of the bearded stranger who had so surprisingly\r\nentered her life, as though in fulfilment of all the fortune-tellers\xe2\x80\x99\r\nprophecies that ever were made; had entered, yes, and intimately made\r\nhimself at home. She had been delighted, when the telegram came that\r\nmorning, to think that at last she was going to find out something more\r\nabout this man of mystery. For dark and mysterious he had remained,\r\nremote even in the midst of the most intimate contacts. Why, she didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\neven know his name. \xe2\x80\x9cCall me Toto,\xe2\x80\x9d he had suggested, when she asked him\r\nwhat it was. And Toto she had had to call him, for lack of anything more\r\ndefinite or committal. But to-day he was letting her further into his\r\nsecret. Rosie was delighted. Her pink underclothing, she decided, as she\r\nlooked in the long glass, was really ravishing. She examined herself,\r\nturning first one way, then the other, looking over her shoulder to see\r\nthe effect from behind. She pointed a toe, bent and straightened a knee,\r\napplauding the length of her legs (\xe2\x80\x9cMost women,\xe2\x80\x9d Toto had said, \xe2\x80\x9care\r\nlike dachshunds\xe2\x80\x9d), their slenderness and plump suavity of form. In their\r\nwhite stockings of Milanese silk they looked delicious; and how\r\nmarvellously, by the way, those Selfridge people had mended those\r\nstockings by their new patent process! Absolutely like new, and only\r\ncharged four shillings. Well, it was time to dress. Good-bye, then, to\r\nthe pink underclothing and the long white legs. She opened the wardrobe\r\ndoor. The moving glass reflected, as it swung through its half-circle,\r\npink bed, rose-wreathed walls, little friends of her own age, and the\r\ndying saint at his last communion. Rosie selected the frock she had\r\nbought the other day at one of those little shops in Soho, there they\r\nsell such smart things so cheaply to a clientage of minor actresses and\r\ncocottes. Toto hadn\xe2\x80\x99t seen it yet. She looked extremely distinguished in\r\nit. The little hat, with its inch of veil hanging like a mask,\r\nunconcealing and inviting, from the brim, suited her to perfection. One\r\nlast dab of powder, one last squirt of synthetic lilies of the valley,\r\nand she was ready. She closed the door behind her. St. Jerome was left\r\nto communicate in the untenanted pinkness.\r\n\r\nMr. Mercaptan sat at his writing-table\xe2\x80\x94an exquisitely amusing affair in\r\npapier m\xc3\xa2ch\xc3\xa9, inlaid with floral decorations in mother-of-pearl and\r\npainted with views of Windsor Castle and Tintern in the romantic manner\r\nof Prince Albert\xe2\x80\x99s later days\xe2\x80\x94polishing to its final and gem-like\r\nperfection one of his middle articles. It was on a splendid subject\xe2\x80\x94the\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Jus Prim\xc3\xa6 Noctis, or Droit du Seigneur\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthat delicious _droit_,\xe2\x80\x9d wrote\r\nMr. Mercaptan, \xe2\x80\x9con which, one likes to think, the Sovereigns of England\r\ninsist so firmly in their motto, _Dieu et mon Droit\xe2\x80\x94de Seigneur_.\xe2\x80\x9d That\r\nwas charming, Mr. Mercaptan thought, as he read it through. And he liked\r\nthat bit which began elegiacally: \xe2\x80\x9cBut, alas! the Right of the First\r\nNight belongs to a Middle Age as mythical, albeit happily different, as\r\nthose dismal epochs invented by Morris or by Chesterton. The Lord\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nright, as we prettily imagine it, is a figment of the baroque\r\nimagination of the seventeenth century. It never existed. Or at least it\r\ndid exist, but as something deplorably different from what we love to\r\npicture it.\xe2\x80\x9d And he went on, eruditely, to refer to that Council of\r\nCarthage which, in 398, demanded of the faithful that they should be\r\ncontinent on their wedding-night. It was the Lord\xe2\x80\x99s right\xe2\x80\x94the _droit_ of\r\na heavenly Seigneur. On this text of fact, Mr. Mercaptan went on to\r\npreach a brilliant sermon on that melancholy sexual perversion known as\r\ncontinence. How much happier we all should be if the real historical\r\n_droit du Seigneur_ had in fact been the mythical right of our \xe2\x80\x98pretty\r\nprurient imaginations\xe2\x80\x99! He looked forward to a golden age when all\r\nshould be seigneurs possessing rights that should have broadened down\r\ninto universal liberty. And so on. Mr. Mercaptan read through his\r\ncreation with a smile of satisfaction on his face. Every here and there\r\nhe made a careful correction in red ink. Over \xe2\x80\x98pretty prurient\r\nimaginations\xe2\x80\x99 his pen hung for a full minute in conscientious\r\nhesitation. Wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it perhaps a little too strongly alliterative, a\r\nshade, perhaps, cheap? Perhaps \xe2\x80\x98pretty lascivious\xe2\x80\x99 or \xe2\x80\x98delicate\r\nprurient\xe2\x80\x99 would be better. He repeated the alternatives several times,\r\nrolling the sound of them round his tongue, judicially, like a\r\ntea-taster. In the end, he decided that \xe2\x80\x98pretty prurient\xe2\x80\x99 was right.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Pretty prurient\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94they were the _mots justes_, decidedly, without a\r\nquestion.\r\n\r\nMr. Mercaptan had just come to this decision and his poised pen was\r\nmoving farther down the page, when he was disturbed by the sound of\r\narguing voices in the corridor, outside his room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it, Mrs. Goldie?\xe2\x80\x9d he called irritably, for it was not difficult\r\nto distinguish his housekeeper\xe2\x80\x99s loud and querulous tones. He had given\r\norders that he was not to be disturbed. In these critical moments of\r\ncorrection one needed such absolute tranquillity.\r\n\r\nBut Mr. Mercaptan was to have no tranquillity this afternoon. The door\r\nof his sacred boudoir was thrown rudely open, and there strode in, like\r\na Goth into the elegant marble vomitorium of Petronius Arbiter, a\r\nhaggard and dishevelled person whom Mr. Mercaptan recognized, with a\r\ncertain sense of discomfort, as Casimir Lypiatt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo what do I owe the _pleasure_ of this unexpected...?\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mercaptan\r\nbegan with an essay in offensive courtesy.\r\n\r\nBut Lypiatt, who had no feeling for the finer shades, coarsely\r\ninterrupted him. \xe2\x80\x9cLook here, Mercaptan,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI want to have a talk\r\nwith you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDelighted, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mercaptan replied. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd _what_, may I ask,\r\nabout?\xe2\x80\x9d He knew, of course, perfectly well; and the prospect of the talk\r\ndisturbed him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout this,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lypiatt; and he held out what looked like a roll of\r\npaper.\r\n\r\nMr. Mercaptan took the roll and opened it out. It was a copy of the\r\n_Weekly World_. \xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mercaptan, in a tone of delighted\r\nsurprise, \xe2\x80\x9c_The World_. You have read my little article?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was what I wanted to talk to you about,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lypiatt.\r\n\r\nMr. Mercaptan modestly laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cIt hardly deserves it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nPreserving a calm of expression which was quite unnatural to him, and\r\nspeaking in a studiedly quiet voice, Lypiatt pronounced with careful\r\ndeliberation: \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a disgusting, malicious, ignoble attack on me,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, _come_!\xe2\x80\x9d protested Mr. Mercaptan. \xe2\x80\x9cA critic must be allowed to\r\ncriticize.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut there are limits,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lypiatt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I _quite_ agree,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mercaptan eagerly conceded. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, after all,\r\nLypiatt, you can\xe2\x80\x99t pretend that I have come anywhere near those limits.\r\nIf I had called you a _mur_derer, or even an _adul_terer\xe2\x80\x94then, I admit,\r\nyou would have some cause to complain. But I haven\xe2\x80\x99t. There\xe2\x80\x99s nothing\r\nlike a personality in the whole thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLypiatt laughed derisively, and his face went all to pieces, like a pool\r\nof water into which a stone is suddenly dropped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve merely said I was insincere, an actor, a mountebank, a quack,\r\nraving fustian, spouting mock heroics. That\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Mercaptan put on the expression of one who feels himself injured and\r\nmisunderstood. He shut his eyes, he flapped deprecatingly with his hand.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _merely_ suggested,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you protest _too_ much. You\r\ndefeat your own ends; you lose emphasis by trying to be over-emphatic.\r\nAll this _folie de grandeur_, all this hankering after _terribilt\xc3\xa0_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsagely Mr. Mercaptan shook his head, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s led so _many_ people astray.\r\nAnd, in any case, you can\xe2\x80\x99t _really_ expect _me_ to find it very\r\nsympathetic.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Mercaptan uttered a little laugh and looked\r\naffectionately round his boudoir, his retired and perfumed poutery\r\nwithin whose walls so much civilization had finely flowered. He looked\r\nat his magnificent sofa, gilded and carved, upholstered in white satin,\r\nand so deep\xe2\x80\x94for it was a great square piece of furniture, almost as\r\nbroad as it was long\xe2\x80\x94that when you sat right back, you had of necessity\r\nto lift your feet from the floor and recline at length. It was under the\r\nwhite satin that Cr\xc3\xa9billon\xe2\x80\x99s spirit found, in these late degenerate\r\ndays, a sympathetic home. He looked at his exquisite Condor fans over\r\nthe mantelpiece; his lovely Marie Laurencin of two young girls,\r\npale-skinned and berry-eyed, walking embraced in a shallow myopic\r\nlandscape amid a troop of bounding heraldic dogs. He looked at his\r\ncabinet of _bibelots_ in the corner where the nigger mask and the superb\r\nChinese phallus in sculptured rock crystal contrasted so amusingly with\r\nthe Chelsea china, the little ivory Madonna, which might be a fake, but\r\nin any case was quite as good as any medi\xc3\xa6val French original, and the\r\nItalian medals. He looked at his comical writing-desk in shining black\r\npapier m\xc3\xa2ch\xc3\xa9 and mother-of-pearl; he looked at his article on the \xe2\x80\x9cJus\r\nPrim\xc3\xa6 Noctis,\xe2\x80\x9d black and neat on the page, with the red corrections\r\nattesting his tireless search for, and his, he flattered himself, almost\r\ninvariable discovery of, the inevitable word. No, really, one couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nexpect _him_ to find Lypiatt\xe2\x80\x99s notions very sympathetic.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t expect you to,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lypiatt, \xe2\x80\x9cand, good God! I don\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\nyou to. But you call me insincere. That\xe2\x80\x99s what I can\xe2\x80\x99t and won\xe2\x80\x99t stand.\r\nHow dare you do that?\xe2\x80\x9d His voice was growing louder.\r\n\r\nOnce more Mr. Mercaptan deprecatingly flapped. \xe2\x80\x9cAt the most,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ncorrected, \xe2\x80\x9cI said that there was a certain look of insincerity about\r\nsome of the pictures. Hardly avoidable, indeed, in work of this kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nQuite suddenly, Lypiatt lost his self-control. All the accumulated anger\r\nand bitterness of the last days burst out. His show had been a hopeless\r\nfailure. Not a picture sold, a press that was mostly bad, or, when good,\r\nthat had praised for the wrong, the insulting reasons. \xe2\x80\x9cBright and\r\neffective work.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Lypiatt would make an excellent stage designer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nDamn them! damn them! And then, when the dailies had all had their yelp,\r\nhere was Mercaptan in the _Weekly World_ taking him as a text for what\r\nwas practically an essay on insincerity in art. \xe2\x80\x9cHow dare you?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nfuriously shouted. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x94how dare you talk about sincerity? What can you\r\nknow about sincerity, you disgusting little bug!\xe2\x80\x9d And avenging himself\r\non the person of Mr. Mercaptan against the world that had neglected him,\r\nagainst the fate that had denied him his rightful share of talent,\r\nLypiatt sprang up and, seizing the author of the \xe2\x80\x9cJus Prim\xc3\xa6 Noctis\xe2\x80\x9d by\r\nthe shoulders, he shook him, he bumped him up and down in his chair, he\r\ncuffed him over the head. \xe2\x80\x9cHow can you have the impudence,\xe2\x80\x9d he asked,\r\nletting go of his victim, but still standing menacingly over him, \xe2\x80\x9cto\r\ntouch anything that even attempts to be decent and big?\xe2\x80\x9d All these\r\nyears, these wretched years of poverty and struggle and courageous hope\r\nand failure and repeated disappointment; and now this last failure, more\r\ncomplete than all. He was trembling with anger; at least one forgot\r\nunhappiness while one was angry.\r\n\r\nMr. Mercaptan had recovered from his first terrified surprise. \xe2\x80\x9cReally,\r\n_really_\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated, \xe2\x80\x9c_too_ barbarous. Scuffling like hobbledehoys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you knew,\xe2\x80\x9d Lypiatt began; but he checked himself. If you knew, he\r\nwas going to say, what those things had cost me, what they meant, what\r\nthought, what passion\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94But how could Mercaptan understand? And it would\r\nsound as though he were appealing to this creature\xe2\x80\x99s sympathy. \xe2\x80\x9cBug!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nshouted instead, \xe2\x80\x9cbug!\xe2\x80\x9d And he struck out again with the flat of his\r\nhand. Mr. Mercaptan put up his hands and ducked away from the slaps,\r\nblinking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally,\xe2\x80\x9d he protested, \xe2\x80\x9c_really_....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nInsincere? Perhaps it was half true. Lypiatt seized his man more\r\nfuriously than before and shook him, shook him. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd then that vile\r\ninsult about the vermouth advertisement,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried out. That had\r\nrankled. Those flaring, vulgar posters! \xe2\x80\x9cYou thought you could mock me\r\nand spit at me with impunity, did you? I\xe2\x80\x99ve stood it so long, you\r\nthought I\xe2\x80\x99d always stand it? Was that it? But you\xe2\x80\x99re mistaken.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nlifted his fist. Mr. Mercaptan cowered away, raising his arm to protect\r\nhis head. \xe2\x80\x9cVile bug of a coward,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lypiatt, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you defend\r\nyourself like a man? You can only be dangerous with words. Very witty\r\nand spiteful and cutting about those vermouth posters, wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it? But\r\nyou wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t dare to fight me if I challenged you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, as a matter of _fact_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mercaptan, peering up from under\r\nhis defences, \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t invent _that_ particular piece of criticism. I\r\nborrowed the _ap\xc3\xa9ritif_.\xe2\x80\x9d He laughed feebly, more canary than bull.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou borrowed it, did you?\xe2\x80\x9d Lypiatt contemptuously repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd who\r\nfrom, may I ask?\xe2\x80\x9d Not that it interested him in the least to know.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if you really _want_ to know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Mercaptan, \xe2\x80\x9cit was from\r\nour friend Myra Viveash.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLypiatt stood for a moment without speaking, then putting his menacing\r\nhand in his pocket, he turned away. \xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d he said noncommittally, and\r\nwas silent again.\r\n\r\nRelieved, Mr. Mercaptan sat up in his chair; with the palm of his right\r\nhand he smoothed his dishevelled head.\r\n\r\nAirily, outside in the sunshine, Rosie walked down Sloane Street,\r\nlooking at the numbers on the doors of the houses. A hundred and\r\nninety-nine, two hundred, two hundred and one\xe2\x80\x94she was getting near now.\r\nPerhaps all the people who passed, strolling so easily and elegantly and\r\ndisengagedly along, perhaps they all of them carried behind their eyes a\r\nsecret, as delightful and amusing as hers. Rosie liked to think so; it\r\nmade life more exciting. How nonchalantly distinguished, Rosie\r\nreflected, she herself must look. Would any one who saw her now,\r\nsauntering along like this, would any one guess that, ten houses farther\r\ndown the street, a young poet, or at least very nearly a young poet, was\r\nwaiting, on the second floor, eagerly for her arrival? Of course they\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t and couldn\xe2\x80\x99t guess! That was the fun and the enormous\r\nexcitement of the whole thing. Formidable in her light-hearted\r\ndetachment, formidable in the passion which at will she could give rein\r\nto and check again, the great lady swam beautifully along through the\r\nsunlight to satisfy her caprice. Like Diana, she stooped over the\r\nshepherd boy. Eagerly the starving young poet waited, waited in his\r\ngarret. Two hundred and twelve, two hundred and thirteen. Rosie looked\r\nat the entrance and was reminded that the garret couldn\xe2\x80\x99t after all be\r\nvery sordid, nor the young poet absolutely starving. She stepped in and,\r\nstanding in the hall, looked at the board with the names. Ground floor:\r\nMrs. Budge. First floor: F. de M. Rowbotham. Second floor: P. Mercaptan.\r\n\r\nP. Mercaptan.... But it was a charming name, a romantic name, a real\r\nyoung poet\xe2\x80\x99s name! Mercaptan\xe2\x80\x94she felt more than ever pleased with her\r\nselection. The fastidious lady could not have had a happier caprice.\r\nMercaptan ... Mercaptan.... She wondered what the P. stood for. Peter,\r\nPhilip, Patrick, Pendennis even? She could hardly have guessed that Mr.\r\nMercaptan\xe2\x80\x99s father, the eminent bacteriologist, had insisted,\r\nthirty-four years ago, on calling his first-born \xe2\x80\x98Pasteur.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nA little tremulous, under her outward elegant calm, Rosie mounted the\r\nstairs. Twenty-five steps to the first floor\xe2\x80\x94one flight of thirteen,\r\nwhich was rather disagreeably ominous, and one of twelve. Then two\r\nflights of eleven, and she was on the second landing, facing a front\r\ndoor, a bell-push like a round eye, a brass name-plate. For a great lady\r\nthoroughly accustomed to this sort of thing, she felt her heart beating\r\nrather unpleasantly fast. It was those stairs, no doubt. She halted a\r\nmoment, took two deep breaths, then pushed the bell.\r\n\r\nThe door was opened by an aged servant of the most forbiddingly\r\nrespectable appearance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Mercaptan at home?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe person at the door burst at once into a long, rambling, angry\r\ncomplaint, but precisely about what Rosie could not for certain make\r\nout. Mr. Mercaptan had left orders, she gathered, that he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t to be\r\ndisturbed. But some one had come and disturbed him, \xe2\x80\x9cfairly shoved his\r\nway in, so rude and inconsiderate,\xe2\x80\x9d all the same. And now he\xe2\x80\x99d been once\r\ndisturbed, she didn\xe2\x80\x99t see why he shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be disturbed again. Then, as soon as Rosie had stepped across the\r\nthreshold, she cut off her retreat with a bang and went off, muttering\r\nall the time, towards her kitchen.\r\n\r\nIt certainly wasn\xe2\x80\x99t a garret. And he walked\r\ntowards the door. Like vermouth posters, like vermouth posters!\xe2\x80\x94so that\r\nwas Myra\xe2\x80\x99s piece of mockery! All his anger had sunk like a quenched\r\nflame. In the light of Mr.\r\nMercaptan\xe2\x80\x99s boudoir her hammered copper and her leadless glaze certainly\r\ndid look a bit comical.\r\n\r\nAfter tea Mr. Mercaptan played cicerone in a tour of inspection round\r\nthe room. They visited the papier m\xc3\xa2ch\xc3\xa9 writing-desk, the Condor fans,\r\nthe Marie Laurencin, the 1914 edition of _Du C\xc3\xb4t\xc3\xa9 de chez Swann_, the\r\nMadonna that probably was a fake, the nigger mask, the Chelsea figures,\r\nthe Chinese object of art in sculptured crystal, the scale model of\r\nQueen Victoria in wax under a glass bell. Toto, it became clear, had\r\nbeen no more than a forerunner; the definitive revelation was Mr.\r\nMercaptan\xe2\x80\x99s. Yes, poor Aunt Aggie! And indeed, when Mr. Mercaptan began\r\nto read her his little middle on the \xe2\x80\x9cDroit du Seigneur,\xe2\x80\x9d it was poor\r\neverybody. Poor mother, with her absurd, old-fashioned, prudish views;\r\npoor, earnest father, with his Unitarianism, his _Hibbert Journal_, his\r\nletters to the papers about the necessity for a spiritual regeneration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBravo!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried from the depths of Cr\xc3\xa9billon. She was leaning back in\r\none corner, languid, serpentine, and at ease, her feet in their mottled\r\nsnake\xe2\x80\x99s leather tucked up under her. \xe2\x80\x9cBravo!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried as Mr. Mercaptan\r\nfinished his reading and looked up for his applause.\r\n\r\nMr. Mercaptan bowed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou express so exquisitely what we\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d and waving her hand in a\r\ncomprehensive gesture, she pictured to herself all the other fastidious\r\nladies, all the marchionesses of fable, reclining, as she herself at\r\nthis moment reclined, on upholstery of white satin, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat we all only\r\nfeel and aren\xe2\x80\x99t clever enough to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Mercaptan was charmed. He got up from before his writing-desk,\r\ncrossed the room and sat down beside her on Cr\xc3\xa9billon. \xe2\x80\x9cFeeling,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cis the im_port_ant thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosie remembered that her father had once remarked, in blank verse: \xe2\x80\x98The\r\nthings that matter happen in the heart.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI quite agree,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nLike movable raisins in the suet of his snouty face, Mr. Mercaptan\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbrown little eyes rolled amorous avowals. He took Rosie\xe2\x80\x99s hand and\r\nkissed it. Cr\xc3\xa9billon creaked discreetly as he moved a little nearer.\r\n\r\nIt was only the evening of the same day. Rosie lay on her sofa\xe2\x80\x94a poor,\r\nhire-purchase thing indeed, compared with Mr. Mercaptan\xe2\x80\x99s grand affair\r\nin white satin and carved and gilded wood, but still a sofa\xe2\x80\x94lay with her\r\nfeet on the arm of it and her long suave legs exposed, by the slipping\r\nof the kimono, to the top of her stretched stockings. She was reading\r\nthe little vellum-jacketed volume of Cr\xc3\xa9billon, which Mr. Mercaptan had\r\ngiven her when he said \xe2\x80\x98good-bye\xe2\x80\x99 (or rather, \xe2\x80\x98_\xc3\x80 bient\xc3\xb4t, mon amie_\xe2\x80\x99);\r\ngiven, not lent, as he had less generously offered at the beginning of\r\ntheir afternoon; given with the most graceful of allusive dedications\r\ninscribed on the fly-leaf:\r\n\r\n                                   To\r\n\r\n                       BY-NO-OTHER-NAME-AS-SWEET,\r\n\r\n                            WITH GRATITUDE,\r\n\r\n                                  FROM\r\n\r\n                          CR\xc3\x89BILLON DELIVERED.\r\n\r\n_\xc3\x80 bient\xc3\xb4t_\xe2\x80\x94she had promised to come again very soon. She thought of the\r\nessay on the \xe2\x80\x9cJus Prim\xc3\xa6 Noctis\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94ah! what we\xe2\x80\x99ve all been feeling and none\r\nof us clever enough to say. We on the sofas, ruthless, lovely and\r\nfastidious....\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am proud to constitute myself\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Mr. Mercaptan had said of\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9c_l\xe2\x80\x99esprit d\xe2\x80\x99escalier des dames galantes_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosie was not quite sure what he meant; but it certainly sounded very\r\nwitty indeed.\r\n\r\nShe read the book slowly. Her French, indeed, wasn\xe2\x80\x99t good enough to\r\npermit her to read it anyhow else. She wished it were better. Perhaps it\r\nif were better she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be yawning like this. It was disgraceful:\r\nshe pulled herself together. Mr. Mercaptan had said that, it was a\r\nmasterpiece.\r\n\r\nIn his study, Shearwater was trying to write his paper on the regulative\r\nfunctions of the kidneys. He was not succeeding.\r\n\r\nWhy wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t she see me yesterday? he kept wondering. With anguish he\r\nsuspected other lovers; desired her, in consequence, the more. Gumbril\r\nhad said something, he remembered, that night they had met her by the\r\ncoffee-stall. What was it? He wished now that he had listened more\r\nattentively.\r\n\r\nShe\xe2\x80\x99s bored with me. Already. It was obvious.\r\n\r\nPerhaps he was too rustic for her. Shearwater looked at his hands. Yes,\r\nthe nails _were_ dirty. He took an orange stick out of his waistcoat\r\npocket and began to clean them. He had bought a whole packet of orange\r\nsticks that morning.\r\n\r\nDeterminedly he took up his pen. \xe2\x80\x9cThe hydrogen ion concentration in the\r\nblood ...\xe2\x80\x9d he began a new paragraph. But he got no further than the\r\nfirst seven words.\r\n\r\nIf, he began thinking with a frightful confusion, if\xe2\x80\x94if\xe2\x80\x94if\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94 Past\r\nconditionals, hopelessly past. He might have been brought up more\r\nelegantly; his father, for example, might have been a barrister instead\r\nof a barrister\xe2\x80\x99s clerk. He mightn\xe2\x80\x99t have had to work so hard when he was\r\nyoung; might have been about more, danced more, seen more young women.\r\nIf he had met her years ago\xe2\x80\x94during the war, should one say, dressed in\r\nthe uniform of a lieutenant in the Guards....\r\n\r\nHe had pretended that he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t interested in women; that they had no\r\neffect on him; that, in fact, he was above that sort of thing. Imbecile!\r\nHe might as well have said that he was above having a pair of kidneys.\r\nHe had only consented to admit, graciously, that they were a\r\nphysiological necessity.\r\n\r\nO God, what a fool he had been!\r\n\r\nAnd then, what about Rosie? What sort of a life had she been having\r\nwhile he was being above that sort of thing? Now he came to think of it,\r\nhe really knew nothing about her, except that she had been quite\r\nincapable of learning correctly, even by heart, the simplest facts about\r\nthe physiology of frogs. Having found that out, he had really given up\r\nexploring further. How could he have been so stupid?\r\n\r\nRosie had been in love with him, he supposed. Had he been in love with\r\nher? No. He had taken care not to be. On principle. He had married her\r\nas a measure of intimate hygiene; out of protective affection, too,\r\ncertainly out of affection; and a little for amusement, as one might buy\r\na puppy.\r\n\r\nMrs. Viveash had opened his eyes; seeing her, he had also begun to\r\nnotice Rosie. It seemed to him that he had been a loutish cad as well as\r\nan imbecile.\r\n\r\nWhat should he do about it? He sat for a long time wondering.\r\n\r\nIn the end he decided that the best thing would be to go and tell Rosie\r\nall about it, all about everything.\r\n\r\nAbout Mrs. Viveash too? Yes, about Mrs. Viveash too. He would get over\r\nMrs. Viveash more easily and more rapidly if he did. And he would begin\r\nto try and find out about Rosie. He would explore her. He would discover\r\nall the other things besides an incapacity to learn physiology that were\r\nin her. He would discover her, he would quicken his affection for her\r\ninto something livelier and more urgent. And they would begin again;\r\nmore satisfactorily this time; with knowledge and understanding; wise\r\nfrom their experience.\r\n\r\nShearwater got up from his chair before the writing-table, lurched\r\npensively towards the door, bumping into the revolving bookcase and the\r\narm-chair as he went, and walked down the passage to the drawing-room.\r\nRosie did not turn her head as he came in, but went on reading without\r\nchanging her position, her slippered feet still higher than her head,\r\nher legs still charmingly avowing themselves.\r\n\r\nShearwater came to a halt in front of the empty fireplace. He stood\r\nthere with his back to it, as though warming himself before an imaginary\r\nflame. It was, he felt, the safest, the most strategic point from which\r\nto talk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you reading?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Le Sopha_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d Rosie scornfully echoed. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s one of the great\r\nFrench classics.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho by?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCr\xc3\xa9billon the younger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever heard of him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shearwater. There was a silence. Rosie went\r\non reading.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt just occurred to me,\xe2\x80\x9d Shearwater began again in his rather\r\nponderous, infelicitous way, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you mightn\xe2\x80\x99t be very happy, Rosie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosie looked up at him and laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat put that into your head?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nasked. \xe2\x80\x9c_I_\xe2\x80\x99m perfectly happy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShearwater was left a little at a loss. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99m very glad to hear\r\nit,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI only thought ... that perhaps _you_ might think ...\r\nthat _I_ rather neglected you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosie laughed again. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is all this about?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have it rather on my conscience,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shearwater. \xe2\x80\x9cI begin to\r\nsee ... something has made me see ... that I\xe2\x80\x99ve not.... I don\xe2\x80\x99t treat\r\nyou very well....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t n\xe2\x80\x94notice it, I assure you,\xe2\x80\x9d put in Rosie, still smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI leave you out too much,\xe2\x80\x9d Shearwater went on with a kind of\r\ndesperation, running his fingers through his thick brown hair. \xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nshare enough together. You\xe2\x80\x99re too much outside my life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut after all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosie, \xe2\x80\x9cwe are a civ\xe2\x80\x94vilized couple. We don\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\nto live in one another\xe2\x80\x99s pockets, do we?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but we\xe2\x80\x99re really no more than strangers,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shearwater. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t right. And it\xe2\x80\x99s my fault. I\xe2\x80\x99ve never tried to get into touch with\r\nyour life. But you did your best to understand mine ... at the beginning\r\nof our marriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, _then\xe2\x80\x94n_!\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosie, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cYou found out what a little idiot\r\nI was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t make a joke of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shearwater. \xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t a joke. It\xe2\x80\x99s very\r\nserious. I tell you, I\xe2\x80\x99ve come to see how stupid and inconsiderate and\r\nun-understanding I\xe2\x80\x99ve been with you.
